Causality and Created Things
intermediate
4 min
Refuting the Analogy Between Creating Accidents and Bodies
Al-Maturidi refutes the objection that if humans create accidents, they should also create bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Maturidi's refutation of the objection that if humans can create accidents, they should also be able to create bodies.

Core Idea
Human agency lacks the essential aspect needed to create bodies.

Al-Maturidi addresses an objection raised by his opponents. The objection claims that if humans can bring accidents into existence, they should also be able to create bodies. In this passage, he refutes that reasoning by clarifying the nature of the act required for body creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice that the passage begins with an objection and then the author's refutation.
Pay attention to the structure: objection, redefinition, answer, refutation, analogy.
2
Identify the core difference
Al-Maturidi points out that the creation of a body involves a specific aspect (the essence) that humans do not realize in their own acts. Identify this key distinction.
The difference lies in the 'aspect of existence' (وجه وجود الجسم).
3
Understand the Zaid analogy
The analogy with Zaid illustrates that while humans can act with power, they cannot replicate a specific act unless empowered. Reflect on how this supports the refutation.
Zaid does not empower us over his act, but God empowers humans over the meaning of the body.
4
Grasp the logical consequence
Al-Maturidi concludes that the opponent's own criteria would force them to admit that humans create bodies, which they deny, thus their position is inconsistent.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بأنه إذا قدرتم على إخراج الأعراض من العدم إلى الوجود لمَ لا جاز أن تقدروا على ذلك في الجسم؟ قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: وليس هذا تقدير السؤال، ولكن بما ليس معنى خلق الجسم إلا خروجه من العدم ووجوده بعد أن لم يكن، وبه وصفتم أنفسكم في فعل الأعراض، كيف لا جاز الوصف بخلق الجسم وليس ثمة غير؟ ولا قوة إلا بالله. فأجاب بالفعل. وذلك فاسد، لأننا لم نحقق لنا في فعلنا الوجه الذي هو وجه وجود الجسم وكونه، ليلزمنا ذلك، وهم قد حققوا فيلزمهم. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم قال: إذ ليس فعل زید سوی فعل بقدرة وأنتم تفعلون بها كيف ما فعلتم فعل زيد؟ قيل: لأن زيدا لا يقدرنا على فعله، فلم نفعل، والله قد أقدركم على المعنى الذي به كان الجسم، فلزمكم ما قابلناكم به. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he objected to himself: if you are able to bring forth accidents from non-existence into existence, why are you not able to do that for the body? Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'This is not the manner of the question, but rather because the meaning of the creation of the body is only its emergence from non-existence and its existence after it was not, and by this you described yourselves regarding the act of accidents, how is the description of creating the body not permissible while there is no other [agent]?' And there is no power except with Allah. So he answered by the act. And that is corrupt, because we have not realized for ourselves in our act the aspect that is the aspect of the existence of the body and its being, so that it would be necessary for us, while they have realized it, so it is necessary for them. And there is no power except with Allah. Then he said: Is not the act of Zaid only an act by power, and you act with it just as you acted the act of Zaid? It was said: Because Zaid does not empower us over his act, so we did not act, while Allah has empowered you over the meaning by which the body came into being, so what we have confronted you with is necessary for you. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its refutation concerning the ability to create bodies versus accidents. The opponent (al-Ka'bi) raises a self-objection: if you (the Mu'tazila) claim that you can bring accidents from non-existence to existence, why can you not do the same for a body? Abu Mansur responds by redefining the question: the creation of a body means bringing it from non-existence to existence. The Mu'tazila describe their own acts as causing accidents to exist, so why would they not be described as creating bodies, especially since there is no other agent involved? Then the opponent answers by appealing to the act itself, but Abu Mansur deems this answer corrupt. The reason is that in their own acts, the Mu'tazila have not realized the aspect that constitutes the very existence and being of a body (i.e., the essence), so the necessity of that act being creation does not apply to them. However, the opponent (the one who claims accidents are created by humans) has indeed realized that aspect, so the same necessity applies to them. Abu Mansur then uses an example: Zaid's act is merely an act performed by his power, and you can act with your power just as Zaid does. The reply is that Zaid does not enable us over his act (i.e., we cannot replicate his specific act), but God has given you the ability over the meaning by which the body exists, so the same objection we raised (that you create the body) becomes necessary for you.

Key Terms
أعراض
جسم
وجود
عدم
فعل
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If humans can create accidents, they should also be able to create bodies by analogy.
Correction: Al-Maturidi shows that creating a body requires realizing the essential aspect of existence, which humans do not achieve in their own acts.

Checkpoint
According to al-Maturidi, what is the flaw in the opponent's argument that if humans can create accidents, they should also be able to create bodies?
A) The creation of bodies requires a different type of power.
B) Humans have not realized the aspect that constitutes the existence of a body in their own acts.
C) Bodies are eternal.
D) Accidents are not real.

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi argues that creating a body requires realizing its essential aspect, which humans do not achieve in their own acts, thus the analogy fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that creating a body requires realizing its essential aspect, something you cannot do. How does this recognition of your limitation in creation affect your reliance on your own power versus God's power? Commit to one specific area where you will consciously depend on God today.
?
Al-Maturidi points out that the opponent's logic forces them to admit they would be creators of bodies. Is there an inconsistency in your own life where you claim God as Creator of everything, yet act as if your own actions are independent? Write down one action you will attribute entirely to God's creation after this lesson.
?
The analogy fails because humans lack the 'aspect of existence' of a body. What is one thing you have been trying to 'create' or bring about in your life that is beyond your reach, and how can you surrender that to God?

The Writer and Painter Example
Maturidi presents and refutes the writer/painter example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili argument from the writer/painter example and Maturidi's initial refutation.

Core Idea
Inability to replicate creation points to divine agency.

In this passage, Maturidi engages with a Mu'tazili analogy: a writer cannot produce an exact copy of his own work, suggesting the original was not truly his. Maturidi begins to dismantle this argument by examining the causes involved.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the translation to understand the key points.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili claim
The opponent argues that if a writer cannot replicate his own work, that shows the first work was not produced by him alone.
This is the claim Maturidi refutes.
3
Understand Maturidi's response
Maturidi points out that human actions require multiple causes (mind, memory, etc.) that rarely align perfectly, so inability is due to incomplete conditions, not lack of agency.
4
Note the counter-objections
The opponent raises that if this argument necessitated another agent, it would also necessitate another painter for the second, and that inability does not prove non-agency.
Maturidi uses these to refine his position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: واحتج بالكاتب والمصور، إنه لو أراد أن يخرج الثاني على ما عليه الأول لم يمكنه، دل أن الأول لم يخرج على ذلك به. فزعم أولاً أنه يجوز أن يكون كذلك بما ألقاه الله فيه تلك القدرة. فإن قيل: لم لا يأتي بمثله؟ فأجاب بأنا، وإن كنا نفعل بتلك القدرة، نفعله بأسباب لا تجتمع بكليتها حتى لا يخرج منها شيء من نحو الذهن والحفظ وأنواع الأشغال. قال: ولو وجب بهذا محدث آخر ليجب به مصور آخر. وعارض بالفعل؛ إنه فعلك لم يدل العجز على أنا لم نفعل. ولو كان العجز يدل على ما ذكروا لكان الثاني إذا كان أحسن، فدل أن الأول له. ثم عارض أنه ما يمنعه؟ فقال: لأنا نفعل بآلة وقدرة وعلاج وفكر، ولا تستوى هذه، ولو استوت أمكننا ذلك. فيقال له: الوجوه التي تمنعك هل هي أو لا؟ فإن قال: لا، أعظم القول أن علاجه وفكره ونحو ذلك كان من الله، وهو الذي به أنكر خلق ذلك، فقد أقر به. وإن قال: بلى، قيل: السؤال عن ذلك كله أن القدرية زعمت أن الله لو أبقاها، وقد أبقاها للفعل، وكل ذلك أفعال لها القدرة، فما بالها لم تستو، وقد قصدت أن تستوى، وكانت لك القدرة؟ فهذا يبيّن أنه على غير تقديرك يخرج. وما ذكر من المعنى هو الدليل الواضح أن قد يخرج أحسن من الأول وأسوى على قصد الاستواء على أن ذلك كان كذلك لا به. مع ما لا يبلغ علم أحد إلى تقدير حركته من الهواء والمكان ومن ارتفاع اليد وانخفاضها لو اجتهد كل الجهد والفعل لا يخلو عنه، ثبت أنه لغيره من هذا الوجه. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعلى ذلك لا أحد يقصد قصد تقبيح الفعل، وقد يكون كذلك، ثبت أنه من ذلك الوجه ليس له. ولو جاز وقوع فعل من وجه لا يعلمه ولا يريده ولو اجتهد كل جهده ليعرف حده ومبلغه ويكون له على ما هو عليه ذلك الفعل، لجاز مثله في جميع العالم وآيات الرسل وغيرها. والقول في المصور هو القول في الفاعل، وفي الصورة هو القول في الفعل، لا فرق بينهما. ثم قوله: «لو أبقى الله القوة» ومن مذهبه أن القوة لا تبقى وقتين لا معنى له. ثم ما عارض من الفعل فهو يختاره ويعلم ما يفعله ويقصده فهو من ذلك الوجه له، وقد بينا في ذلك ما ليس هو بعالم بذلك الوجه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
He said: And he argued by the writer and painter: if he wanted to produce the second like the first, he cannot, indicating that the first was not produced by him in that way. So he claimed first that it is permissible that it be so because God cast that power into him. If it is said: Why does he not produce its like? He answered: We, even if we act by that power, we act by causes that do not completely come together so that nothing emerges from them from such as the mind, memory, and types of occupations. He said: And if by this another instigator were necessitated, then another painter would be necessitated by it. And he opposed by action: Surely your action – inability does not indicate that we did not act. And if inability indicated what they mentioned, then the second, if it were better, would indicate that the first belongs to him. Then he opposed: What prevents him? He said: For we act with an instrument, power, treatment, and thought, and these are not equal, and if they were equal we could do that. So it is said to him: The aspects that prevent you – are they or not? If he says: No, he makes great the statement that his treatment, thought, and the like were from God, and it is by that which he used to deny the creation of that, so he has admitted it. And if he says: Yes, it is said: The question about all that is that the Qadariyya claimed that if God had preserved them – and He has preserved them for action – and all that are acts having power, why then are they not equal, and you intended that they be equal, and you had the power? This shows that it emerges contrary to your estimation. And what was mentioned of the meaning is the clear proof that perhaps something more excellent and more equal than the first may emerge with the intention of equality, and that that was so not by it. Along with the fact that no one's knowledge reaches to estimating its movement from air, place, and the raising and lowering of the hand, even if he exerted every effort, and the action is not free from that, it is established that it is from other than him from this aspect. And there is no power except with God. And upon that, no one intends the intention of making the action ugly, and it may be so, it is established that from that aspect it is not his. And if an action were permissible to occur from an aspect he does not know nor intend – even if he exerted every effort to know its limit and extent and that it be his according to what it is – then its like would be permissible in the entire world, the miracles of the messengers, and other things. And the statement about the painter is the statement about the agent, and about the picture is the statement about the action, there is no difference between them. Then his saying: "If God had preserved the power" – and from his doctrine is that power does not last two moments – has no meaning. Then what he opposed from action: he chooses it, knows what he does, and intends it, so from that aspect it is his – and we have shown in that what is not that he knows that aspect. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectical argument. The opponent (Mu'tazili) uses the example of a writer or painter: if someone cannot produce an identical copy of his own work, that shows the first was not truly his act but from God. The opponent first claims that the power to create the likeness is from God. Then the question is asked: why cannot one produce a similar work? The answer given is that human actions require many causes (memory, mind, etc.) that never fully coincide. The opponent argues that if such an argument necessitated another agent (muhdith) for the first, it would also necessitate another painter for the second. Then he opposes with the point that inability does not prove that we did not act; if it did, then if a second work is better, it would indicate the first is the agent's. Further objection: what prevents one from creating a perfect copy? The response: humans act with instruments, power, treatment, and thought, which are not all equal; if they were equal, they could do it. Then Maturidi presents a dilemma: either these preventing aspects are from God or not. If they are from God, then the opponent has admitted that creation is from God. If they are from the agent, then why are they not equal, given the Qadariyya believe God has given the power? This inconsistency shows that the act emerges from a source other than the human. The inability to control all variables (air, place, hand movements) proves the act is from another (God). Moreover, an act cannot be attributed to a person if it occurs from an aspect he does not know or intend, and this would unravel miracles and other divine signs. Finally, Maturidi equates the painter to the agent and the picture to the action, and dismisses the opponent's argument about the non-permanence of power as meaningless. He concludes that although the opponent claims to choose and know his act, Maturidi has shown that the agent does not truly know that aspect.

Key Terms
الكاتب والمصور
العجز
القدرة
الأسباب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's initial response, why can a writer not produce an identical copy of his work?
A) Because God directly prevents him
B) Because the necessary causes (mind, memory, etc.) do not fully come together
C) Because he lacks the original power
D) Because the first work was created by God alone

Key Takeaway
The writer/painter example, when properly analyzed, shows that human inability to replicate is due to incomplete causes, not absence of agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fail to produce a perfect result—whether in writing, art, or any endeavor—do you immediately blame your own shortcomings, or do you see Allah's hand in the incompleteness of causes? How will you remind yourself today that every success and failure is under His control?
?
The writer cannot replicate his work because the causes never align. How does this truth bite into your pride? Write down one achievement you are most proud of, then sincerely thank Allah for it, acknowledging that He granted the power and aligned the causes for you.
?
If you truly believed that your inability to do something (like creating a perfect copy) does not deny your agency but reveals your dependence on Allah, how would your attitude change when you fall short? Commit to one specific action today that reflects this reliance.

The Dilemma of Preventing Factors
Maturidi poses a dilemma to the Mu'tazili about the source of preventing factors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's dilemma argument concerning the factors that prevent a perfect replication.

Core Idea
The factors that prevent replication either come from God or from the agent—both lead to divine involvement.

After the initial exchange, Maturidi presents a critical dilemma. He asks the opponent whether the aspects preventing an exact copy are from God or not. This dilemma exposes the inconsistency in the Mu'tazili position.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma passage
Focus on the question: 'Are the preventing factors from God or not?' and the two possible answers.
2
Analyze the first option
If the opponent says the factors are not from God, then he admits that all causes (treatment, thought, etc.) are from God, which means he accepts divine creation.
This contradicts his original denial.
3
Analyze the second option
If the factors are from the agent himself, ask why they are not equal when he has power from God. This shows inconsistency in the Qadariyya belief.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The outcome is that the act emerges contrary to the agent's estimation, indicating it is from another (God).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: واحتج بالكاتب والمصور، إنه لو أراد أن يخرج الثاني على ما عليه الأول لم يمكنه، دل أن الأول لم يخرج على ذلك به. فزعم أولاً أنه يجوز أن يكون كذلك بما ألقاه الله فيه تلك القدرة. فإن قيل: لم لا يأتي بمثله؟ فأجاب بأنا، وإن كنا نفعل بتلك القدرة، نفعله بأسباب لا تجتمع بكليتها حتى لا يخرج منها شيء من نحو الذهن والحفظ وأنواع الأشغال. قال: ولو وجب بهذا محدث آخر ليجب به مصور آخر. وعارض بالفعل؛ إنه فعلك لم يدل العجز على أنا لم نفعل. ولو كان العجز يدل على ما ذكروا لكان الثاني إذا كان أحسن، فدل أن الأول له. ثم عارض أنه ما يمنعه؟ فقال: لأنا نفعل بآلة وقدرة وعلاج وفكر، ولا تستوى هذه، ولو استوت أمكننا ذلك. فيقال له: الوجوه التي تمنعك هل هي أو لا؟ فإن قال: لا، أعظم القول أن علاجه وفكره ونحو ذلك كان من الله، وهو الذي به أنكر خلق ذلك، فقد أقر به. وإن قال: بلى، قيل: السؤال عن ذلك كله أن القدرية زعمت أن الله لو أبقاها، وقد أبقاها للفعل، وكل ذلك أفعال لها القدرة، فما بالها لم تستو، وقد قصدت أن تستوى، وكانت لك القدرة؟ فهذا يبيّن أنه على غير تقديرك يخرج. وما ذكر من المعنى هو الدليل الواضح أن قد يخرج أحسن من الأول وأسوى على قصد الاستواء على أن ذلك كان كذلك لا به. مع ما لا يبلغ علم أحد إلى تقدير حركته من الهواء والمكان ومن ارتفاع اليد وانخفاضها لو اجتهد كل الجهد والفعل لا يخلو عنه، ثبت أنه لغيره من هذا الوجه. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعلى ذلك لا أحد يقصد قصد تقبيح الفعل، وقد يكون كذلك، ثبت أنه من ذلك الوجه ليس له. ولو جاز وقوع فعل من وجه لا يعلمه ولا يريده ولو اجتهد كل جهده ليعرف حده ومبلغه ويكون له على ما هو عليه ذلك الفعل، لجاز مثله في جميع العالم وآيات الرسل وغيرها. والقول في المصور هو القول في الفاعل، وفي الصورة هو القول في الفعل، لا فرق بينهما. ثم قوله: «لو أبقى الله القوة» ومن مذهبه أن القوة لا تبقى وقتين لا معنى له. ثم ما عارض من الفعل فهو يختاره ويعلم ما يفعله ويقصده فهو من ذلك الوجه له، وقد بينا في ذلك ما ليس هو بعالم بذلك الوجه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
He said: And he argued by the writer and painter: if he wanted to produce the second like the first, he cannot, indicating that the first was not produced by him in that way. So he claimed first that it is permissible that it be so because God cast that power into him. If it is said: Why does he not produce its like? He answered: We, even if we act by that power, we act by causes that do not completely come together so that nothing emerges from them from such as the mind, memory, and types of occupations. He said: And if by this another instigator were necessitated, then another painter would be necessitated by it. And he opposed by action: Surely your action – inability does not indicate that we did not act. And if inability indicated what they mentioned, then the second, if it were better, would indicate that the first belongs to him. Then he opposed: What prevents him? He said: For we act with an instrument, power, treatment, and thought, and these are not equal, and if they were equal we could do that. So it is said to him: The aspects that prevent you – are they or not? If he says: No, he makes great the statement that his treatment, thought, and the like were from God, and it is by that which he used to deny the creation of that, so he has admitted it. And if he says: Yes, it is said: The question about all that is that the Qadariyya claimed that if God had preserved them – and He has preserved them for action – and all that are acts having power, why then are they not equal, and you intended that they be equal, and you had the power? This shows that it emerges contrary to your estimation. And what was mentioned of the meaning is the clear proof that perhaps something more excellent and more equal than the first may emerge with the intention of equality, and that that was so not by it. Along with the fact that no one's knowledge reaches to estimating its movement from air, place, and the raising and lowering of the hand, even if he exerted every effort, and the action is not free from that, it is established that it is from other than him from this aspect. And there is no power except with God. And upon that, no one intends the intention of making the action ugly, and it may be so, it is established that from that aspect it is not his. And if an action were permissible to occur from an aspect he does not know nor intend – even if he exerted every effort to know its limit and extent and that it be his according to what it is – then its like would be permissible in the entire world, the miracles of the messengers, and other things. And the statement about the painter is the statement about the agent, and about the picture is the statement about the action, there is no difference between them. Then his saying: "If God had preserved the power" – and from his doctrine is that power does not last two moments – has no meaning. Then what he opposed from action: he chooses it, knows what he does, and intends it, so from that aspect it is his – and we have shown in that what is not that he knows that aspect. And there is no power except with God.

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectical argument. The opponent (Mu'tazili) uses the example of a writer or painter: if someone cannot produce an identical copy of his own work, that shows the first was not truly his act but from God. The opponent first claims that the power to create the likeness is from God. Then the question is asked: why cannot one produce a similar work? The answer given is that human actions require many causes (memory, mind, etc.) that never fully coincide. The opponent argues that if such an argument necessitated another agent (muhdith) for the first, it would also necessitate another painter for the second. Then he opposes with the point that inability does not prove that we did not act; if it did, then if a second work is better, it would indicate the first is the agent's. Further objection: what prevents one from creating a perfect copy? The response: humans act with instruments, power, treatment, and thought, which are not all equal; if they were equal, they could do it. Then Maturidi presents a dilemma: either these preventing aspects are from God or not. If they are from God, then the opponent has admitted that creation is from God. If they are from the agent, then why are they not equal, given the Qadariyya believe God has given the power? This inconsistency shows that the act emerges from a source other than the human. The inability to control all variables (air, place, hand movements) proves the act is from another (God). Moreover, an act cannot be attributed to a person if it occurs from an aspect he does not know or intend, and this would unravel miracles and other divine signs. Finally, Maturidi equates the painter to the agent and the picture to the action, and dismisses the opponent's argument about the non-permanence of power as meaningless. He concludes that although the opponent claims to choose and know his act, Maturidi has shown that the agent does not truly know that aspect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that human inability to replicate a perfect copy is purely due to human limitation, with no divine involvement.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the very factors preventing replication point to divine agency, as they are either from God or inconsistent with human power.

Checkpoint
According to the dilemma, what does the opponent admit if he says the preventing factors are not from God?
A) That he has full control over his actions
B) That his treatment and thought are from God
C) That he can produce a perfect copy
D) That God does not exist

Key Takeaway
The dilemma forces the opponent to admit divine involvement in the factors that prevent replication, undermining his original argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan and work hard, do you ever feel that your success is entirely your own doing? How does this passage's claim that even your thoughts and treatments are from God challenge your sense of self-reliance?
?
Think of a time you failed despite your best efforts. Can you see in hindsight that there were preventing factors beyond your control? Does this dilemma help you accept that your outcomes are ultimately in God's hands, not yours?
?
If you truly believed that every factor that prevents or enables your actions comes from God, how would that change the way you approach your daily tasks and your attitude toward success and failure?

The Clear Proof and Final Conclusion
Maturidi presents the final clear proof and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's final proof that human acts are from God and the implications for miracles.

Core Idea
The inability to control all variables proves acts are from God; otherwise miracles would be ascribable to humans.

Maturidi concludes his refutation by presenting a clear proof: no one can fully estimate all the variables in an act (air, place, hand movements), so the act must come from another (God). He then extends this reasoning to deny that an act can be attributed to a person if it occurs from an aspect they do not know or intend, which would otherwise make miracles ordinary.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the argument about the inability to estimate movement.
2
Understand the proof from variables
Maturidi argues that no one can estimate the exact movement of air, place, and hand, so the act cannot be fully attributed to the human.
This shows the act is from another, i.e., God.
3
Examine the extension to miracles
If an act could occur from an aspect unknown and unintended, then miracles and divine signs would be no different from ordinary acts.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Note the final points
Maturidi equates the painter to the agent and the picture to the action, and dismisses the opponent's argument about power not lasting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: واحتج بالكاتب والمصور، إنه لو أراد أن يخرج الثاني على ما عليه الأول لم يمكنه، دل أن الأول لم يخرج على ذلك به. فزعم أولاً أنه يجوز أن يكون كذلك بما ألقاه الله فيه تلك القدرة. فإن قيل: لم لا يأتي بمثله؟ فأجاب بأنا، وإن كنا نفعل بتلك القدرة، نفعله بأسباب لا تجتمع بكليتها حتى لا يخرج منها شيء من نحو الذهن والحفظ وأنواع الأشغال. قال: ولو وجب بهذا محدث آخر ليجب به مصور آخر. وعارض بالفعل؛ إنه فعلك لم يدل العجز على أنا لم نفعل. ولو كان العجز يدل على ما ذكروا لكان الثاني إذا كان أحسن، فدل أن الأول له. ثم عارض أنه ما يمنعه؟ فقال: لأنا نفعل بآلة وقدرة وعلاج وفكر، ولا تستوى هذه، ولو استوت أمكننا ذلك. فيقال له: الوجوه التي تمنعك هل هي أو لا؟ فإن قال: لا، أعظم القول أن علاجه وفكره ونحو ذلك كان من الله، وهو الذي به أنكر خلق ذلك، فقد أقر به. وإن قال: بلى، قيل: السؤال عن ذلك كله أن القدرية زعمت أن الله لو أبقاها، وقد أبقاها للفعل، وكل ذلك أفعال لها القدرة، فما بالها لم تستو، وقد قصدت أن تستوى، وكانت لك القدرة؟ فهذا يبيّن أنه على غير تقديرك يخرج. وما ذكر من المعنى هو الدليل الواضح أن قد يخرج أحسن من الأول وأسوى على قصد الاستواء على أن ذلك كان كذلك لا به. مع ما لا يبلغ علم أحد إلى تقدير حركته من الهواء والمكان ومن ارتفاع اليد وانخفاضها لو اجتهد كل الجهد والفعل لا يخلو عنه، ثبت أنه لغيره من هذا الوجه. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعلى ذلك لا أحد يقصد قصد تقبيح الفعل، وقد يكون كذلك، ثبت أنه من ذلك الوجه ليس له. ولو جاز وقوع فعل من وجه لا يعلمه ولا يريده ولو اجتهد كل جهده ليعرف حده ومبلغه ويكون له على ما هو عليه ذلك الفعل، لجاز مثله في جميع العالم وآيات الرسل وغيرها. والقول في المصور هو القول في الفاعل، وفي الصورة هو القول في الفعل، لا فرق بينهما. ثم قوله: «لو أبقى الله القوة» ومن مذهبه أن القوة لا تبقى وقتين لا معنى له. ثم ما عارض من الفعل فهو يختاره ويعلم ما يفعله ويقصده فهو من ذلك الوجه له، وقد بينا في ذلك ما ليس هو بعالم بذلك الوجه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
He said: And he argued by the writer and painter: if he wanted to produce the second like the first, he cannot, indicating that the first was not produced by him in that way. So he claimed first that it is permissible that it be so because God cast that power into him. If it is said: Why does he not produce its like? He answered: We, even if we act by that power, we act by causes that do not completely come together so that nothing emerges from them from such as the mind, memory, and types of occupations. He said: And if by this another instigator were necessitated, then another painter would be necessitated by it. And he opposed by action: Surely your action – inability does not indicate that we did not act. And if inability indicated what they mentioned, then the second, if it were better, would indicate that the first belongs to him. Then he opposed: What prevents him? He said: For we act with an instrument, power, treatment, and thought, and these are not equal, and if they were equal we could do that. So it is said to him: The aspects that prevent you – are they or not? If he says: No, he makes great the statement that his treatment, thought, and the like were from God, and it is by that which he used to deny the creation of that, so he has admitted it. And if he says: Yes, it is said: The question about all that is that the Qadariyya claimed that if God had preserved them – and He has preserved them for action – and all that are acts having power, why then are they not equal, and you intended that they be equal, and you had the power? This shows that it emerges contrary to your estimation. And what was mentioned of the meaning is the clear proof that perhaps something more excellent and more equal than the first may emerge with the intention of equality, and that that was so not by it. Along with the fact that no one's knowledge reaches to estimating its movement from air, place, and the raising and lowering of the hand, even if he exerted every effort, and the action is not free from that, it is established that it is from other than him from this aspect. And there is no power except with God. And upon that, no one intends the intention of making the action ugly, and it may be so, it is established that from that aspect it is not his. And if an action were permissible to occur from an aspect he does not know nor intend – even if he exerted every effort to know its limit and extent and that it be his according to what it is – then its like would be permissible in the entire world, the miracles of the messengers, and other things. And the statement about the painter is the statement about the agent, and about the picture is the statement about the action, there is no difference between them. Then his saying: "If God had preserved the power" – and from his doctrine is that power does not last two moments – has no meaning. Then what he opposed from action: he chooses it, knows what he does, and intends it, so from that aspect it is his – and we have shown in that what is not that he knows that aspect. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectical argument. The opponent (Mu'tazili) uses the example of a writer or painter: if someone cannot produce an identical copy of his own work, that shows the first was not truly his act but from God. The opponent first claims that the power to create the likeness is from God. Then the question is asked: why cannot one produce a similar work? The answer given is that human actions require many causes (memory, mind, etc.) that never fully coincide. The opponent argues that if such an argument necessitated another agent (muhdith) for the first, it would also necessitate another painter for the second. Then he opposes with the point that inability does not prove that we did not act; if it did, then if a second work is better, it would indicate the first is the agent's. Further objection: what prevents one from creating a perfect copy? The response: humans act with instruments, power, treatment, and thought, which are not all equal; if they were equal, they could do it. Then Maturidi presents a dilemma: either these preventing aspects are from God or not. If they are from God, then the opponent has admitted that creation is from God. If they are from the agent, then why are they not equal, given the Qadariyya believe God has given the power? This inconsistency shows that the act emerges from a source other than the human. The inability to control all variables (air, place, hand movements) proves the act is from another (God). Moreover, an act cannot be attributed to a person if it occurs from an aspect he does not know or intend, and this would unravel miracles and other divine signs. Finally, Maturidi equates the painter to the agent and the picture to the action, and dismisses the opponent's argument about the non-permanence of power as meaningless. He concludes that although the opponent claims to choose and know his act, Maturidi has shown that the agent does not truly know that aspect.

القوة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that human actions are fully under human control and that God's involvement is not needed for ordinary acts.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even the simplest actions involve countless unknowable variables, proving divine agency.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say would happen if an action could occur from an aspect the agent does not know or intend?
A) Only the best actions would occur
B) Miracles and divine signs would be ordinary and permissible for anyone
C) Humans would gain full control
D) God's power would be limited

Key Takeaway
Human inability to control all variables proves acts are from God, and denying this would undermine miracles.

Why Power Cannot Remain After Inability
Maturidi argues that power cannot remain after the occurrence of inability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why power cannot remain after the occurrence of inability according to Maturidi's argument.

Core Idea
Power after inability is a logical contradiction.

Maturidi presents a concise proof that if power were to continue after one becomes incapable, it would paradoxically be directed at an impossible action. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for understanding divine and human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the structure of the argument sink in before you analyze it.
2
Identify the key premises
The argument relies on two premises: (1) power is for actions, and (2) inability can occur after one has power. Notice these in the text.
3
Trace the conditional logic
Follow the conditional: 'if power were for the action after it [inability], then it would be for that which he is incapable of.' See how this follows from the premises.
4
Recognize the contradiction
The conditional leads to a contradiction: power cannot be for something impossible. Thus the original assumption (that power remains) is false.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The final phrase 'There is no power except through Allah' emphasizes that all true power comes from Allah, aligning with the refutation of independent human power after inability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن القوة إذ هي للأفعال وجائز حدوث العجز بعد الوصف بالقدرة، فلو كانت القدرة للفعل بعده لكانت لما لكانت لما هو عنه عاجز، وذلك متناقض فاسد. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, if power is for actions, and it is possible for inability to occur after being described with power, then if power were for the action after it, it would be for that which he is incapable of, and that is a contradictory corruption. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by contradiction. Maturidi begins with the premise that power (qudra) is inherently for actions. He then observes that it is possible for inability (ajz) to occur after one has been described with power. The conditional conclusion is that if power were to remain for the action even after the onset of inability, then power would be directed toward something that the agent is incapable of performing. This is a logical contradiction because power is supposed to enable action, not be associated with its impossibility. Therefore, the supposition that power remains after inability is invalid. The argument concludes with the statement 'There is no power except through Allah,' emphasizing reliance on divine power.

Key Terms
قوة
قدرة
عجز
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that power can exist even when one is unable to act.
Correction: Maturidi shows this leads to a contradiction: power would then be for an impossible action, which is incoherent.

Checkpoint
What is the main refutation in this argument?
A) Power is not for actions
B) Power cannot remain after inability because it would be for an impossible action
C) Inability cannot occur after power
D) Power and inability are the same

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's proof shows that power cannot remain after the arrival of inability because it would entail a logical contradiction.

Against Independence from God Through Powers
Maturidi argues against independence from God through powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that powers (quwa) cannot be independent entities because that would lead to human independence from Allah, which contradicts divine transcendence and need.

Core Idea
Powers are not self-sufficient; they are created by Allah.

Imam Maturidi presents a theological argument against the notion that human powers are independent causal agents. He shows that if powers were for states, humans would be independent of Allah before any action exists. This argument reinforces the fundamental Islamic principle that all power belongs to Allah alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the argument's flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
2
Identify the premises and conclusion
Find the hypothesis ('if powers were for states'), the consequence ('independence from Allah'), the counter-premise ('Allah made creation needy'), and the final conclusion ('no power except through Allah').
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the principle
Maturidi states a principle: powers do not persist, so need for persistence ceases. Understand why this undermines the idea of independent powers.
This connects to the broader concept of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن القوى لو كانت لأحوال ترد لكان بها يستغنى عن الله في جميع الأفعال قبل وجود الأفعال، والله جل ثناؤه صيّر الخلق جميعًا فقراء إليه وهو الغنى الحميد؛ لم يجز أن يقع لهم الغنى عنه بأحق ما لزمت الحاجة. والأصل أنها إذ كانت لا تبقى تزول حاجة البقاء، والفعل ليس بموجود، فيصير غنيا عن الله قبل كونه، وذلك قبيح في السمع. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, if powers were for states that occur, then by them one would be independent of Allah in all actions before the existence of actions, and Allah, may His praise be exalted, made all creation needy to Him, and He is the Rich, the Praiseworthy; it is not permissible for them to be independent of Him with the most deserving thing that necessitates need. And the principle is that since they do not persist, the need for persistence ceases, and the action does not exist, so one becomes independent of Allah before its existence, and that is ugly in hearing. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This argument refutes the idea that powers (quwa) exist as independent entities that allow humans to act without divine involvement. Maturidi states that if powers were for states that come to be, humans would become independent of God before actions occur. But God has made all creation needy to Him. The principle is that powers do not persist, so there is no ongoing need for them. Since the action is not yet existent, claiming independence before the action is blameworthy. The argument underscores that all power belongs to God.

No Capable Agent Is Without an Act
Maturidi argues that capability and action are logically inseparable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi insists that a capable agent cannot exist without acting, and why common counterexamples like death or sleep are invalid.

Core Idea
Capability necessarily entails action; inability necessarily precludes it.

This lesson introduces a foundational principle of Maturidi's theology: the inseparable connection between ability and action. Imam Maturidi argues that no being with genuine capability can be devoid of action, just as an incapable being cannot perform an act. He addresses potential objections involving death and life, showing they do not undermine this logical necessity. Grasping this argument clarifies the nature of divine and human agency in the Maturidi school.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the parallel phrasing between 'no capable agent without act' and 'no incapable agent acting'.
Pay attention to the logical symmetry.
2
Identify the main claim
Maturidi's central claim is that capability and action are inseparable. He asserts that it is impossible to have a capable agent who is not acting.
3
Examine the counterexamples
Maturidi addresses death and life as potential exceptions, but dismisses them. Death is a form of inability, and life does not guarantee action in every moment.
This reinforces the rule, not breaks it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إنه لا يوجد قادر غير فاعل ألبتة كما لا يوجد عاجز فاعلاً، لم يجز القضاء بالقدرة ونفي الفعل، كما لا يجوز العجز ووجوده؛ إذ هما جميعا في الخروج عن الموجود واحد؛ مع القول بالبعد عن ذلك من طريق العقل، من حيث تضاد المعنى في الحقيقة. وليس بالموت ونحوه معتبر، لأن الموت عجز في الجملة وليست الحياة بقدرة في الجملة، ولما يجوز وجود الحياة أوقاتاً لا فعل معها، ولا يجوز وجود قادر وقتين لا فعل له. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, there is absolutely no capable agent who is not acting, just as there is no incapable agent who is acting; so it is not permissible to judge [him] with ability and negate action, just as it is not permissible [to judge with] inability and [negate] its existence; for both are one in exiting from the existent; along with the saying of remoteness from that by way of reason, from the aspect of opposition of meaning in reality. And death and the like are not considered, because death is inability in general, and life is not ability in general, and it is not permissible for life to exist at times with no action, nor is it permissible for a capable [agent] to exist for two periods with no action. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This argument establishes the inseparability of capability and action. Maturidi states that a capable agent (qadir) must always be acting, just as an incapable agent (ajiz) cannot act. Therefore, any judgment that combines ability with the negation of action is invalid. He dismisses exceptions like death or sleep because death is a form of inability, and life is not equivalent to ability. The argument reinforces that ability is inherently linked to performance.

عجز
فعل

Misconception: Some think that a capable being can exist without acting, such as during death or sleep.
Correction: Maturidi argues that death is a form of inability, not a state of capability, and life does not guarantee action at every moment—but the principle holds that no capable agent is ever without action.

Key Takeaway

Causes Necessitate Effects in the Witnessed World
Maturidi argues that causes in the empirical world necessarily produce their effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that empirical causes necessarily bring about their effects, and how this relates to divine action in preeternity.

Core Idea
In the created world, causes necessitate effects by necessity or choice.

Imam Maturidi begins by grounding his theology in observable causality. He argues that in the witnessed world, when a cause is present, its effect inevitably follows—whether the cause is coercive or voluntary. This principle, he shows, even applies to our understanding of God's eternal action, though without temporal attribution to Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic first, then the literal translation. Notice the examples Maturidi uses to illustrate causality.
2
Identify the two types of causes
Maturidi distinguishes between coercive (necessary) causes and voluntary (choice-based) causes. Find examples of each in the text.
Coercive: exiting with removal. Voluntary: God's guardianship with faith.
3
Understand the application to divine action
The passage ends by clarifying that God's action is eternal, but when attributed to a temporal object, the time belongs to the object. This preserves divine transcendence.
This shows how causality does not force temporality on God.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why Maturidi establishes causality in the witnessed world. It lays the foundation for his arguments about divine attributes and creation.
This is a key move in his rational theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سبب وأيضا إنا نجد الأسباب في الشاهد إذا كانت بحيث لا توجد دون ما له هي أوقاتا توجب كون الأشياء، مع ما كان ذلك اختيارًا أو اضطرارًا؛ من ذلك نحو الخروج مع الإخراج والزوال مع الإزالة والألم مع الضرب واللذة مع الملذ والتعب والعناء مع الفعل؛ ثم الاختيار من ذلك نحو ولاية الله مع الإيمان وعداوته مع الكفر، وكذلك القبول والرد ونحو ذلك. وعلى ذلك حق التسمية بالأشياء والحكم بها. وإن كان الله تعالى موصوفا بالفعل في الأزل، فإنه عند اقتران ذكره بغيره يذكر الوقت له مما لذلك الغير، كما يقال: لم يزل به كائنا وقت كونه وموجودًا وقت وجوده ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Reason: And also we find causes in the witnessed world, when they are such that they do not exist without that which they have at times, they necessitate the existence of things, whether that is by choice or necessity. Among that are: exiting with removal, and vanishing with elimination, and pain with striking, and pleasure with the pleasurable, and fatigue and hardship with action. Then among that by choice are: the guardianship of Allah with faith and His enmity with disbelief, and likewise acceptance and rejection and the like. And upon that is the proper naming of things and judgment on them. And if Allah, exalted, is described with action in preeternity, then when His mention is coupled with another, the time is mentioned for Him due to that other, as it is said: He has not ceased being with it at the time of its existence and existing at the time of its existence. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage discusses causality in the empirical world. Maturidi notes that causes necessitate their effects, whether by coercion or choice. He gives examples: being taken out necessitates going out, removal necessitates disappearance, striking necessitates pain, etc. In voluntary matters, faith brings about God's guardianship, and disbelief brings about His enmity. He then relates this to divine action: although God is described as acting in preeternity, when His action is connected to a temporal object, the time attribution is to that object. This maintains divine transcendence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's action in preeternity means He acts in time.
Correction: Maturidi explains that when God's action is mentioned with a temporal object, the time refers to the object, not to God. God's action is eternal without temporal location.

Checkpoint
In this passage, what example does Maturidi give of a voluntary cause?
A) Exiting with removal
B) Pain with striking
C) Guardianship of Allah with faith
D) Vanishing with elimination

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes empirical causality as a basis for understanding divine action, ensuring that God's eternity is not compromised by temporal attribution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though every cause in your life—from your morning coffee to your health—is dependent on Allah's power, or do you functionally attribute independence to these causes? What would change if you internalized that 'there is no power except through Allah' in your daily routine?
?
Maturidi says that God's guardianship is necessarily linked to faith, and His enmity to disbelief. How does your heart respond to the weight of this causal reality? Are you relying on your faith as the means to Allah's guardianship, or are you assuming His goodwill without the corresponding cause?
?
If God's action is eternal but manifests in time, how does that reshape your view of your own actions? Your deeds today are not just isolated events; they are the temporal expressions of something eternal. Does this make you more mindful of what you choose to do and why?

Causes in the Witnessed World and Divine Action
Al-Maturidi argues from observed causality to divine action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Maturidi uses observed causality to explain how we attribute actions to God without temporalizing His essence.

Core Idea
Causes necessitate effects; God's action is timelessly related to creation.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi presents a key argument for how we understand God's relationship to the world. He looks at the witnessed world where causes are regularly linked to effects, whether voluntary or involuntary. Then he applies that pattern to divine attributes like friendship and enmity, showing that God's eternal action is expressed in time through the existence of created things.

Guided Path
1
Observe cause-effect in the witnessed world
Read the first part where al-Maturidi states that causes necessitate effects, and note the examples of physical and voluntary causes.
Notice that he distinguishes between choice (ikhtiyar) and compulsion (idtirar).
2
Apply to divine attributes
See how he links God's friendship with faith and enmity with disbelief, establishing a consistent pattern.
This shows the cause-effect relationship is constant even in voluntary matters.
3
Understand temporal attribution
Read the final part about God's pre-eternal action and how we speak of time only in relation to created things.
This preserves divine transcendence while acknowledging temporal relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إنا نجد الأسباب في الشاهد - إذا كانت بحيث لا توجد دون ما له هي أوقاتا - توجب كون الأشياء، مع ما كان ذلك اختيارًا أو اضطرارًا؛ من ذلك نحو الخروج مع الإخراج والزوال مع الإزالة والألم مع الضرب واللذة مع الملذ والتعب والعناء مع الفعل؛ ثم الاختيار من ذلك نحو ولاية الله مع الإيمان وعداوته مع الكفر، وكذلك القبول والرد ونحو ذلك. وعلى ذلك حق التسمية بالأشياء والحكم بها. وإن كان الله تعالى موصوفا بالفعل في الأزل، فإنه عند اقتران ذكره بغيره يذكر الوقت له مما لذلك الغير، كما يقال: لم يزل به كائنا وقت كونه وموجودا وقت وجوده. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, we find that the causes in the witnessed world—when they are such that they do not exist without that for which they are times—necessitate the coming to be of things, whether that is by choice or by compulsion. Among that is like: emergence with bringing out, disappearance with removal, pain with striking, pleasure with the pleasant, fatigue and exertion with the action. Then choice from that is like: God's friendship with faith and His enmity with disbelief, and likewise acceptance and rejection and the like. And upon that is the true naming of things and judging by them. And if God Most High is described as acting in pre-eternity, then when His mention is coupled with another, the time is mentioned for Him due to that other, as it is said: He never ceased being with it at the time of its coming to be, and existing at the time of its existence. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is an argument from analogy (shahid) to support the relationship between causes and their effects, and then applies it to divine action. The author begins by stating that in the witnessed world, causes are such that they are concomitant with their effects (they do not exist without the effect occurring at their time). These causes necessitate the occurrence of things, whether the action is voluntary or involuntary. He gives examples: physical actions like emergence with bringing out, pain with striking, pleasure with pleasure, and fatigue with action. Then he mentions voluntary associations: God's friendship (wilaya) with faith, and enmity with disbelief, and acceptance/rejection. This shows that the cause-effect relationship is constant. Then he applies this to God: although God is described as acting from eternity (fi al-azal), when we mention God in relation to something created, we attribute time to God in terms of that created thing (e.g., God was with it at the time of its existence). This preserves divine transcendence while acknowledging temporal relations.

الاختيار
الاضطرار

عداوة
الأزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage argues that causes are independent of God's power.
Correction: Rather, it establishes a pattern for how we speak about God's relationship to creation, preserving divine omnipotence and transcendence.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue about causes in the witnessed world?
A) They are independent of God.
B) They necessitate their effects whether by choice or compulsion.
C) They only necessitate effects when chosen.
D) They are irrelevant to understanding divine action.

Key Takeaway
The pattern of cause and effect in the created world provides a basis for understanding how God's eternal attributes relate to temporal events without implying change in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that in the witnessed world, causes necessitate effects—pain follows striking. If that pattern holds, then your faith should necessarily bring about God's friendship (wilaya). Is your faith producing that closeness? If not, what does that say about the reality of your belief? Today, will you treat faith as a cause that must yield its effect, or as a mere label?
?
The passage says that when God is mentioned in relation to a creature, time is attributed to Him 'due to that other.' That means your every action at this moment is directly met by God's eternal act. Do you live as though your choices are met by an eternal, unchanging God, or do you imagine Him distant and indifferent? What would it mean to truly act knowing that God 'never ceased being with it at the time of its coming to be'?
?
God's enmity is tied to disbelief in the same necessary way that pain is tied to striking. If you find yourself in a state of heedlessness or sin, have you considered that you are actively choosing a cause that necessarily brings about divine distance? Are you willing to examine the causes you are setting in motion today—and change them before the effect becomes irreversible?

Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Distinction Between Prevention and Loss of Power
Imam al-Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite distinction between prevention and loss of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazilite distinction between prevention and loss of power logically leads to the nullification of tawhid.

Core Idea
If power does not produce its act when present, it cannot when absent.

The Mu'tazila claimed that an act being prevented is different from the loss of power to perform it, arguing that an act can occur absolutely despite prevention. Imam al-Maturidi refutes this by showing that both prevention and loss of power equally render the act impossible, and that their reasoning would imply an act occurs without any power—contradicting the very evidence for God's power and undermining tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the flow. Then read the English translation to understand the argument.
The passage is dense with theological reasoning; take your time.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
Find where the author states what the Mu'tazila claimed: that the prevented (mamnu') is not the same as the loss of power (fawat al-qudra).
They argued that an act could still occur absolutely even if prevented.
3
Follow the refutation chain
Notice how the author equates prevention and loss of power. Then trace the logical consequence: if power does not produce its act while it exists, it cannot produce it when absent.
This leads to the absurdity that the act occurs without any power.
4
Understand the consequence for tawhid
The author shows that this would make the act evidence that the agent is not powerful, which invalidates the argument for God's power from creation.
This is a serious problem because it undermines tawhid itself.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author ends by stating that if power is useless when present, its existence and non-existence are equal, forcing either an act without power or a power that is with the act (absurd).
Consider why this refutation is crucial for Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه آخر مما زعم جماعة المعتزلة أن الممنوع لا بفوت القدرة، فيقع له الفعل مع الإطلاق. فما أنكروا ذلك بفوت القدرة والمنع؛ وفوت القدرة وإحالة الفعل معه واحد. مع ما لا يجوز وجود الفعل في حال وقوع المنع بحال، ويجوز مع فقد القدرة بما تقدم من القدرة. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والأصل في ذلك أن القدرة لو لم يكن لها فعل وهي موجودة يكون بها فعل وهي غير موجودة، فتكون سببا لفعل إذا عدم القدرة في التحقيق، فيصير القول به قولا بوجود الفعل بعدم القدرة، فيكون الفعل دليلا على أن ليس الفاعل بقادر؛ وبه استدلوا على أن الله قادر، فبطل موضع الاستدلال بالشاهد؛ إذ الحق فيه أن يعلم أنه كان غير قادر وقت الفعل، فيصير الفعل دليل نفي القدرة، وفي ذلك إبطال التوحيد. ولا قوة إلا بالله. على أن وجود القدرة - إذ كانت لا تنفع وهي موجودة - فوجودها وقت الوجود وعدمها سواء، وفي ذلك لزوم القول بالفعل لا بقدرة عليه ألبتة، أو بجعل القدرة معه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And another aspect from what a group of the Mu'tazila claimed: that the prevented (mamnu') is not [the same as] the loss of power (fawat al-qudra), so the act occurs for him absolutely. They did not deny that by loss of power and prevention; and loss of power and the impossibility of the act with it are one. With [that which?] it is not permissible for the act to occur in a state of prevention at all, and it is permissible with the loss of power due to what preceded of power. And there is no power except with God. And the principle in that is: if power had no act while it exists, [but] an act occurs by it while it is non-existent, then it would be a cause for an act when power is actually absent. So the statement becomes a statement of the existence of an act without power, and the act becomes evidence that the agent is not powerful. And by it they argued that God is powerful, so the locus of inference from the witnessed world becomes invalid. For the truth in it is that it would be known that He was not powerful at the time of the act, so the act becomes evidence negating power, and in that is the nullification of tawhid. And there is no power except with God. Moreover, the existence of power—since it does not benefit while it exists—its existence at the time of existence and its non-existence are equal. And in that is the necessary conclusion of saying the act occurs without any power over it, or making power with it. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazilite argument. The Mu'tazila claimed that there is a difference between an act being prevented (mamnu') and the loss of power to perform it. They argued that if an act is prevented (e.g., by divine decree), the agent does not have power, but if power is lost, the act cannot occur. The author refutes this by stating that prevention and loss of power are effectively the same in that the act cannot occur. He then goes deeper: if power does not produce its act while it exists (i.e., it is present but the act does not happen), then it would be absurd to say that the same power produces the act when it is absent. That would mean the act occurs without power, which would imply the agent was not powerful at the time of the act. This would undermine the argument for God's power, because if God's power is compared to created powers, the analogy would break down. In tawhid, we must affirm that God is powerful at the time of His act. The argument shows that the Mu'tazilite position leads to the nullification of tawhid. The author concludes that if power is useless when present, then its presence or absence makes no difference, leading to the absurdity that acts occur without any power.

Key Terms
الممنوع
فوت القدرة
القدرة
التوحيد
الاستدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is rejecting divine predestination or suggesting that prevention is unreal.
Correction: The author is not denying predestination; he is refuting a specific logical argument from the Mu'tazila about the relationship between power and act. He argues that distinguishing prevention from loss of power leads to logical contradictions, not that prevention does not occur.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Maturidi, what is the consequence if power does not produce its act while it exists?
A) The act can still occur when power is absent.
B) The act cannot occur when power is absent.
C) Power is unnecessary for the act.
D) The agent is powerful at all times.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite attempt to differentiate prevention from loss of power collapses because it would require that an act can occur without any power, which contradicts the principle that the act proves the agent's power and thus nullifies tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila used the argument from creation to prove God's power, but their own logic would lead to the opposite. Examine your personal arguments for God's existence. Are they logically sound, or do they contain hidden contradictions you haven't confronted? This week, take one argument you use and test it for consistency—your faith in Him deserves a foundation you are not afraid to inspect.
?
If power without effect is useless, then your claim to have power over your own actions demands that you actually produce the actions you intend. Where in your life have you excused your inaction by blaming prevention rather than admitting your own lack of resolve? Before tonight, name one thing you have attributed to 'circumstances beyond your control' and ask yourself whether you truly lacked the power or simply avoided the cost.
?
This lesson shows that the act itself is evidence of the agent's power. If your actions do not testify to your belief in God's power, what do they testify to? Consider your daily habits—prayer, charity, patience. Do they reflect genuine reliance on His power or mere routine? Commit today to one act that can only be sustained by admitting your utter dependence on His qudra.

God’s Actions Transcend Human Moral Judgment
Maturidi refutes analogical reasoning from human to divine actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that what appears harmful or unjust in human relations cannot be applied to God, because God is transcendent and His wisdom is beyond human comprehension.

Core Idea
Human standards of good and evil do not apply to God's actions.

Maturidi engages with a common objection: if a master gives his slave something knowing it will lead to disobedience, isn't that foolish? He uses this analogy to show that what is considered ugly in the human world does not transfer to God. The Mu'tazila had argued that God's giving strength to the disbeliever is problematic, but Maturidi explains that God's wisdom includes purposes beyond our limited understanding. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi builds his argument: he states a principle, raises an objection, then replies.
The passage contains a self-objection (معارضة) which is a rhetorical device.
2
Identify the key analogy
Find the analogy of the master and slave. What is the scenario? Why might it be considered 'ugly'? How does Maturidi reframe it as possibly wise?
3
Understand the Mu'tazila position
Locate where Maturidi mentions the Mu'tazila claim. What do they assert about God giving strength for faith? Why does Maturidi reject that comparison?
The Mu'tazila argued that God's giving strength to the disbeliever is inconsistent with His wisdom.
4
Grasp the transcendence conclusion
The final paragraph states that what is ugly in the witnessed world (shahid) does not apply to the unseen (ghaib). Why? Because God is exalted above human reasoning. Note the phrase 'لا يحتمل أمر الغائب' (it cannot apply to the unseen).
This is a foundational principle in Maturidi's theology: God's actions are not analogous to human actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال: ذلك الذي قيل فيمن يفعل الحاجة قيل. والأول أيضا قبح ممن لا يملك التقوية لو طلب منه وتضرع إليه. ثم عارض نفسه بمن يدفع إلى عبده ما يعلم أنه يعصيه به; فقال: قد يكون ذلك حكمة، نحو من يعلم بخبر الرسول أنه لا يؤمن يجوز أن يطعمه، ونحو ذلك مما يعلم من تأمله جهله بما عارض به نفسه، لأن الذي ذكره لا يجوز أن يعطيه ليؤمن بذلك بعد علمه بأنه لا يفعل، وإنما يعطيه لمنافع سوى هذا. والمعتزلة تزعم أنه أعطى القوة ليؤمن بها، وهو يعلم أنه يكفر بها، فليس ذلك مما قدر في شيء. ثم المعارضة كانت فيمن يعصيه، فلا أحد يعد نفسه في الحكماء إذا علم أن عبده بالذي يعطيه يعصيه ولا يكتسب رضاه بل يعمل بعدواته وشتمه ولا قوة إلا بالله. ولكن ذا عندنا إنما قبح في الشاهد لأن ذلك يضره ويدخل عليه الألم، وذلك لا يحتمل أمر الغائب. والله الموفق. ثم قال: لأنه ليس لمن حضر أن يمتحن ونحو ذلك; فأنى له هذا بعد تقديره فعل الغائب بالشاهد على تحقيق ما يجد فيه لأنه يقابل بجميع ما أنكر وادعى الخروج من الحكمة. إن ذلك في فعل من ذلك وصفه، فأما الله سبحانه فهو متعال عن وقوف عقل مثله على حقيقة ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he said: That which was said about one who does a need is said. And the first [action] is also ugly from one who does not have the power to give strength if it is requested of him and he is supplicated. Then he objected to himself with [the case of] one who gives to his slave what he knows that he will disobey him with; he said: That may be wisdom, such as one who knows by the news of the messenger that he will not believe, [yet] it is permissible to feed him, and similar to that which he knows from his reflection—ignorance of what he objected with to himself, because what he mentioned is not permissible for him to give to him so that he may believe by that after knowing that he will not do [so]; rather, he gives it for benefits other than this. And the Mu'tazila claim that He gave the strength so that he may believe by it, while He knows that he will disbelieve by it; that is not something that is determined in anything. Then the objection was regarding one who disobeys Him, so no one counts himself among the wise if he knows that his slave [by what he gives him] disobeys him and does not earn his pleasure but rather works with hostility and insult—and there is no power except through God. But this, with us, is considered ugly in the witnessed [world] because it harms him and brings him pain, and that cannot apply to the unseen. And God is the giver of success. Then he said: Because it is not for one who is present to test and the like; so how can he have this after his estimation of the action of the unseen by the witnessed to verify what he finds in it, because it is confronted by all that he denied and claimed to be outside of wisdom. That is in the action of one who has that description; as for God the Exalted, He is exalted above the comprehension of a mind like his over the reality of that. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is an argument using an analogy of a master and slave to show that God's actions cannot be judged by human standards of good and evil. Maturidi begins by noting that an action deemed ugly in a person who lacks power does not apply to God. He then presents a self-objection: a master giving his slave something knowing it will lead to disobedience might still be wise if there are other benefits. The Mu'tazila claim that God gives strength for faith while knowing the result, but Maturidi argues this is not the same. In human terms, such an action is considered ugly because it causes harm, but this does not apply to God because He is transcendent. Thus, what appears evil in creation does not entail that God's actions are evil. The conclusion is that God's wisdom is beyond human understanding.

Key Terms
شاهد
غائب
حكمة
قبح
متعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That actions which appear harmful or unjust in human relationships imply the same about God's actions.
Correction: Maturidi explains that what is considered ugly in the witnessed world (shahid) because it involves harm and pain does not apply to God (ghaib), because God is free from such limitations and His wisdom is beyond human analogy.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say that the master giving his slave something to disobey is considered ugly in the witnessed world?
A) Because it always leads to harm.
B) Because it causes the master pain and harm.
C) Because it is always without any benefit.
D) Because the slave does not deserve it.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi firmly establishes that God's actions are not to be judged by human standards of good and evil because God is transcendent and beyond the limitations of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or see someone else suffering, do you ever secretly question God’s wisdom or fairness? After this lesson, how will you fight that inclination by reminding yourself that His actions are beyond your moral categories?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed that God’s giving strength to a disbeliever is unjust because He knows the outcome. Have you ever judged God’s allowance of evil as 'unfair'? What step will you take today to replace that judgment with trust in His hidden wisdom?
?
If you truly internalize that God is not harmed by your disobedience the way a human master would be, how would your fear of God change? Would it shift from fear of punishment to a deeper reverence for His majesty? Commit to one change in your relationship with Him starting now.

Action Occurs with Power: The Motion Analogy
Maturidi uses motion analogy to prove action accompanies power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that action must accompany power, illustrated by the example of motion of necessity and choice.

Core Idea
Power and action are simultaneous; action does not occur by power's absence.

Maturidi begins his proof by drawing an analogy from motion. He distinguishes between necessary motion (like involuntary movement) and voluntary motion (choice), each associated with either incapacity or power. This sets up the key insight: if power were such that action never accompanies it, then action would be attributed to the absence of power, which is incoherent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice the opening analogy with motion.
2
Identify the two types of motion
Find the distinction between motion of necessity and motion of choice, and how each relates to incapacity or power.
This is the foundation of the analogy.
3
Understand the conditional
Grasp the point: if power never coexists with action, then action would be by power's absence, which is absurd.
This leads to the conclusion that action occurs with power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كالحركة مع خلق الله الجسم، لا حركة ضرورة ولا اختيار؛ ونوع حركة الضرورة قد يكون مع العجز، ومثله الاختيار مع القدرة على أن القدرة لو كانت بحيث لا يجامعها الفعل ليبطل أن يكون بها الفعل بل بعدمها يكون ولا قوة إلا بالله ألا ترى أن الأحوال والأسباب مع قيامها بفعل [تحصل] على بقائها بوصف تقدمها وبحال إبقاء القدرة، فمثل ذلك وصف التقدم. والله أعلم. وفي المسألة سوى ما قدمنا ذكره من الأدلة أنه لو كان العجز المتقدم يمنع الفعل لوقت القدرة ليجب أن تكون القدرة المتقدمة توجب الفعل لوقت العجز، وفي إحالة ذلك إحالة الأول. ولو كان الفعل يقع لفقد القدرة لكان كلما دام دام الفعل؛ إذ أسباب الأشياء لها، كلما دامت أوجبت دوامها، وفي ذلك لزوم القول بالوجود معها.
Like motion with God's creation of the body, not motion of necessity nor choice; and the type of motion of necessity may be with incapacity, and likewise choice with power, such that if power were such that action does not accompany it, then action being by it would be invalid; rather by its absence action occurs, and there is no power except with God. Do you not see that states and causes, with their being in action, [occur] upon their remaining with the description of their precedence and with the state of preserving power, so like that is the description of precedence. And God knows best. And in the matter, besides what we have mentioned of proofs, is that if prior incapacity prevented action at the time of power, then prior power would necessitate action at the time of incapacity, and the impossibility of that is the impossibility of the first. And if action occurred due to lack of power, then whenever it [lack] continues, action would continue; since the causes of things are for them, whenever they continue they necessitate their continuation, and in that is the necessary claim of existence with them.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that action occurs with power, not without it. Maturidi begins by drawing an analogy with motion: motion can be of necessity (like involuntary movement) or choice (voluntary), and each is associated with either incapacity or power. He argues that if power were such that action never accompanies it, then action would not be caused by power, but rather by its absence. He then introduces a conditional reductio argument: if prior incapacity prevented action at the time of power, then by the same logic prior power would necessitate action at the time of incapacity. Both are impossible. Furthermore, if action occurred only when power is absent, then as long as power is absent action would continue indefinitely, which is absurd because causes necessitate their effects only while they exist. The conclusion is that action must exist together with power.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
فعل (fi'l)
عجز ('ajz)
حركة ضرورة (daruriyya)
حركة اختيار (ikhtiyariyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think power and action are sequential, so power comes first and then action occurs later.
Correction: The analogy shows that action occurs together with power, not sequentially.

Checkpoint
What does the motion analogy primarily illustrate?
A) Necessary motion occurs with incapacity and voluntary motion with power.
B) All motion is created by God.
C) Power and action are independent.
D) Action occurs only when power is absent.

Key Takeaway
The motion analogy demonstrates that action occurs together with power, not by its absence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you ever feel that your own power is the primary cause, forgetting that power and action are simultaneous gifts from God? How does this lesson challenge that feeling? What will you do this week to remind yourself that every action is accompanied by God's power?
?
The analogy shows that voluntary action only occurs with power. Is there an area of your life where you are acting as if your own power precedes your actions, rather than acknowledging God's constant accompaniment? Write down one concrete step to shift your reliance on God's power today.
?
If action were by the absence of power, that would mean you act when God's power is withdrawn. How does this highlight the danger of attributing any action to your own independent ability? What practical change in your daily mindset does this require?
