Reductio Arguments: Action Cannot Occur Without Power
Maturidi uses two reductio arguments to prove action occurs with power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's two reductio arguments that refute the idea that action occurs only when power is absent.

Core Idea
If prior incapacity prevented action, then prior power would necessitate action—both impossible.

Maturidi continues his proof by considering two hypothetical scenarios. First, he argues that if prior incapacity prevented action at the time when power exists, then by symmetry, prior power would necessitate action at the time of incapacity. Both are absurd. Second, he shows that if action occurred only because of absence of power, then it would be perpetual, which is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
This passage contains two distinct arguments.
2
Follow the first reductio
Assume prior incapacity prevents action when power exists. Then by symmetry, prior power would necessitate action during incapacity. Both are impossible.
This refutes the idea that incapacity can block action.
3
Analyze the second argument
If action occurs due to lack of power, then as long as power is absent action would continue. But causes only necessitate while they exist, so this is absurd.
This shows action cannot be caused by absence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كالحركة مع خلق الله الجسم، لا حركة ضرورة ولا اختيار؛ ونوع حركة الضرورة قد يكون مع العجز، ومثله الاختيار مع القدرة على أن القدرة لو كانت بحيث لا يجامعها الفعل ليبطل أن يكون بها الفعل بل بعدمها يكون ولا قوة إلا بالله ألا ترى أن الأحوال والأسباب مع قيامها بفعل [تحصل] على بقائها بوصف تقدمها وبحال إبقاء القدرة، فمثل ذلك وصف التقدم. والله أعلم. وفي المسألة سوى ما قدمنا ذكره من الأدلة أنه لو كان العجز المتقدم يمنع الفعل لوقت القدرة ليجب أن تكون القدرة المتقدمة توجب الفعل لوقت العجز، وفي إحالة ذلك إحالة الأول. ولو كان الفعل يقع لفقد القدرة لكان كلما دام دام الفعل؛ إذ أسباب الأشياء لها، كلما دامت أوجبت دوامها، وفي ذلك لزوم القول بالوجود معها.
Like motion with God's creation of the body, not motion of necessity nor choice; and the type of motion of necessity may be with incapacity, and likewise choice with power, such that if power were such that action does not accompany it, then action being by it would be invalid; rather by its absence action occurs, and there is no power except with God. Do you not see that states and causes, with their being in action, [occur] upon their remaining with the description of their precedence and with the state of preserving power, so like that is the description of precedence. And God knows best. Commentary
This passage presents a proof that action occurs with power, not without it. Maturidi begins by drawing an analogy with motion: motion can be of necessity (like involuntary movement) or choice (voluntary), and each is associated with either incapacity or power. He argues that if power were such that action never accompanies it, then action would not be caused by power, but rather by its absence. He then introduces a conditional reductio argument: if prior incapacity prevented action at the time of power, then by the same logic prior power would necessitate action at the time of incapacity. Both are impossible. Misconception: Some think that incapacity can prevent action without any logical consequence.
Correction: The first reductio shows that such a claim leads to symmetrical impossibility.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does Maturidi draw from the second reductio?
A) Action occurs only when power is present.
B) Action would continue indefinitely if it were due to lack of power.
C) Power and action are independent.
D) Causes do not necessitate their effects.

Key Takeaway
The reductio arguments prove that action cannot occur due to lack of power and must occur with power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on a sin you committed, do you find yourself thinking it was because you 'lacked the power to resist'? How does this lesson challenge that excuse and hold you accountable for the moment you actually had power?
?
This argument proves that your actions only occur when Allah gives you the ability in that exact moment. How does this truth affect your reliance on Him for even the smallest good deed? What would change in your day if you truly lived as if every action is only possible by His concurrent power?
?
The second reductio shows that absence cannot be a cause—it is a void. Are there areas in your life where you have assumed that God's absence or silence causes something? How must you adjust your understanding of causality in your relationship with Allah?

Rebuttal of the Well Analogy Against Simultaneous Power and Faith
Maturidi rebuts the well analogy against simultaneous power and faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the well analogy by appealing to the common simultaneity of causes and effects and by exposing the opponent's inconsistency.

Core Idea
Simultaneity of cause and effect is common in religion and worldly affairs.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi confronts an objector who uses a clever analogy—a person stuck in a well needing a rope to get out, but the rope only comes when he gets out—to argue that faith and power cannot coexist simultaneously. Maturidi systematically dismantles this analogy by pointing out that many things occur together with their causes without temporal priority. He then highlights the opponent's own self-contradiction in another example, showing the weakness of their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objector's analogy is presented and how Maturidi begins his response.
The passage includes two main parts: the rebuttal of the well analogy and the critique of the opponent's contradictory example.
2
Understand the well analogy
Analyze the analogy: a person in a well cannot get out until a rope comes, but the rope comes only when he gets out. The objector uses this to claim that faith and power cannot coexist because each requires the other first.
3
Identify Maturidi's first argument
Look for Maturidi's statement that many things occur with causes that neither precede nor lag behind. He asserts that most religious and worldly affairs involve two things existing together without priority.
This is the core of his rebuttal.
4
Spot the self-contradiction
Notice that Maturidi points out the opponent himself accepts the example of throwing (where the throw and the object's motion are simultaneous). The opponent then gives a strained definition of throwing to avoid the contradiction.
This shows the weakness of the opponent's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: «إن لا يؤمن حتى يقدر ولا يقدر حتى يؤمن، فهو يبقى أبدا غير مؤمن، كالواقع في البئر إذا كان لا يخرج حتى يأتيه الحبل، ولا يأتيه حتى يخرج». فجواب هذا قد تضمنه ما ذكرت من الأشياء التي تقع مع أسباب لها لا تتقدم ولا تتأخر. ثم لم يقل ذلك للعلم بأنها تقع إذا لم يغفل عنه ولا يعرض فمثله الذي ذكرت. والأصل [في] الذي زعم، إنما يعظم وجوده إذا جعل كل واحد منهما يوجد بوجود الآخر متقدما. فأما وجود ذلك معا فعليه أكثر أمر الدين والدنيا من وجود شيئين معا لا يجوز تقدم أحدهما على الآخر. ثم عارض نفسه بالإلقاء على ما سبق وصفه؛ وتكلف إجابته بما لو رزق الحياء ما سمحت له نفسه بالتفوه به، فقال: «إن إلقاء الشيء هو خروجه من يده لا غيره، والاستطاعة...
He said: 'If he does not believe until he has power, and he does not have power until he believes, then he remains forever a non-believer, like the one who falls into a well: if he does not get out until a rope comes to him, and the rope does not come until he gets out.' The answer to this is already contained in what I have mentioned about things that occur with causes that neither precede nor lag behind. Then he [the objector] did not say that due to knowing that they occur if he is not heedless of him and nothing occurs, so likewise what I mentioned. The principle of what he claimed only becomes great if he makes each one of them exist by the existence of the other in a prior manner. As for their existence together, upon that rests most of the affairs of religion and this world, of two things existing together where priority of one over the other is not permissible. Then he himself contradicted [his own principle] with the example of throwing, as previously described; and he strained to answer with something such that, if he had been granted modesty, his soul would not have permitted him to utter it, so he said: 'The throwing of a thing is its leaving his hand, nothing else, and capability...'
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage refutes an analogy: the opponent claims that if faith requires power and power requires faith, then neither can occur first, analogous to a person in a well needing a rope to get out, but the rope only comes when he gets out. Maturidi answers that this reasoning fails because many things occur simultaneously with their causes without temporal priority—this is common in religion and worldly affairs. The opponent's analogy falsely assumes that one must precede the other. Moreover, the opponent contradicts his own principle by accepting the example of throwing (where the throw and the object's motion are simultaneous) while denying the same for power and faith. The opponent then gives a strained definition of throwing (as merely the release from the hand), which Maturidi criticizes as shameless.

Key Terms
إيمان (iman)
قدرة (qudra)
سبب (sabab)
تقديم (taqdim)
تأخير (ta'khir)
إلقاء (ilqa')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The well analogy is a valid argument that faith and power cannot coexist because each requires the other first.
Correction: Maturidi shows that simultaneity of cause and effect is common, and the opponent's own example of throwing disproves his claim.

Checkpoint
What mainstream principle does Maturidi appeal to in refuting the well analogy?
A) All causes must precede their effects.
B) Two things can exist simultaneously without priority.
C) Faith is prior to power by necessity.
D) The well metaphor is invalid because wells have no rope.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the well analogy by showing that simultaneity of cause and effect is common and that the opponent contradicts himself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever delayed a step of faith because you felt you lacked the power, only to realize that faith itself gives power? What specific area of your life is waiting for a rope you are not reaching for?
?
The opponent used a strained definition to avoid admitting a contradiction. In what area of your faith do you use mental gymnastics to avoid facing a contradiction between what you believe and how you live?
?
If most of religion and world affairs involve simultaneous causes and effects, what does that say about your habit of waiting for the 'right time' to act? What would happen if you treated faith and action as one simultaneous event starting today?

The Well Analogy: Faith and Power Coexisting
Maturidi refutes the circular dependency objection using simultaneous causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the circular dependency argument about faith and power by demonstrating that simultaneous existence is common in religion and worldly affairs.

Core Idea
Faith and power can coexist without temporal priority.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses an objection that claims faith and power are mutually dependent in a circular way, illustrated by the analogy of a person in a well. He responds by pointing out that many things in religion and worldly affairs occur simultaneously with their causes. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: objection first, then response.
The analogy of the well is a vivid illustration of circular dependency.
2
Identify the objection and analogy
The opponent argues that faith cannot occur without power and power cannot occur without faith, leading to an infinite regress. The well analogy captures this dilemma.
Consider why this objection seems compelling at first glance.
3
Understand Maturidi's response
Maturidi refutes by noting that many events happen simultaneously with their causes; temporal precedence is not always required. The objection only holds if one insists on strict sequence, but coexistence is the norm.
Think of examples in daily life where two things occur together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: «إنْ لا يؤمن حتى يقدر ولا يقدر حتى يؤمن، فهو يبقى أبدا غير مؤمن، كالواقع في البئر إذا كان لا يخرج حتى يأتيه الحبل، ولا يأتيه حتى يخرج». فجواب هذا قد تضمنه ما ذكرت من الأشياء التي تقع مع أسباب لها لا تتقدم ولا تتأخر. ثم لم يقل ذلك للعلم بأنها تقع إذا لم يغفل عنه ولا يعرض فمثله الذي ذكرت . والأصل في الذي زعم ، إنما يعظم وجوده إذا جعل كل واحد منهما يوجد بوجود الآخر متقدما. فأما وجود ذلك معا فعليه أكثر أمر الدين والدنيا من وجود شيئين معا لا يجوز تقدم أحدهما على الآخر.
He said: 'He does not believe until he is able and he is not able until he believes, so he remains forever a non-believer, like the one who falls into a well if he does not come out until the rope comes to him, and it does not come to him until he comes out.' The answer to this is contained in what I mentioned of things that occur with causes that neither precede nor are delayed. Then he did not say that out of knowledge that they occur if he is not heedless of it and no obstacle occurs, so the like of that which I mentioned. And the principle of what he claimed is that its existence becomes great only if he makes each one exist by the existence of the other preceded. As for their existence together, most of the matter of religion and worldly life is upon that: two things existing together where neither can precede the other.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from al-Ka'bi using the analogy of a person in a well who cannot get out until the rope comes, and the rope does not come until he gets out—implying a circular dependency between faith and power. Maturidi responds that this is not a valid objection because many things occur simultaneously with their causes without temporal precedence. He argues that the opponent's assumption only has force if he insists that each must exist only through the prior existence of the other, but in reality, many aspects of religion and worldly affairs involve two things existing together without one preceding the other. Therefore the analogy fails.

Key Terms
الإيمان (al-iman)
القدرة (al-qudra)
الأسباب (al-asbab)
التقدم (al-taqaddum)
المعا (al-ma'an)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that faith and power must be sequential, with one necessarily preceding the other.
Correction: Maturidi shows that simultaneous existence is common; faith and power can coexist without temporal priority.

Checkpoint
What is the core point of Maturidi's response to the well analogy?
A) Faith and power are the same thing
B) Many things exist together without temporal sequence
C) The rope and the person are both created by God
D) The analogy is false because wells do not exist

Key Takeaway
Faith and power can coexist without temporal precedence; this is common in religion and worldly affairs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you assume that faith must come first before action, or action before faith? Where might you be missing the grace of their coexistence?
?
Think of a spiritual struggle you face—like patience in hardship or hope despite failure. Does this lesson's insight about simultaneous causation free you from waiting for a perfect sequence? What would it look like to act now, trusting that power and belief arise together?
?
The passage says that many things in religion and worldly affairs exist together without temporal order. Are there relationships or duties you've postponed because you thought you needed to be 'ready' first? How can you embrace the moment and let faith and action arise together?

The Throwing Analogy: Defining Action and Capacity
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's redefinition of throwing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of al-Ka'bi's claim that throwing is merely the exit of an object, and how this argument defends the view that all actions depend on God's power.

Core Idea
Throwing is the exit itself—no separate action, all power belongs to God.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi engages with a counterargument from al-Ka'bi concerning the definition of 'throwing' (al-ilqa'). Al-Ka'bi attempted to distinguish between the act of throwing and the resulting movement of the object, but Maturidi exposes the flaw in this distinction with a sharp intuitive argument. By reading this lesson, you will grasp the theological stakes behind a simple physical analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the words of Imam Maturidi and al-Ka'bi's claim register before analysis.
2
Identify al-Ka'bi's claim
Focus on the sentence: 'The throwing of a thing is its exit from his hand, nothing else, and capacity is other than the action.' This is al-Ka'bi's attempt to separate action from effect.
3
Understand Maturidi's refutation
Notice Maturidi's response: he says throwing is the exit by intuition (badiha). If they are identical, then there is no distinct action, and the exit can occur without human agency, proving all power is from God.
This refutation uses a basic logical reduction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بالإلقاء على ما سبق وصفه؛ وتكلف إجابته بما لو رزق الحياء ما سمحت له نفسه بالتفوه به، فقال: «إن إلقاء الشيء هو خروجه من يده لا غيره، والاستطاعة غير الفعل. فمن نظر إليه يعرف كذبه ، فإن الإلقاء هو الخروج - لا غير ـ بالبديهة بلا تأمل. وإذا لم يكن غير، فإذا ليس ثمة إلا الخروج فما لزمه الفعل ؛ ويجوز كون الخروج ولا صنع معه له ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then he opposed himself by the throwing as previously described; and he burdened his answer with that which, if he had been granted modesty, his soul would not have allowed him to utter. He said: 'The throwing of a thing is its exit from his hand, nothing else, and capacity (al-istita'a) is other than the action.' Whoever looks at him knows his lie, for throwing is the exit—no different—by intuition without reflection. And if it is nothing else, then there is nothing other than the exit, so what obligated the action? And it is possible that the exit occurs while he has no action with it, and there is no power except by God.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ka'bi's attempt to defend his position by redefining 'throwing' (al-ilqa') as merely the exit of the object from the hand, separate from the action itself, and claiming that capacity (al-istita'a) is distinct from the action. Maturidi refutes this by stating that it is intuitively obvious (bil-badiha) that throwing is the exit—there is no difference. Therefore, if throwing is nothing but the exit, then there is nothing else, so what is the action? He argues that the exit can occur without any action (sina') from the person, and thus there is no power except by God. The point is that al-Ka'bi's distinction collapses because the exit is the very action, not something additional.

Key Terms
الإلقاء (al-ilqa')
الخروج (al-khuruj)
الاستطاعة (al-istita'a)
الفعل (al-fi'l)
الصنع (al-sina')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'throwing' involves a distinct mental or physical action separate from the resulting motion of the object, and that human capacity is independently effective.
Correction: Maturidi argues that throwing is identical to the exit—there is no separate action. Therefore, all actions are ultimately dependent on God's power, not independent human capacity.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi claim about the definition of throwing (al-ilqa')?
A) It is the intention to throw
B) It is the exit of the object, nothing else
C) It requires a separate power
D) It is the same as capacity

Key Takeaway
Throwing is identical to the exit; there is no separate human action, and all power belongs to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you last threw something—a ball, a piece of trash—did you feel that you were the one doing it? How does Maturidi's claim that all power belongs to God challenge that feeling? What would it mean for you to genuinely believe that your hand's motion is only an exit with no separate action from you?
?
You rely on your 'capacity' (istita'a) every day to get things done. But Maturidi implies that capacity is not really yours. Can you pinpoint a moment today where you acted as if your ability was independent? How can you train yourself to attribute all your effectiveness to God without falling into laziness?
?
If throwing is just the exit and you have no true action, then what is left of your personal responsibility? Does this doctrine absolve you of sin? If not, what is the basis for accountability? Search your heart: are you using God's power as an excuse to avoid effort or as a reason to be grateful?

The 'I Cannot' Objection: Activity vs. Power
Maturidi interprets 'I cannot' as lack of activity, not lack of power, giving two answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi interprets the phrase 'I cannot' as a lack of activity rather than a lack of power, and the twofold answer he provides to the objection.

Core Idea
Saying 'I cannot' can be truthful even if one possesses power.

This passage addresses a common objection regarding human action and divine power. The opponent, al-Ka'bi, claims that when a person says 'I cannot' despite having no apparent disability, they are denying activity, not power. The interlocutor counters that the person has helped others before, so why can't they help now? Maturidi offers two insightful answers that clarify the relationship between power and activity, showing that power is not a permanent attribute but is granted by God when action is undertaken.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: objection, counter, two answers.
2
Understand the objection
Al-Ka'bi claims that when someone says 'I cannot', they do not deny power (qudra) but deny activity (nashāt). Grasp this distinction.
3
Analyse Maturidi's first answer
Both the person and the interlocutor are truthful because lack of activity removes power. Power is conditioned by the presence of activity.
This shows that power is not a permanent attribute.
4
Study the second answer
The person spoke according to custom: if they undertake the action, capacity would come. The previous power has passed away.
Maturidi uses the interlocutor's own example as proof.
5
Reflect on implications
Consider how this view affects our understanding of human responsibility and divine omnipotence. Discuss the nuance of 'I cannot'.
This connects to broader themes in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج لخصمه بقول للمساءل القيام بحاجته: «لا أستطيع»، وهو ممن لا علة به. فزعم أنه لا يريد به نفي القوة، إنما يريد نفي النشاط ; دليل ذلك ما يَعُود عليه السائل بالقول فيقول: «بل تستطيع، لكنك لا تنبسط بمعونتي ، وقد قمت بحوائج فلان، فكيف تقول : لا أستطيع ؟» قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : له جوابان أحدهما أنهما جميعا صدقا، إذ عدم النشاط يرفع القوة، وأمكن القيام بذلك والنشاط من ذلك، فيوجد بالقوة ، وهو ما يقول. وهما أمران معروفان، لذلك لا يجوز إلحاق الكذب بواحد منهما، وعلى ما يقوله عنده إلحاق. والثاني أنه قال على الأمر المعتاد: إنه لو قام به لأتته القدرة». ألا ترى أنه احتج بالقيام بحوائج غيره، ومعلوم أن تلك القدرة قد زالت عنه . والله الموفق.
Then he argued for his opponent by the saying of the one asked to fulfill his need: 'I cannot', while he is one with no ailment. So he claimed that he does not intend by it the negation of power, rather he intends the negation of activity; the proof of that is what the questioner returns to him by saying: 'Rather you can, but you do not spread out with my assistance, and you have fulfilled the needs of so-and-so, so how do you say: I cannot?' Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: He has two answers. One of them is that both are truthful, for lack of activity removes power, and it was possible to fulfill that while activity comes from that, so it exists by power, and that is what he says. And they are two known matters, therefore it is not permissible to impute falsehood to either of them, and according to what he says there is imputation. The second is that he spoke according to the customary matter: that if he were to undertake it, capacity would come to him. Do you not see that he argued by the fulfillment of the needs of others, and it is known that that power had left him? And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection where a person requested to fulfill a need says 'I cannot' despite having no visible ailment. Al-Ka'bi claims this does not mean denial of power (qudra) but denial of activity (nashāt). The interlocutor replies that the person could do it, as they have done similar tasks for others. Maturidi (Abu Mansur) gives two answers: (1) Both statements are true because lack of activity removes power (i.e., power is conditioned by activity), and activity is necessary for power to be effective; thus the person genuinely cannot because the activity is absent. (2) The person spoke according to normal custom: if he were to undertake it, ability would come to him, just as his previous ability to help others has now left him. Maturidi points out that the interlocutor's own argument (that he helped others) shows that that specific power has now passed away. The point is that power is not a permanent entity; it is given by God and can be removed.

Key Terms
الاستطاعة (al-istita'a)
النشاط (al-nashāt)
القوة (al-quwwa)
القدرة (al-qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that saying 'I cannot' always denotes a complete lack of capability.
Correction: Maturidi shows it can mean a lack of vigor or that power is temporarily removed.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's first answer, why does the person truthfully say 'I cannot'?
A) He is lying to avoid work
B) Lack of activity removes power
C) He has a hidden ailment
D) Power is eternal and never leaves

Key Takeaway
Maturidi defends the truthfulness of 'I cannot' by arguing that power depends on activity and is not inherently permanent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I cannot' to a difficult task, do you truly mean you lack the ability, or are you masking laziness or fear? How can this lesson's distinction change your approach to responsibilities?
?
This lesson shows that power is not a fixed possession but a gift given when you act. Are there areas in your life where you have waited for power to come before starting, rather than starting and trusting power will come from God?
?
The interlocutor points out that the person 'cannot' now but did similar tasks before. Reflect on a time you convinced yourself you couldn't do something you have done before. What does this reveal about your reliance on your own perceived ability versus God's provision?

The Act is Identical to the Movement
Maturidi refutes the claim that the act is separate from the movement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the act of throwing is nothing other than the physical movement itself, and how this refutes the claim of a separate act.

Core Idea
The act is the movement, not a separate entity.

Maturidi directly confronts a claim that the act is distinct from the movement. He argues by appealing to intuitive knowledge (badīha) and logical necessity. This passage establishes a key principle in kalam about the nature of human acts and their dependence on divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the brevity and logical structure of Maturidi's argument.
The passage uses the word 'al-khurūj' (exit) for the movement, and 'al-ilqā' (throwing) as the act under discussion.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
What is the opponent asserting? The passage begins with 'Other than the act' referring to someone who says the act is other than the movement. Recognize this as the thesis Maturidi refutes.
3
Understand Maturidi's intuitive refutation
Maturidi claims that anyone who looks at the act of throwing knows intuitively that it is exactly the exit, with no extra element. This is a direct appeal to self-evident knowledge.
He says 'by intuition without reflection.' This is a key epistemological move in kalam.
4
Analyze the conditional argument
Maturidi then uses a conditional: if there is no separate entity, then there is only the exit, so the act must be nothing but the exit. This reinforces the identity thesis logically.
The phrase 'فما لزمه الفعل' can be interpreted as 'the act is necessary' i.e., necessarily tied to the exit.
5
Appreciate the concluding affirmation of divine power
The passage ends with a reminder that the exit can occur without any independent action by the agent, and that all power comes from Allah. This ties the discussion to tawhid.
'لا قوة إلا بالله' emphasizes that no act is independent of Allah's power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير الفعل. فمن نظر إليه يعرف كذبه، فإن الإلقاء هو الخروج - لا غير - بالبديهة بلا تأمل. وإذا لم يكن غير، فإذا ليس ثمة إلا الخروج فما لزمه الفعل؛ ويجوز كون الخروج ولا صنع معه له ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Other than the act. So whoever looks at it knows his lie, for throwing is the exit – nothing else – by intuition without reflection. And if there is no other, then there is nothing except the exit, so the act is necessary; and it is possible that the exit occurs with no action with him, and no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a brief logical refutation of an opponent who claims that the act (فعل) is something separate from the physical movement (الخروج). Maturidi argues that this claim is false, because the act of throwing (الإلقاء) is intuitively known to be nothing but the exit itself. He then presents a conditional: if there is no separate entity, then there is only the exit, so the act must be identical to the exit. Finally, he notes that it is possible for the exit to occur without any additional action on the agent's part — the exit is the act. The proof concludes with the formula 'لا قوة إلا بالله' (There is no power except with Allah), emphasizing dependence on divine power.

الخروج
الإلقاء
قدرة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The act of throwing is a separate metaphysical entity distinct from the physical movement.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the act of throwing is nothing other than the physical movement itself; there is no separate entity.

Key Takeaway
The act of throwing is nothing other than the physical movement of exiting, and such movement depends solely on Allah's power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How can you use this lesson to take full responsibility for your actions as one single reality?

What would it look like to start each day affirming that every movement, every act, is only by Allah's power? Will you commit to a specific practice this week to remind yourself of this truth?

Maturidi's Two Responses to the Claim of 'I Cannot'
Maturidi gives two responses to the opponent's interpretation of 'I cannot'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's two rational responses to the opponent's interpretation of the phrase 'I cannot' as meaning lack of activity rather than lack of power.

Core Idea
'I cannot' can be truthful even when one has power, due to lack of activity or habitual state.

In this passage, Maturidi engages with a nuanced theological-linguistic argument about the phrase 'I cannot'. An opponent claims that when a capable person says 'I cannot', they mean lack of willingness, not inability. Maturidi gives two distinct responses defending the truthfulness of the statement in both senses. This lesson unpacks these responses and their implications for understanding human capacity and divine power.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
Read the passage to grasp the opponent's argument: 'I cannot' negates activity (نشاط), not power (قوة). They cite how a bystander would reply to the speaker.
The opponent uses a common conversational rebuttal as evidence.
2
First response: both truthful
Maturidi argues that both the speaker and the interlocutor can be truthful. Lack of activity effectively removes power in practice, even if power exists potentially.
This preserves truthfulness on both sides without contradiction.
3
Second response: habitual state
The speaker may be referring to his usual state: power would come if he acted, but it is not present at the moment.
This accounts for cases where capability is not actualized.
4
See the opponent's argument turned
Maturidi points out that the opponent's own example (fulfilling others' needs) shows that power can leave, undermining their position.
This is a dialectical move strengthening Maturidi's case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج لخصمه بقول المساءل القيام بحاجته: «لا أستطيع»، وهو ممن لا علة به. فزعم أنه لا يريد به نفي القوة، إنما يريد نفي النشاط؛ دليل ذلك ما يَعُود عليه السائل بالقول فيقول: «بل تستطيع، لكنك لا تنبسط بمعونتي، وقد قمت بحوائج فلان، فكيف تقول: لا أستطيع؟» قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: له جوابان: أحدهما أنهما جميعا صدقا، إذ عدم النشاط يرفع القوة، وأمكن القيام بذلك والنشاط من ذلك، فيوجد بالقوة، وهو ما يقول. وهما أمران معروفان، لذلك لا يجوز إلحاق الكذب بواحد منهما، وعلى ما يقوله عنده إلحاق. والثاني أنه قال على الأمر المعتاد: إنه لو قام به لأتته القدرة. ألا ترى أنه احتج بالقيام بحوائج غيره، ومعلوم أن تلك القدرة قد زالت عنه. والله الموفق.
Then he argued for his opponent with the statement of the questioner about fulfilling his need: 'I cannot,' while he was someone with no ailment. So he claimed that he did not intend by it the negation of power, but rather the negation of activity. The evidence of that is what the questioner replies to him by saying: 'Rather, you are able, but you are not active with my assistance, and you have fulfilled the needs of so-and-so, so how can you say: I cannot?' Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: He has two answers: One, that both are truthful, since lack of activity removes power, and it is possible to do that and activity is from that, so it is found with power, which is what he says. And both are known matters, therefore it is not permissible to attribute lying to either of them, and according to his statement, attribution is required. The second is that he spoke according to the usual state: that if he had done it, power would have come to him. Do you not see that he argued by the fulfillment of the needs of others, and it is known that that power has left him? And Allah gives success.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument about the meaning of the statement 'I cannot' when uttered by someone who is physically capable. The opponent (likely a Mu'tazili) claims that such a person means lack of willingness (نشاط) not lack of power (قوة) – the evidence being that a bystander would reply 'You are able but not active.' Maturidi provides two responses. First, both statements can be true: lack of activity effectively removes the power in practice, though the power exists potentially. Both parties (the one saying 'I cannot' and the one saying 'You are able') speak truthfully because they refer to different aspects – thus accusing either of lying is unjustified. Second, the person may be speaking based on his usual state: if he had undertaken the task, the power would come to him, but it is not present at the moment. Maturidi then notes that the opponent's own argument (citing examples of fulfilling others' needs) actually undermines his position, because in those other cases the power had already left.

Key Terms

نشاط
قدرة
صدق
كذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: When someone says 'I cannot' while capable, they are lying or denying their God-given power.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'I cannot' can be truthful either because lack of activity effectively removes power in practice, or because power is not present in that moment but would come with action.

Checkpoint

A) Negation of all ability
B) Negation of activity (نشاط)
C) Physical incapacity
D) Lack of desire

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's two responses show that the statement 'I cannot' can be truthful from a capable person due to lack of activity or habitual absence of power, preserving truthfulness in human speech about capacity.

Think of a specific time you said 'I cannot' about a task. Was it truly a lack of ability, or a lack of activity? In light of this lesson, what would it mean to be honest with yourself and with Allah about your capacity? What one action can you take today to move from 'I cannot' to 'I will try, trusting Allah for power'?

Maturidi's second answer suggests that power comes when you undertake the action. Where in your life are you waiting for confidence or ability before starting something good? What is the first step you can take right now, even if you feel powerless, believing that Allah will grant you the power?

Refutation of Temporary Emptiness of Action
Imam Maturidi refutes an opponent's claim about temporary emptiness of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi rejects the idea that a being can be temporarily devoid of action, and how he exposes the logical inconsistency in the opponent's argument.

Core Idea
Logical consistency demands that if an action can be absent at one moment, it can be absent at any moment.

Imam Maturidi confronts an intriguing claim: that a being can be without its action at the moment of existence, but never thereafter. This short but powerful refutation exposes the arbitrariness of such a distinction. In this lesson, we will read the original Arabic, see the translation, and understand the Imam's reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the opponent's claim and the Imam's immediate rejection.
2
Identify the logical flaw
The opponent says emptiness is permissible at the time of existence but never after. Ask yourself: what is the difference between those moments? The Imam points out there is none.
This is the core of the refutation.
3
Reflect on the Imam's dismissal
After the refutation, the opponent tries to defend his view as rational. The Imam dismisses this as driven by confusion. Consider why he chooses not to engage further.
The Imam prioritises beneficial discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في الصحيح السليم: إنه يجوز أن يخلو عن الفعل وقت كونه، ثم لم يجز أبدا. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وما يقوله خطأ، بل يجوز ذلك. ثم زعم أن ذلك معقول، وهو عقل من حق العقل [عنده] الخروج عما خاله عقلاً. ثم تكلم في العلم بما لم أظن أحدا تأمله إلا عرف أن الحيرة دفعته إليه، فتركته لقلّة نفعه.
And he said in 'al-Sahih al-Salim': It is permissible that it be empty of action at the time of its existence, then it is never permissible after that. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: What he says is wrong; rather, that is permissible. Then he claimed that that is rational, and it is reason that according to him it is the right of reason to depart from what it considered rational. Then he spoke about knowledge in a way that I do not think anyone would contemplate it except that he would know that confusion drove him to it, so I left it due to its lack of benefit.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage refutes a specific argument from an opponent (likely al-Ka'bi or another Mu'tazilite). The opponent claims that it is possible for a being to be empty of action at the moment it comes into existence, but impossible thereafter. The Shaykh (Imam Maturidi) declares this statement wrong, asserting that if it is permissible at one moment, it should be permissible at all moments. The opponent then tries to justify his view as rational, but the Imam dismisses this as a departure from true reason, motivated by confusion. The Imam decides not to engage further with this argument because it is not beneficial.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A being can be empty of action only at the first moment of existence, but must always have action thereafter.
Correction: If being empty of action is possible at one moment, it is possible at any moment, because the nature of the being does not change.

D) Because knowledge is too complex.

Key Takeaway
Logical consistency compels us to accept that if a being can be without action at any moment, it can be without action at every moment; otherwise, the distinction is arbitrary.

The Three Causes of Action and True Will
Maturidi classifies causes of action and defines true will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how actions are classified in the witnessed world and how true will differs from wish and compulsion.

Core Idea
Actions are either by will, compulsion, or forgetfulness; true will always produces its act.

Maturidi begins by establishing a fundamental principle for understanding human action: every act in the witnessed world falls under one of three categories. This classification helps us understand the nature of divine will and its difference from mere wish. The passage also contrasts the Sunni position on the timing of will with that of the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Let the author's classification come through.
Notice the three categories at the start.
2
Identify the three causes
Find the three causes of action: will, compulsion, forgetfulness. Understand that if an action is not under compulsion or forgetfulness, it is attributed to will.
This is the basic taxonomy.
3
Contrast true will with wish
The passage defines true will as that by which the action necessarily occurs. In contrast, wish (tamanni) may or may not result in the action. Note also the Sunni-Mu'tazila difference on when will occurs relative to the act.
This distinction is key to understanding divine will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل الذي يقع عليه الفعل في الشاهد أن يكون على إرادة أو غلبة أو سهو فكل من خرج في شيء عن الوصف بالغلبة فيه والسهو لزم الوصف بالإرادة التي هي لا بها في الحقيقة فهي أقسام قد بينا ذلك فيما تقدم والله الموفق. على أن القول في الشاهد فيما في الحقيقة إرادة فهي التي يكون بها الفعل لا محالة فعندنا تكون معه وعند المعتزلة قبل الفعل بلا فصل وما عدا ذلك مما قد يكون الفعل إذا وجدت ولا يكون فهي التمني المعروف والله يجل عن هذا الوصف ثبت أن إرادته على الوجه الأول وأنه يتحقق الفعل على الوجه الذي أراد به ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then the principle upon which an action occurs in the witnessed world is that it is either by will, or compulsion, or forgetfulness. So whoever is exempt from description of compulsion and forgetfulness in something, the description of will is necessary for him, which is that [action] is not by it [will] in reality? So these are divisions; we have explained that previously. And God is the Grantor of success. As for the statement in the witnessed world concerning what is in reality will, it is that by which the action occurs necessarily. So with us [Ahl al-Sunna], it [will] is with the act; and with the Mu'tazila, it is before the act without interval. And other than that—what sometimes the action occurs when it [will] exists and sometimes it does not—that is the well-known wish (tamanni), and God is exalted above this description. It is established that His will is of the first type, and that the act is actualized in the manner He willed. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of the causes of human actions in the witnessed world (al-shahid). Actions are attributed to one of three things: will (irada), compulsion (ghalaba), or forgetfulness (sahu). If an action is not done under compulsion or forgetfulness, it must be attributed to will, which in reality is that thing 'by which' the action is done? The text has a slightly ambiguous phrase 'لا بها في الحقيقة' which might mean 'not by it in reality' but the context suggests it is a definition. Then Maturidi specifies what true will is: it is that by which the action occurs necessarily (la mahala). He distinguishes the Sunni position that will is simultaneous with the act from the Mu'tazilite position that will precedes the act without interval. Anything else—where the action may or may not occur when the will exists—is mere wish (tamanni), which is beneath God. Therefore, God's will is of the first type, and what He wills inevitably occurs. This affirms deterministic Sunni theology against Mu'tazilite views on the timing of will.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
غلبة (compulsion)
سهو (forgetfulness)
تمني (wish)
شاهد (witnessed world)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that wish (tamanni) is the same as true will.
Correction: True will necessitates the action, while wish may or may not result in the action.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between true will and wish?
A) True will is simultaneous with the act; wish may or may not produce the act.
B) True will is God's attribute; wish is human only.
C) True will causes compulsion; wish causes forgetfulness.
D) True will is before the act; wish is after.

Key Takeaway
God's will is true will: it always brings about what He wills, unlike human wish which may not result in action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to want something (like a stronger relationship with Allah or a good deed) but do not act on it, are you exercising true will or mere wish? How can you transform your wishes into true will today?
?
If you truly believe that God's will never fails to achieve its purpose, why do you still feel anxiety when your plans fall apart? What does that reveal about your trust in His will over your own?
?
In what area of your life have you been excusing inaction by saying 'I didn't have the will' when really you just had a wish? What step can you take right now to align your will with your faith?

Second Similarity: Mu'tazila Deny God Creates Accidents
Maturidi presents the second point of similarity: Mu'tazila deny God creates actions of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila parallel the Magians by attributing the creation of accidents to bodies rather than to God.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila divide causation: God creates substances, bodies create their own actions.

In this lesson, we examine the second point of comparison between the Mu'tazila and the Magians. Imam al-Maturidi highlights how the Mu'tazila, while affirming God as the creator of substances, deny that He creates the accidents and actions that arise from those substances. This effectively limits divine power and mirrors the dualist error of the Magians, who attribute good and evil to separate sources. The passage is unfortunately cut off, but its core argument is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the list of actions that the Mu'tazila exclude from God's creation: aggregation, separation, motion, rest, and all generated things.
The passage is incomplete; focus on the stated examples.
2
Identify the central claim
The Mu'tazila claim that the world is 'by God and by bodies.' Understand that this means God creates the substances (bodies), but the accidents (actions like motion) are created by the bodies themselves.
This is a form of dual agency.
3
Connect to the Magian comparison
Recall from earlier lessons that the Magians attribute good to God and evil to Iblis. Here, the Mu'tazila similarly split causation: God creates substances, creatures create their own acts. This undermines tawhid.
The parallel is between dualism in origins vs. dualism in agency.
4
Reflect on the theological issue
Ask yourself: If God does not create the actions of bodies, is He truly omnipotent? This passage shows the Mu'tazila limiting divine power, which contradicts orthodox Sunni belief.
This lesson is based on a fragment; the full argument may have included additional points.

Source Text

Then the Mu'tazila make the world [to exist] by God and by bodies, without that being from God: from aggregation and separation, motion and rest, and all generated things from what comes from creation, whether separated [؟] or
Source pages p. 403

This passage begins the second point of comparison between the Magians and the Mu'tazila. Maturidi states that the Mu'tazila attribute the world to God and to bodies, meaning they divide causation: God creates the substances (bodies), but the accidents and actions (aggregation, separation, motion, rest, and all generated effects) are not from God—they come from the creatures themselves. This parallels the Magian dualism where good comes from God and evil from Iblis. However, the passage is cut off, so the full argument is not available in this window. The incomplete sentence suggests Maturidi was explaining that Mu'tazila exclude accidents from God's creative act, thus limiting His power.

Maturidi's Doctrine of Kasb: The Middle Path
Maturidi states his position on human acts as real acquisition (kasb) while denying creation by humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi affirms real human acquisition (kasb) while maintaining that Allah alone creates actions.

Core Idea
Actions are truly ours by acquisition, but created only by Allah.

The question of human action divided early Islamic theologians. Some denied any real human role; others claimed humans create their own acts. Maturidi presents a precise middle path: we truly acquire our actions, but Allah creates them. This passage lays out that position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note the ellipsis in the Arabic—it may indicate a lacuna in the manuscript.
2
Identify the two opposing views
The passage begins with a disagreement: some say actions are only metaphorically for humans (really Allah's), while others (implied) claim humans create their own. Find the phrase 'منهم من جعلها لهم مجازا'.
The opposing views represent Jabriyya and Qadariyya/Mu'tazila.
3
Understand Maturidi's key phrase
Look at 'وعندنا لازم تحقيق الفعل لهم'. This is Maturidi's central claim: it is necessary to affirm the action truly for humans.
'تحقيق' means making real, as opposed to metaphorical.
4
See the reconciliation with divine creation
Maturidi then explains that this affirmation does not negate Allah's role: 'لله بأن خلقها... وللخلق على ما هي ما كسبوها'. Notice the dual attribution: Allah creates, humans acquire.
'كسب' (kasb) is the key term for acquisition.
5
Reflect on recompense
The passage ends with 'ونعادها' (they are recompensed). This shows that real acquisition is the basis for moral responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلف منتحلو الإسلام في أفعال الخلق ، فمنهم من جعلها لهم مجازا ، وحقيقتها الله ..... وعندنا لازم تحقيق الفعل لهم . وليس في الإضافة إلى الله سبحانه نفي ذلك ، لله بأن خلقها على ما هي عليه ، وأوجدها بعد أن لم تكن ، وللخلق على ما هي ما كسبوها ونعادها
The claimants of Islam differed concerning the actions of creation. Some of them made them metaphorical for the creatures, while their reality is from Allah... And with us, it is necessary to affirm the action for them. And there is no negation of that in the attribution to Allah, the Glorified, because He created them as they are, and brought them into existence after they were not, and for the creation, as they are, they have acquired them and are recompensed for them.
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's doctrine on human actions (af'al al-khalq). He notes the disagreement: some (the Jabriyya) say actions are only metaphorically ascribed to humans, really belonging to Allah; others (the Qadariyya/Mu'tazila) say humans create their own actions. Maturidi states his position: it is necessary to affirm a real connection of the action to the human (lihqiq al-fi'l lahum). But this does not negate Allah's attribution, because Allah is the creator who creates the actions as they are and brings them into existence. The human role is acquisition (kasb) – they acquire the action and are recompensed for it. This is the Maturidi middle path: real human acquisition while affirming Allah's sole creation.

Key Terms
أفعال الخلق
الكسب
الإحداث
المجاز
التحقيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi denies human freedom or reduces actions to mere metaphor.
Correction: Maturidi actually affirms real human acquisition (kasb) while denying human creation of actions. Actions are truly ours for moral accountability, but their existence is entirely from Allah.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the relationship between human actions and Allah?
A) Humans create their own actions independently.
B) Actions are only metaphorically attributed to humans.
C) Allah creates the actions and humans acquire them.
D) Humans have no role in their actions.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that human actions are truly acquired by us, but their existence is wholly from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you do a good deed, do you feel a sense of pride as if you created it yourself? How does knowing that Allah alone creates the action, and you only acquire it, humble that pride? Will you consciously remind yourself of this in your next action?
?
When you sin, do you ever think, 'It was Allah's will, so I am not fully responsible'? How does the doctrine of kasb force you to accept full responsibility for what you have acquired, without blaming Allah? What would it mean for your repentance if you truly believed you acquired that sin?
?
Are you willing to stand before Allah and say, 'I acquired this action' for both your good and bad deeds, acknowledging that the power to do so came from Him? If not, what does that reveal about your understanding of your own agency? How can you train yourself to live with this balance of humility and accountability?

Cause vs Normal Condition: Hearing Internal Speech
Maturidites distinguish cause and condition to reject analogy for hearing internal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidite distinction between a real cause (illa) and a normal condition (shart adi), and how it is used to reject the analogy between hearing internal speech and seeing God.

Core Idea
The difference between a real cause and a normal condition decides what is possible.

This passage addresses a crucial theological debate: Can God's internal speech (kalam nafsi) be heard? The Mu'tazilites argued that just as vision has conditions that make seeing God impossible, so too hearing internal speech is impossible. The Maturidites respond with a subtle distinction—between a real cause required for hearing (sound and letters) and mere normal conditions for vision. This distinction blocks the analogy and preserves the possibility of seeing God while denying hearing His speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the key terms 'illa' and 'shart adi'.
The passage is dense; pay attention to contrasts.
2
Identify the distinction
Find where the Maturidites distinguish between a real cause (illa) and a normal condition (shart adi). Understand why sound and letters are considered causes for hearing.
A cause is necessary and sufficient; a condition is merely customary.
3
Apply the distinction to the analogy
See how this distinction allows them to reject the analogy with vision. The conditions for vision are not real causes, but sound and letters are real causes for hearing.
Thus hearing internal speech (without sound) is impossible, but seeing God (without direction) is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الماتريدية الذين أحالوا سماع الكلام النفسي لاشتراط الصوت والحرف فقد فرقوا بين العلة والشرط العادي ، وبذلك يرفضون قياس السماع على الرؤية ؛ لأن ما يذكره المعتزلة من شروط للرؤية ليست إلا شروطاً عادية للرؤية وليست عللاً حقيقية للرؤية ؟ وذلك بخلاف الصوت والحرف لأنهما علتان حقيقيتان للسماع .
As for the Maturidites who considered hearing the internal speech impossible due to the requirement of sound and letter, they distinguished between the cause (al-illa) and the normal condition (al-shart al-adi). By this they reject the analogy of hearing to vision, because what the Mu'tazilites mention as conditions for vision are only normal conditions for vision and not real causes for vision. This is unlike sound and letter, because they are real causes for hearing.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage presents the reasoning of those Maturidites who deny the possibility of hearing the internal speech (kalam nafsi). They respond to an objection that draws an analogy between hearing and vision: just as the Mu'tazilites argue that vision is impossible due to certain conditions (like direction, distance, etc.), so too hearing internal speech is impossible. The Maturidites here make a crucial distinction between a real cause (illa) and a normal condition (shart adi). The Mu'tazilite conditions for vision are merely normal conditions (e.g., being in front, proper distance), not intrinsic causes. But sound and letter, they argue, are real causes for hearing. That is, hearing necessarily requires sound and letter as its cause; without them, hearing cannot occur. Therefore, the analogy with vision fails: the conditions for vision are contingent, but the requirement of sound and letter is essential for hearing. Hence, hearing internal speech, which is without sound and letters, is impossible.

Key Terms
العلة
الشرط العادي
السماع
الرؤية
الكلام النفسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Maturidites also deny the possibility of seeing God, since they reject analogy.
Correction: They only use the distinction to defend the possibility of seeing God (by saying vision conditions are merely normal) while denying hearing internal speech (because sound/letters are real causes).

Checkpoint
What distinction do these Maturidites use to argue that hearing internal speech is impossible?
A) The difference between hearing and sight.
B) The difference between real cause and normal condition.
C) The difference between audible and inaudible.
D) The difference between divine and human speech.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidite distinction between real cause and normal condition shows that hearing internal speech is impossible because sound and letters are essential causes, while conditions for vision are merely normal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own theological reasoning, do you ever confuse normal conditions with real causes? Where might this happen in your understanding of divine attributes? Examine one belief you hold and ask: is the condition I assume essential for God's action actually a real cause, or merely a normal condition?
?
When you hear arguments that equate divine attributes (like seeing and hearing) based on human experience, do you check whether the underlying causes are the same in God? How would you respond to someone who says 'just as we can see God, we can hear Him'?
?
If internal speech cannot be heard because sound is a real cause for hearing, what does this reveal about the limits of human perception in matters of faith? Are you content to accept that some divine realities are beyond your senses, or do you find yourself trying to force God into your sensory categories?

The Necessity of a Beginning for Sequential Existence
Maturidi argues against infinite regress using an eating analogy and applies it to the doubling of reckoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why any sequence of contingent events must have a starting point, as argued by Imam al-Maturidi.

Core Idea
Without a beginning, nothing real can ever begin to exist.

This passage from Imam al-Maturidi tackles a fundamental logical problem: can a chain of causes go back forever? Using a simple eating example, he shows that if every action requires a prior action, no action ever starts. The same principle applies to the doubling of reward—without a starting point, no increase can occur.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic once through, then the English translation. Let the argument sink in before analyzing.
Notice the two eating scenarios.
2
Understand the eating example
The first scenario: 'Do not eat anything until you eat something else' leads to never eating. The second: 'Whenever you eat a morsel, eat another' leads to endless eating. Ask: what does the first illustrate about infinite regress?
3
Apply to the doubling of reckoning
Maturidi says: if no beginning is made for the doubling, no part can exist. With a beginning, it can increase forever. Relate this to the eating example.
This shows the principle is universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن الشيء إذا لم يكن إلا بغير يتقدمه وذلك شرط كل الأغيار قيبطل كون الجميع ، ولا كذلك أمرُ البقاء . ألا يُرى أن من قال لآخر : لا تأكل شيئا حتى تأكل غيره وكذا كل غير فيه ذلك الشرط - فبقى أبدا غير " اكل . ولو قال كلما أكلت لقمة فكل أخرى فهو يبقى أبدا" في الأكل ، فمثله الأول : وعلى ذلك أمر تضاعف الحساب ، إنه إذا لم يجعل له ابتداء يبدأ لا يجوز وجود شيء منه بتة ، وإذا بتة ، وإذا حصل البداية يجوز أن يبقى فيه فيما يزيد ، ثم يزيد دائما ، / ولا قوة إلا بالله
And also: if a thing is not except through another that precedes it, and that is the condition of all contingencies (al-aghyār), then the existence of all is nullified, but not so with the matter of permanence (al-baqā'). Do you not see that the one who says to another: 'Do not eat anything until you eat something else' – and likewise everything with that condition – then he remains forever not eating. And if he said: 'Whenever you eat a morsel, then eat another', he remains forever eating. So the first is like that. And upon that is the matter of the doubling of reckoning: if no beginning is made for it, it is not permissible for any part of it to exist at all; but when it has a beginning, it is permissible for it to remain increasing, then increase forever. And there is no power except in God.
Source pages p. 613

Commentary
This is an argument against infinite regress. Maturidi uses an example: if you are required to eat something only after having eaten something else, you will never eat because that condition goes back infinitely. Similarly, if every contingent thing requires a prior condition, nothing would exist. But permanence (baqā') is different because it does not require such a prior condition. He then applies this to the doubling of reward: without a starting point, no reward can begin; with a start, it can increase forever.

Key Terms
شرط
الأغيار
بقاء
ابتداء
تضاعف الحساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies any form of infinity, including future endlessness.
Correction: Maturidi only denies an infinite past regress; he explicitly allows infinite increase once a beginning is established (e.g., eternal reward).

Checkpoint
What does the eating example illustrate?
A) That perpetual motion is possible
B) That a chain of conditions without a beginning prevents actual existence
C) That eating is forbidden
D) That permanence is impossible

Key Takeaway
Any chain of conditions without a beginning cannot produce actual existence; a beginning is essential for anything to come into being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the first eating analogy: 'Do not eat anything until you eat something else.' In your spiritual life, what 'condition' are you waiting for before you take a step toward Allah? Are you like the one who never eats? What would it take for you to simply begin today, without waiting for another condition?
?
Maturidi shows that with a beginning, things can increase forever. What small act of obedience—prayer, charity, forgiveness—could you start right now, even imperfectly, so that it may multiply into lasting reward? What is stopping you from making that beginning?
?
The lesson argues that without a beginning, nothing real ever begins. Is there a long-standing sin or habit you have accepted as 'too hard to start fixing'? How does this argument challenge your excuse? What first step can you commit to today, and to whom will you hold yourself accountable?

Maturidi's Proof for the Beginning of Time
Maturidi argues that every present motion implies a beginning of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's concise rational argument that every motion or gathering we observe today is the endpoint of a past series, and that this series cannot exist without a beginning, thereby establishing that time had a starting point.

Core Idea
Every present moment is the end of a past that must have a beginning.

Imam Maturidi presents a sharp, economical argument for why the past cannot stretch back infinitely. He points to any motion or gathering we can observe and argues that it is the final term of a sequence of past events. If the past had no beginning, this end point could never be reached. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Carefully read the Arabic sentence once. Notice the key terms: حركة (motion), اجتماع (gathering), نهاية (end), ماضى (past), ابتداء (beginning).
There is a textual repetition in the manuscript; the core idea remains clear.
2
Understand the argument structure
Maturidi states an observation: every motion or gathering we point to is the end of what has passed of that kind. Then he concludes: it is impossible for the end of the past to exist without a beginning.
This is a simple conditional argument: if the end exists, the series must have a beginning.
3
Grasp the logical necessity
Ask: why can't the end exist without a beginning? Because to reach an endpoint in an infinite series, you would have to traverse an infinite number of steps, which is impossible. Therefore, the series must be finite in the past direction.
This is the core of the argument against an infinite regress.
4
Reflect on the implication
Maturidi's argument implies that time itself is not eternal but had a beginning. This aligns with the Islamic doctrine that Allah created the universe out of nothing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن كل حركة أو اجتماع كة أو اجتماع نشير إليه هو نهاية ما مضى من ذلك النوع محال وجود نهاية الماضى بلا ابتداء له ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And also: every motion or gathering (or gathering) that we point to is the end of what has passed of that kind; it is impossible for the end of the past to exist without a beginning for it. And there is no power except in God.
Source pages p. 613

Commentary
This is a concise argument for the necessity of a beginning of time. Maturidi states that any specific motion or gathering we observe is the final term of a sequence of past events. Since this end point exists, the series must have a starting point; otherwise, an infinite regress would prevent any end from being reached. The argument relies on the impossibility of traversing an infinite past.

Key Terms
حركة
اجتماع
نهاية
الماضى
ابتداء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this argument denies the possibility of an infinite future or applies to all sequences regardless of direction.
Correction: Maturidi only addresses the past direction. An infinite future is not problematic because it is not traversed. The argument is specific to sequences that have been completed up to the present.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's argument, why can't the end of a past series exist without a beginning?
A) Because God created time
B) Because an infinite past cannot be traversed to reach an endpoint
C) Because motion requires space
D) Because gathering is not a real attribute

Key Takeaway
Every observable motion or gathering today is proof that the series of past events had a beginning, and therefore time is not eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every motion you make today is the end of a past that must have a beginning, how does that make you feel about the reality that your own existence also had a start? What does that demand of you right now?
?
The proof shows that time is not eternal. If your life is confined to a timeline with a start and an end, what does that urgency call you to change today—not tomorrow—in the way you use your time?
?
You point to events every day. Maturidi says that act of pointing itself proves a beginning. When was the last time you sincerely acknowledged that your ability to move, think, and exist is only possible because of the Creator who made time? What will you do to let that truth shape your next hour?

Knowledge Beyond the Senses: Maturidi's Refutation
Maturidi refutes the denial of creation from nothing by appealing to non-sensory knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that creation from nothing is rationally knowable even though it cannot be perceived by the senses.

Core Idea
Not all knowledge comes from the senses; rational reasoning can access realities beyond perception.

Maturidi refutes those who claim that creation from nothing cannot be known because it is not sensed. He argues that many fundamental truths—including the very faculties we use to perceive—are known without direct sense experience. This passage establishes that denying creation from nothing on sensory grounds is inconsistent with how we accept other non-sensory realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the repeated phrases and the structure of the argument. The text uses analogy to show inconsistency in the opponent's position.
The Arabic has some unclear phrases due to OCR; focus on the overall argument.
2
Identify the key claim and analogy
Extract Maturidi's main claim: that knowledge is not confined to sense perception. Identify the example of separation (tafarruq) and what it analogizes to.
Consider why Maturidi mentions both separation and annihilation.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument refutes the denial of creation from nothing. Why does the opponent's own makeup—intellect, hearing, etc.—weaken his position?
The opponent affirms his own faculties without sense perception, so denying creation from nothing on sensory grounds is contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن قال : لا يُعلم صُنع شيء لا من شيء فهو المقدر بالموجود حيسا ، والمعارف نفسها خارجة عن الحس ، وكذلك القول بيحوز ولا يجوز . وكذلك . ما يدعى من التفرق - لا الهلاك - الا تعرفه بالحس لا الهلاك - ا لا تعرفه بالحس ، ثم قال به ، فمثله ما نحن فيه ، مع ما كان فيه عقل وسمع وبصر وروح وغير ذلك مما لا يدرى مم ، فذلك يمنعه القول بما قال .
And whoever says: 'The making of something from nothing is not known'—he is the one who estimates by the existent [؟]—and the knowledges themselves are beyond sense perception, and likewise the statement of 'it is permissible and not permissible.' And likewise what is claimed of separation—not annihilation—you only know it by sense, not annihilation—you only know it by sense, then he affirms it. So the like of this is our case here, along with what it contains of intellect, hearing, sight, spirit, and other things whose origin is unknown. That prevents him from saying what he said.
Source pages p. 614

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who deny that creation from nothing can be known. Maturidi argues that knowledge itself is not derived from sense perception. Many things are known through reason, not senses. For example, we perceive separation (tafarruq) through the senses, but we also affirm its opposite (annihilation?) even if not perceived. Similarly, in this issue, we affirm creation from nothing even though it is not sensed. The opponent's own makeup includes intellect, hearing, sight, spirit, etc., which he does not know by sense, yet he affirms them. So his denial of creation from nothing is inconsistent.

Key Terms
صُنع
لا من شيء
معارف
حس
تفرق
هلاك
عقل
سمع
بصر
روح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All knowledge must come from sense perception; what is not sensed cannot be known.
Correction: Maturidi shows that many necessary truths—including the existence of our own faculties—are known through reason, not the senses. Therefore, creation from nothing can be rationally known even if not sensed.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Maturidi use to show that creation from nothing is knowable despite not being sensed?
A) The existence of the soul and its attributes
B) The fact that we know separation through sense but still affirm it
C) The fact that we know annihilation through sense but still believe in it
D) The concept of color

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that denial of creation from nothing on sensory grounds is contradictory because many realities—including our own faculties—are known through reason, not the senses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own intellect, hearing, and sight, do you ever pause to realize that their ultimate origin is unknown to you? If you trust these faculties without seeing their source, why do you hesitate to trust the reality of Allah's creation from nothing?
?
Your life is shaped by countless things you believe without seeing—your own mind, the past, the future. Maturidi points out the inconsistency of denying creation from nothing while affirming such unseen realities. Where in your daily life do you act as if only the visible is real, and what will you do today to realign your heart with the truth of the unseen?
?
You claim to believe in the Creator, but do your worries and fears reveal that you actually trust only what you can perceive? If you truly accepted that knowledge goes beyond the senses, would your anxiety about the unseen future diminish? What step can you take right now to live by that certainty?
