Maturidi's Positive Proof of Divine Choice
Maturidi establishes evidence for divine choice and refutes other causal theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi proves divine choice from the variation and wisdom in creation, and how he refutes alternative causal explanations.

Core Idea
Creation's diversity and wisdom prove a single divine choice, not multiple causes.

Having refuted Al-Ka'bi's formulation, Maturidi presents his own positive evidence for divine choice. He argues that the variety and order in creation point directly to a choosing Agent. He then systematically refutes alternative explanations such as natures, astrology, and biological inheritance, showing they all lead to an infinite regress or impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage from 'ثم الدلالة' to the end. Note the two main parts: the evidence and the refutation of alternatives.
There are OCR uncertainties; focus on the logical flow.
2
Identify the evidence for choice
Maturidi states that the evidence for choice is the emergence of creation with variation (تفاوت) in accordance with wisdom, and the evidence for God's oneness. This indicates a single choice for everything.
3
Examine the alternative explanations
He lists several alternative causal theories: natures/ nutriments, stars, celestial spheres, biological design of parents. Understand each one.
4
Understand the common flaw
All such theories reduce to one thing coming from another. If there is no initial state (أولية), then an infinite regress results. If there is an initial state, it cannot bring itself into existence, showing the need for a single divine choice.
This is the core argument against natural causality.
5
Connect to tawhid
The proof supports the oneness of God, as the diversity and order require a single source of choice, not many.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الكعبي: أفعال الله باختيار؛ لأن المطبوع يكون فعله نوعاً. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: وما قاله حسن، وذلك مذهب أهل التوحيد لكنه لا معنى له على مذهبه؛ لما يقال الخلق اختياره أو غيره، وكذلك ما يقال في أفعاله. فإن قال: اختياره، فمعنى أفعاله إذا اختياره. فالقول بأن أفعال الله باختيار خطأ، بل هو اختيار ولا غير هنالك ليقال الذي قال: وإن قال: غير، فإما أن يكون فعله، فيجب أن يكون باختيار إلى ما لا نهاية له، وذلك محال؛ لأن الخلق متناه، أو هو فعل بلا اختيار، فيبطل قوله. وذلك يلزم من يصف الله بالإرادة في الأزل، وهي اختيار يكون شيء في وقته.

ثم الدلالة عندنا على الاختيار خروج الخلق على تفاوت [؟]، على ما من الحكمة، والدلالة على وحدانية الله، فدل ذلك على اختيار كون كل شيء على ما هو عليه، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم من يقول بخروج الخلق على ما عليه بالطبائع والأغذية، ومن يقول: ذلك عمل النجم والشمس والقمر، ومن يقول: ذلك بدوران الفلك، ومن يقول في التوالد بتدبير الآباء والأمهات، يرجع كله إلى كون شيء بشيء، إذا لم يثبت له أولية يبطل بالأدلة التي مر ذكرها، وإذا ثبت له أو لكل جنس من ذلك أولية، ثم استحال كونه بنفسه؛ لما يوجد نفسه إما حين خدمه، وذلك محال أن يوجد عديما، مع ما إذ رجع إليه تدبير كل شيء لا يحتمل أن يبلغه.
Al-Ka'bi said: God's actions are by choice (ikhtiyar), because the natural (matbu') has only one type of act. Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: What he said is good, and that is the school of the people of tawhid, but it has no meaning according to his school; because it is said that creation is His choice or other than that, and likewise what is said regarding His actions. If he says: it is His choice, then the meaning of His actions is [that they are] His choice. So the statement that God's actions are by choice is an error; rather, it is choice and there is no other [thing] there for one to say what he said. And if he says: other [than choice], then either it is His action, and then it must be by choice ad infinitum, which is impossible because creation is finite; or it is an action without choice, and thus his statement is invalidated. And that is entailed by describing God with will (irada) in pre-eternity, which is a choice of a thing coming to be at its time.

Then for us the evidence for choice is the emergence of creation with variation of its [essences?], in accordance with wisdom, and the evidence for God's oneness (tawhid). So that indicates choice for everything to be as it is. And there is no power except with Allah. Then those who say that creation emerges as it does by natures and nutriments, and those who say that is the work of the star, sun, and moon, and those who say that is by the rotation of the sphere, and those who say regarding generation by the design of fathers and mothers, all of it reduces to one thing coming from another thing. If an initial state is not established for it, it is invalidated by the proofs that have been mentioned. And if an initial state is established for it, or for each type of that, then it is impossible for it to exist by itself; because it would bring itself into existence either at the time of its service, that is impossible for a thing that does not exist to bring itself into existence, especially when the management of everything is referred back to it, it cannot reach it.
Source pages p. 659

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Al-Ka'bi's claim that God's actions are by choice (ikhtiyar). Al-Ka'bi argued that the natural (matbu') has only one type of act (i.e., it acts uniformly), so God's actions being varied implies choice. Maturidi first praises the statement but then shows it is inconsistent with Al-Ka'bi's own school (presumably Mu'tazilism). He presents a dilemma: if one says creation is God's choice, then it is the choice itself, not an action done by choice—so saying 'actions by choice' is an error. If one says creation is other than His choice, then either it is His action (which would require another choice ad infinitum, impossible since creation is finite) or it is without choice (contradicting Al-Ka'bi). This dilemma also applies to those who affirm God's eternal will, which is a choice for a thing to occur at its time. Then Maturidi presents his own positive evidence (dilalah) for choice: the variation and wisdom in creation point to a single choice and to God's oneness. He then refutes alternative explanations (natures, astrology, celestial spheres, biological generation) by arguing that all such views reduce to one thing causing another, leading to an infinite regress unless an initial state is established, but then that initial state cannot bring itself into existence—this impossibility proves the need for a single divine choice.

Key Terms
التفاوت
الأولية
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That creation's diversity can be adequately explained by natural causes like natures, stars, or biological inheritance.
Correction: Maturidi argues that such explanations lead to an infinite regress or an impossible self-causing first state, thus requiring a single divine choice.

Checkpoint
What is the main positive evidence Maturidi gives for divine choice?
A) The fact that creation is finite.
B) The variation and wisdom in creation, along with proof of God's oneness.
C) The impossibility of infinite regress.
D) The fact that parents design their children.

Key Takeaway
True divine choice is demonstrated by the fact that creation exists with variation and order, which cannot be traced back to an infinite series of causes or a self-caused first state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a success or a blessing, do you truly attribute it to Allah's deliberate choice, or do you silently credit your own efforts, circumstances, or luck? What would change in your heart if you affirmed that 'everything is as it is by His choice'? This week, catch yourself in a moment of attribution and consciously replace it with gratitude to the Chooser.
?
The passage refutes attributing events to 'natures' or 'celestial bodies.' Can you identify a modern equivalent—a scientific or cultural explanation you default to instead of seeing Allah's direct hand? What would it cost you to abandon that mental crutch and live with constant awareness of His management? Commit to one situation where you will intentionally override that default.

The Absurdity of Self-Creation: Mu'tazila and Dualists Compared
Maturidi presents the absurdity of the world being self-creator and compares it to dualist theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers the Mu'tazilite view of the world's causation leads to the world being self-creator, and how this resembles the dualist theory of light and darkness mixing.

Core Idea
Claiming the world creates itself is as irrational as the dualist myth of light and darkness.

Maturidi exposes a devastating logical flaw in the Mu'tazilite position: by denying that the world's existence requires a transcendent Creator, they end up making the world both creator and created—a clear contradiction. He then shows that this view closely parallels the dualist (Thunawiyya) belief that the world emerged from the mixture of light and darkness without any external cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the first part of the Arabic passage focusing on the contradiction and the dualist comparison.
Notice the repetition of 'خلق' and 'صنع' roots.
2
Identify the core contradiction
Maturidi says the Mu'tazila made the world a creator while itself created. Why is that contradictory?
Something cannot cause its own existence.
3
Compare with the Dualist view
The Dualists said light and darkness mixed to form the world without any external cause. How does this resemble the Mu'tazilite view?
Both involve self-causation or no external cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أعجب منه أن جعلوا العالم خالقا ونفسه مخلوقة، وجعلوه صانعا ونفسه مصنوعة، فصار العالم عالما لا يصنع لغيره فيه، بصنع لغيره فيه، ثم ألزم نفسه كل اسم دنى لكان أعذر، ولكن ذا آية عواقب السوء. وكل اسم سنى، لو قلب اسم العالم العالم وأيضا من مضاهاتهم فى هذا قول الثنوية : إن الأشياء كانت معدومة ثم وجدت من غير أن كانت [به]، ثم إيجادها غير خروجها من العدم. وقالت الثنوية : كان النور والظلمة متباينين فامتزجا، فكان هذا العالم من غير أن كان، ثم تباين غيرا، وامتزاج غير، فصار العالم عالما بنفسه بعد أن لم يكن عالما؛ إذا غير هنالك أوجب ذلك، وكذا قول المعتزلة على ما ذكرنا، ولا قوة إلا بالله واستدلت المعتزلة وغيرهم على حدث العالم حدث العالم بما لا يخلو عن بما لا يخلو عن محدث، ولم يكن دليلهم على ذلك سوى وجود العالم فأوجبوا حدثه، وقالوا : ذلك بمحدث. ثم
Then more astonishing than that is that they made the world a creator while itself created, and they made it a maker while itself made, so the world became a world that does not make anything else, but makes its own existence, then it imposed upon itself every lowly name would have been more excusable, but this is a sign of evil consequences. And every lofty name, if one were to invert the name of the world to the world. And also from their resemblance in this is the saying of the Dualists (Thunawiyya): that things were non-existent then existed without that [by] it, then their bringing into existence is not their coming out of non-existence. And the Dualists said: light and darkness were separate then they mixed, so this world came to be without that it was, then they separated as other, and mixture as other, so the world became a world by itself after it had not been a world; when the other there necessitated that. And such is the saying of the Mu'tazila according to what we mentioned, and there is no power but with God. And the Mu'tazila and others argued for the temporality of the world by that which does not lack that which does not lack a temporalizer, and their proof for that was nothing but the existence of the world, so they necessitated its temporality, and they said: that is by a temporalizer. Then

Commentary
This passage highlights the absurdity of the Mu'tazilite position: they make the world both creator and created, which is contradictory. Maturidi compares them to the Dualists (Thunawiyya), who held that things come from non-existence without a cause, and that light and darkness mixed to form the world, making the world self-causing. The Mu'tazila similarly claim the world is caused but without a divine cause, leading to self-causation. Maturidi also criticizes their proof for the world's temporality: they merely point to its existence and claim it must have a temporalizer, but this is circular. The passage ends with a reminder that only God gives strength.

Correction: Maturidi shows their view leads to the world being self-creator—a logical contradiction that is no better than dualist mythology.

Critique of the Mu'tazilite Proof for the World's Temporality
Maturidi critiques the Mu'tazilite argument for the world's temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique that the Mu'tazilite proof for the world's temporality is circular and insufficient.

Core Idea
Existence alone does not prove temporality.

After comparing the Mu'tazila to dualists, Maturidi turns to their main argument for the world's temporality. He notes that their proof is based solely on the fact that the world exists, and from that they conclude it must have a temporalizer. Maturidi implies this is an inadequate proof because it does not rule out the possibility of self-causation or eternal existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the proof
Read the second part of the Arabic passage, focusing on how Maturidi describes the Mu'tazilite argument.
Note the repetition 'لا يخلو عن' which indicates a dense phrase.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite proof
According to Maturidi, the Mu'tazila argued for the world's temporality based on what?
They used the fact that the world exists and is composed of temporal accidents.
3
Understand Maturidi's criticism
Why is it insufficient to simply point to the world's existence? Maturidi seems to say they only had the world's existence as evidence.
He implies their reasoning is circular or incomplete.

Refuting Dualism: Allah's Exclusive Causality Over Accidents
Maturidi refutes the possibility of multiple creators for observable accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the claim that accidents in the world could be caused by multiple agents and why this leads to dualism.

Core Idea
If accidents can come from others, then the world points to multiple creators, but Allah has given no sign to distinguish His acts.

Imam Maturidi addresses a dangerous line of reasoning: if we observe that some accidents (like combination and separation) are produced by human actions, might they also be produced by other beings? This would open the door to dualism or polytheism. In this passage, Maturidi dismantles this argument and shows why it collapses under its own assumption.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice the structure: first a premise, then a consequence, then a critique.
2
Identify the premise
Maturidi states that the world contains accidents (combination, separation, motion, rest) that could possibly occur without Allah, as seen in human actions like building ships or writing.
The examples are meant to show that such accidents are not exclusive to God.
3
Understand the consequence
If those accidents can come from creation, then the world as a whole could be attributed to multiple agents, leading to dualism (two creators) or polytheism (multiple natures and stars).
This is the logical outcome the dualists accept.
4
Examine the critique
Maturidi argues that Allah did not provide a clear sign to distinguish His making from others'. Since we observe accidents without seeing their immediate mover, the possibility that they come from other than Him remains—but this reasoning is flawed.
The flaw is that without a distinguishing proof, the dualist argument is baseless.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by equating this reasoning to that of dualists and nature theorists, who claim things come to be by themselves—a sign of incapacity. Maturidi thus refutes the claim by showing it leads to an absurdity.
The refutation is indirect: the premise leads to an unacceptable conclusion (dualism), so the premise is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن العالم عندهم إذ لا يخلو عن اجتماع وافتراق، وزوال وقرار، وهذه أحوال احتمل كونها بغير الله، على ما يكون من اجتماع آجر السفن والبنيان والكتابة ونحوها، وكذلك التفريق، وعلى ما كان سير الشمس والقمر وحركات الخلق وسكونهم، ولا عالم بدون وجود هذين النوعين من الأعراض والأجسام، وكان ذلك جملة يحتمل الكون بالله وبالخلق، فكان العالم جملة يحتمل الكون بالله وبالخلق، فكان العالم جملة بعدد، وذلك قول الزنادقة بإثنين، وأصحاب الطبائع والنجوم بأكثر من اثنين. وبعد، فإن الله جل ثناؤه لم يقم على قولهم حجة إحداثه سوى العالم، ولا حجة قدمه، ثم لم يفصل بين الأعراض التي هي صنع غيره وبين التي هي له، لأن الاجتماع والزوال ونحو ذلك قد يوجد في العيان ولا يرى له الجامع المحرك، ويحتمل أن يكون ذلك لغيره لا له وإن لم يعاين، إذ هو نظير ما يعاين منه، فصار ذلك كقول الثنوية وأصحاب الطبائع: تكون الأشياء بها من غير أن أقام كل منها دليل صنعه مما يفصل به من صنع غيره، وذلك آية العجز.
And further, the world according to them, since it is not free of combination and separation, and passing and settling, and these are states that could possibly occur without Allah, such as the combination of the bricks of ships and buildings and writing and the like, and likewise separation, and such as the movement of the sun and moon and the movements of creatures and their stillness, and no world exists without the existence of these two types of accidents and bodies, and that was a whole that could possibly come to be from Allah and from creation, so the world as a whole could possibly come to be from Allah and from creation, so the world as a whole was with number, and that is the statement of the dualists with two, and the adherents of natures and stars with more than two. And further, Allah, mighty and majestic, did not establish according to their statement a proof of His causing it to occur except the world, nor a proof of His eternity, then He did not distinguish between the accidents that are the making of other than Him and those that are His, because combination and passing and the like may be found in ocular perception and one does not see for it a combiner or mover, and it is possible that that could be from other than Him not from Him, even if it is not witnessed, since it is similar to what is witnessed from it, so that became like the statement of the dualists and the adherents of natures: things come to be by them without each one establishing a proof of its making that distinguishes it from the making of another, and that is a sign of incapacity.
Source pages p. 688

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that the world's accidents (like combination and separation) could be caused by agents other than Allah. Maturidi argues that if such accidents can be observed in human actions (building ships, writing), then they could potentially be attributed to other than God, leading to dualism (two creators) or polytheism (multiple natures and stars). He then points out that Allah has not provided a proof that distinguishes His actions from others'—He only provides the world as a sign. Since no distinguishing mark is given, the claim that accidents might come from another is baseless and resembles the incapacity of dualists.

Key Terms
اجتماع
افتراق
الزنادقة
الطبائع
الثنوية
الجامع المحرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi denies that humans cause any accidents.
Correction: Maturidi does not deny human agency; he refutes the inference that because humans cause some accidents, other agents could cause others, leading to multiple creators.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say is the result of allowing accidents to come from other than Allah?
A) It confirms human free will.
B) It leads to dualism or polytheism.
C) It proves the existence of natures.
D) It is impossible.

Key Takeaway
If accidents could be caused by multiple agents, then no proof exists to distinguish God's acts from others', leading to dualism—a conclusion that refutes itself.

The Invalidity of Asking 'Why' About Creation
Maturidi introduces the view that the question 'why' is invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial argument that the question 'Why did Allah create creation?' is invalid because Allah is wise.

Core Idea
Asking 'why' of a wise being's actions is itself flawed.

Maturidi begins a new section by reporting a disagreement among scholars regarding a fundamental question: why did God create? Some argue that the very question is invalid, because attributing a purpose to the wise Creator in a way that demands justification misapplies human reasoning. This lesson presents that first position.

Identify the key claim

3

This connects to broader discussions of divine attributes in kalam.

The Invalidity of Asking 'Why Creation?' Due to Causal Regress
An argument that the question 'why creation' entails an impossible causal regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some theologians argue that asking 'why did God create?' is logically invalid due to causal regress.

Core Idea
The question 'why creation' forces an impossible either-or: an infinite chain or an eternal co-being.

This passage presents a specific argument that the question of why God created is not simply unanswerable but logically impossible. It shows that any answer presupposes a cause that leads to an absurdity. We'll examine how this reasoning protects God's uniqueness and eternality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the argument's flow become clear.
Notice that the passage starts with a claim and then gives two options.
2
Identify the two options for the cause
The passage presents two possibilities for what the assumed cause could be: either it is itself a creation, or it is not. Understand each.
The Arabic word 'إما' signals the dichotomy.
3
Understand why each option is problematic
If the cause is a creation, the question repeats infinitely. If the cause is not a creation, it implies another eternal entity besides God. Both are unacceptable.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Connect to God's self-sufficiency
The passage concludes that God created by His essence without external cause. This preserves His uniqueness and self-sufficiency.
This is the positive doctrine that the argument supports.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قوم : السؤال محال ؛ لما يوجب تقدم علة لما يخلق ، والعلة إما أن يكون خلقا ، فالسؤال عنها هو السؤال عن جملة ، أو لا يكون ، فيكون غير إله فى الأزل ، بل خلق بأن فعل الخلق بذاته على ما مر بيانه ، والله الموفق .
And a group said: The question is impossible, because it requires a cause preceding what He creates. And the cause is either a creation, so the question about it is the question about the whole, or it is not, so it would be something other than God in pre-eternity, rather He created by His essence as previously explained. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 697

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of a group that the question 'Why did God create?' is logically invalid (محال). The reasoning is that the question assumes a cause (علة) that precedes the creation. If that cause is itself a creation, then the question simply repeats about that cause — leading to an infinite regress. If the cause is not a creation, then it must be something other than God existing in pre-eternity, which contradicts God's uniqueness. Therefore the only valid position is that God created by His essence without external cause, as previously established. The passage concludes with 'والله الموفق' (And God is the Grantor of success), a common closing for a section.

Key Terms
محال
علة
السؤال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this group denies all causation in God's actions.
Correction: They do not deny causation altogether; they reject the presupposition that God's creation requires an external cause. They affirm that God creates by His essence without any prior cause.

Checkpoint

C) Because creation is an illusion and does not exist.
D) Because God is incapable of explaining His actions.

Key Takeaway
The question 'why creation' is invalid because it forces an impossible choice: either an infinite regress of causes or an eternal entity alongside God.

The Three Meanings of the Question and Their Refutation
Another group reduces the question to three possible meanings and refutes each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible meanings of the question about creation and how each is refuted, showing the question to be invalid.

Core Idea
The question 'Why creation?' dissolves when its possible meanings are examined.

Maturidi presents an argument from a group that the question 'Why did God create the world?' can only have three possible meanings. Each meaning is shown to be self-defeating or leading to an infinite regress. This passage illustrates the logical method of refuting skeptical or theological challenges by analyzing their semantic content.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: the group enumerates three meanings and then refutes each.
The second meaning contains an unclear phrase; we will note it later.
2
Identify the first meaning
The first meaning: asking why this world rather than another. The refutation is that this question simply shifts to the other world, leading to an infinite regress. Also, the idea of 'why not earlier' assumes creation is separate from time, but creation makes time exist.
This refutation uses the concept of time as contingent on creation.
3
Examine the second meaning
The second meaning is partially garbled in the text. It seems to involve information about existence in something. Due to textual uncertainty, we note it but cannot analyze fully.
The original text marks an unclear word with [؟].
4
Understand the third meaning
The third meaning: asking about the reality of the world. This turns the question back on the asker—asking 'why do I ask?' leads to a self-refutation. The asker himself prevents the question by being rational.
This is a reductio ad absurdum of the questioner's own position.
5
Summarize the argument
All three possible meanings are invalid. Therefore, the original question 'Why creation?' is itself invalid. The passage concludes with 'And with God is success.'
This is a complete argument defeating a specific objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قوم : السؤال لا يعدو معان : إما أن نقول : لم خلق هذا العالم دون أن يخلق غيره ؟ فيكون هذا السؤال فيه كهو في هذا ، وكذلك في قوله : لم لا خلق الخلق ليكون قبل الوقت الذى كان ؟ على أن الخلق ليس هو غير الوقت يصير كونه وقتا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله بل هو إخبار عن كونه في [؟] ، أو يُسأل عن حقيقة هذا العالم ، فيكون سؤاله منه ، فكأنه قال : ليم أسأل ؟ وليم عقلت أن أسأل ؟ وليم لا كنت غير عاقل ؟ وذلك فاسد ؛ لأنه نفسه منع عن السؤال ، وبالله التوفيق .
And a group said: The question does not exceed three meanings: Either we say: Why did He create this world and not another? Then this question in it is like the same question in this [other world]. Likewise His saying: Why did He not create creation to be before the time that was? Given that creation is not other than time, its existence becomes time. And there is no power except with God. Rather it is informing about its existence in [؟]. Or one asks about the reality of this world, then his question is from it, as if he said: Why do I ask? And why did I understand to ask? And why was I not other than rational? And that is corrupt, because he himself prevented the question. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 697

Commentary
This passage presents a second group's analysis that the question 'Why creation?' can only have three possible meanings, each of which is refuted. The first meaning: asking why this world rather than another — this simply shifts the same question to the other world, leading to an infinite regress. Also asking why creation was not created earlier implies that creation is distinct from time, but in fact creation is what makes time exist. The second meaning appears garbled in the text: it may be an alternative formulation about 'his existence in [something]'. The third meaning: asking about the reality of the world — this turns the question back on the asker (why am I asking? why am I rational? etc.), which is absurd because the asker himself is the one preventing the question. The conclusion is that the question is invalid.

Key Terms
معان
سؤال
العالم
الخلق
الوقت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third meaning might be misunderstood as solipsism or a rejection of objective reality.
Correction: It is a reductio ad absurdum: if the question is about the reality of the world, then the asker himself is part of that reality, and his questioning implies a standpoint that undermines the question.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is one of the three possible meanings of the question 'Why creation?' that is refuted?
A) Asking why God created the world for a purpose.
B) Asking why this world rather than another, which leads to regress.
C) Asking when creation occurred, which has a clear answer.
D) Asking who created God, which is valid.

Key Takeaway
The question 'Why creation?' is invalid because each possible meaning leads to absurdity or self-refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Al-Husayn's Multi-Purpose Answer on Divine Creation
Al-Husayn lists purposes for creation and refutes pure benefit view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Husayn's argument that creation serves multiple purposes beyond mere benefit, including signs, lessons, and trial.

Core Idea
Creation has diverse reasons: indication, lesson, mercy, and trial—not just benefit.

Al-Husayn (al-Najjar) offers a nuanced response to the question 'Why did God create?'. He enumerates several purposes, showing that divine wisdom encompasses more than pure benefit. This passage challenges simplistic views of creation as solely for human ease.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read Al-Husayn's answer carefully, noting the list of purposes he gives.
The list includes: دلالة وحجة, عبرة وعظمة, نعمة ورحمة, غذاء وقوام.
2
Identify the key categories
Notice that Al-Husayn groups purposes into pairs: indication/proof, lesson/majesty, grace/mercy, nourishment/sustenance, and then disposal of needs.
3
Understand the argument against pure benefit
Al-Husayn argues that if creation were only for benefits, no variation (delay, advance, increase, decrease) would be possible. But we see variation, so creation is not purely utilitarian.
He uses the observation of differing outcomes (blessing for one, trial for another) as evidence.
4
Connect to the divine attribute of wisdom
This passage shows that God's wisdom involves placing things in their proper contexts, even turning benefit to harm and vice versa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الحسين في جواب هذا السؤال : إنه خلق لأسباب يكثر منها دلالة وحجة ، ثم عبرة وعظمة ، ثم نعمة ورحمة ، ثم غذاء وقوام ، ومتصرفا في الحوائج، ومنه ما خلق نعمة لأحد بلية على آخر . قال : ولو خلق ابتداء الخلق للمصالح والمنافع لا غير لم يكن يجوز تقديم شي ولا تأخيره ، ولا خلق شيء قبل خلق ، ولا قلب أمرا من حال إلى حال ، ولا زيادة ولا نقصان ، وإذ خلق الممتحن الأوهام واستترت عن نصرة الأنام ، ثبت أن الأمر الخلائق ما لا يحيط ليس على ذلك لكنه في وضع الأشياء موضعها ، وصرف الأمور من النفع إلى الضرر ، والضرر إلى النفع ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . قال الفقيه رحمه الله : وجملة هذا الفصل أنه على قولهم إذ لم يكن له غير الذى فعل لم يكن شيء من فعله مفضلا ؛ إذ هو أبقى بكل فعله صفة الجور ، ولا كان لما يفعله مختارا له ، إذ لو كان منه غير ذلك كان مفسدا ، وكان عن جعل الإصلاح في غيره عاجزا ، وذلك هو النهاية من صفة الدم [؟] ، والله الموفق . ولو كان لا يجوز له غير الذي فعل لكان بفعله منتفعا ، ويفسير هو إليه محتاجا ليجمد به ويثني عليه ، إذ من لا يستحق حمدا ولا مدحا إلا بغيره فهو إليه محتاج فى أن يحق له الثناء ، وبه منتفع ، إذ من قولهم : إن فعله غيره ، ولم
And al-Husayn said in answer to this question: He created for reasons, among them many are indication and proof, then lesson and majesty, then grace and mercy, then nourishment and sustenance, and disposal in needs. And among it He created a blessing for one and a trial for another. He said: If He had created the beginning of creation only for benefits and interests, it would not be permissible to advance anything or delay it, nor to create something before another, nor to turn a matter from one state to another, nor increase nor decrease. And since He created the tested with illusions and they were hidden from helping the people, it is established that the matter of creatures which is not encompassed is not upon that, but rather it is about putting things in their places, and turning matters from benefit to harm and from harm to benefit. And there is no power except with God. The faqih (may God have mercy on him) said: The summary of this chapter is that according to their statement, since there is no other than what He did, nothing of His action is preferable, because He would have kept the attribute of injustice in all His actions. And He would not be free in what He does, for if something else were from Him, it would be corrupt, and He would be incapable of placing reform in another. And that is the ultimate of the attribute of blame? And God is the Grantor of success. And if it were not permissible for Him other than what He did, He would be benefiting by His action, and He would be in need of it to become solid and be praised, since he who does not deserve praise except through another is in need of that other for praise to be due to him, and benefits from it. For from their statement: that His action is other than Him, and not...
Source pages p. 698

Commentary
This passage contains two parts. First, al-Husayn (al-Husayn al-Najjar) gives his answer to the question 'Why creation?'. He enumerates multiple purposes: the creation provides signs and proofs (دلالة وحجة), lessons and majesty (عبرة وعظمة), grace and mercy (نعمة ورحمة), nourishment and sustenance (غذاء وقوام), and general disposal of needs. Moreover, the same thing can be a blessing for one and a trial for another. He argues that if creation were only for benefits, then no change, delay, or variation would be possible. But since we observe variation (misfortune, etc.), it shows that the divine wisdom is not limited to pure benefit but involves placing things in their proper contexts and turning matters from benefit to harm and vice versa. Second, the faqih (Imam al-Maturidi) concludes the chapter by refuting the necessitarian view that God could only do what He actually did. If God had no alternative, then He would have no choice, His action would be unjust (because no act could be better or worse), and He would be dependent on His action for praise — which is impossible for God. The faqih's argument is that if God were compelled to act as He did, then His action would not be freely chosen (مختار), He would be incapable of doing otherwise, and He would need His creation to be praised — all of which are inconsistent with divine perfection.

Key Terms
الحسين
دلالة
عبرة
نعمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creation exists only for human benefit and ease.
Correction: Al-Husayn shows creation has multiple purposes including trial and lesson, and even things that cause harm are part of divine wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Husayn, which of the following is NOT mentioned as a purpose of creation?
A) Indication and proof
B) Lesson and majesty
C) Punishment and anger
D) Grace and mercy

Key Takeaway
Creation serves diverse purposes—signs, lessons, mercy, nourishment, and trial—showing that divine wisdom is not limited to pure benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficulty or trial, do you immediately assume God has abandoned His wisdom or that it is purely negative? How does knowing that God created some things as a trial for one and a blessing for another change your perspective on a current hardship you are experiencing? Will you now ask God to show you the wisdom behind it instead of just seeking removal?
?
Are you living as if your comfort and benefit are the ultimate goals of your existence? If God's creation includes purposes like indication, lesson, and trial, what does that demand from you in terms of your daily priorities and your response to events that don't go your way? Commit today to one specific area where you will seek the 'lesson' instead of just the 'benefit.'
?
This passage teaches that God 'turns matters from benefit to harm and from harm to benefit.' Reflect on a recent turn of events that seemed harmful. Can you now see how that might have been a turning for good in ways you did not expect? What does this say about your trust in God's planning versus your own? Write a short prayer asking for the wisdom to see His hand in every change.

The Faqih's Refutation of Necessitarian Views
The faqih deduces consequences of necessitarian view: injustice, lack of freedom, and dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the faqih's argument that if God could not have done otherwise, His actions would be unjust and He would not be free.

Core Idea
If God had no alternative, He would be unjust and dependent—impossible for the Perfect.

Imam al-Maturidi (the faqih) concludes the chapter by refuting the view that God could only do what He actually did. He argues that such a necessitarian position implies that God's actions are all unjust, that He is not free, and that He would need creation for praise—all contradictions to divine perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the faqih's conclusion carefully, following his logical deductions.
The Arabic has some uncertain readings near the end; focus on the clear parts.
2
Understand the first implication
If God could not do otherwise, then nothing He does can be preferred over another, so He retains the attribute of injustice (الجور).
Because if there is no alternative, then every action is equally necessary, not chosen.
3
Understand the second implication
If God could not do otherwise, He would not be free (مختار). Any other action would be corrupt, implying He cannot reform elsewhere.
This makes Him dependent on a single outcome.
4
Understand the third implication
If God could not do otherwise, He would benefit from His own action and need it for praise—making Him dependent, which is impossible.
Praise is due to God inherently, not because of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الحسين في جواب هذا السؤال : إنه خلق لأسباب يكثر منها دلالة وحجة ، ثم عبرة وعظمة ، ثم نعمة ورحمة ، ثم غذاء وقوام ، ومتصرفا في الحوائج، ومنه ما خلق نعمة لأحد بلية على آخر . قال : ولو خلق ابتداء الخلق للمصالح والمنافع لا غير لم يكن يجوز تقديم شي ولا تأخيره ، ولا خلق شيء قبل خلق ، ولا قلب أمرا من حال إلى حال ، ولا زيادة ولا نقصان ، وإذ خلق الممتحن الأوهام واستترت عن نصرة الأنام ، ثبت أن الأمر الخلائق ما لا يحيط ليس على ذلك لكنه في وضع الأشياء موضعها ، وصرف الأمور من النفع إلى الضرر ، والضرر إلى النفع ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . قال الفقيه رحمه الله : وجملة هذا الفصل أنه على قولهم إذ لم يكن له غير الذى فعل لم يكن شيء من فعله مفضلا ؛ إذ هو أبقى بكل فعله صفة الجور ، ولا كان لما يفعله مختارا له ، إذ لو كان منه غير ذلك كان مفسدا ، وكان عن جعل الإصلاح في غيره عاجزا ، وذلك هو النهاية من صفة الدم [؟] ، والله الموفق . ولو كان لا يجوز له غير الذي فعل لكان بفعله منتفعا ، ويفسير هو إليه محتاجا ليجمد به ويثني عليه ، إذ من لا يستحق حمدا ولا مدحا إلا بغيره فهو إليه محتاج فى أن يحق له الثناء ، وبه منتفع ، إذ من قولهم : إن فعله غيره ، ولم
And al-Husayn said in answer to this question: He created for reasons, among them many are indication and proof, then lesson and majesty, then grace and mercy, then nourishment and sustenance, and disposal in needs. And among it He created a blessing for one and a trial for another. He said: If He had created the beginning of creation only for benefits and interests, it would not be permissible to advance anything or delay it, nor to create something before another, nor to turn a matter from one state to another, nor increase nor decrease. And since He created the tested with illusions and they were hidden from helping the people, it is established that the matter of creatures which is not encompassed is not upon that, but rather it is about putting things in their places, and turning matters from benefit to harm and from harm to benefit. And there is no power except with God. The faqih (may God have mercy on him) said: The summary of this chapter is that according to their statement, since there is no other than what He did, nothing of His action is preferable, because He would have kept the attribute of injustice in all His actions. And He would not be free in what He does, for if something else were from Him, it would be corrupt, and He would be incapable of placing reform in another. And that is the ultimate of the attribute of blame? And God is the Grantor of success. And if it were not permissible for Him other than what He did, He would be benefiting by His action, and He would be in need of it to become solid and be praised, since he who does not deserve praise except through another is in need of that other for praise to be due to him, and benefits from it. For from their statement: that His action is other than Him, and not...
Source pages p. 698

Commentary
This passage contains two parts. First, al-Husayn (al-Husayn al-Najjar) gives his answer to the question 'Why creation?'. He enumerates multiple purposes: the creation provides signs and proofs (دلالة وحجة), lessons and majesty (عبرة وعظمة), grace and mercy (نعمة ورحمة), nourishment and sustenance (غذاء وقوام), and general disposal of needs. Moreover, the same thing can be a blessing for one and a trial for another. He argues that if creation were only for benefits, then no change, delay, or variation would be possible. But since we observe variation (misfortune, etc.), it shows that the divine wisdom is not limited to pure benefit but involves placing things in their proper contexts and turning matters from benefit to harm and vice versa. Second, the faqih (Imam al-Maturidi) concludes the chapter by refuting the necessitarian view that God could only do what He actually did. If God had no alternative, then He would have no choice, His action would be unjust (because no act could be better or worse), and He would be dependent on His action for praise — which is impossible for God. The faqih's argument is that if God were compelled to act as He did, then His action would not be freely chosen (مختار), He would be incapable of doing otherwise, and He would need His creation to be praised — all of which are inconsistent with divine perfection.

Key Terms
الفقيه
مختار
الجور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is compelled to create exactly as He did; He had no choice.
Correction: Al-Maturidi argues that such a view implies God is unjust, not free, and dependent—all impossible for Allah. God is absolutely free and His actions are based on wisdom, not necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the faqih, what does the claim that God could not have done otherwise imply about His freedom?
A) He is completely free.
B) He is not free (مختار).
C) He is free only in some actions.
D) His freedom is increased.

Key Takeaway
If God had no alternative, He would be unjust, unfree, and dependent—this refutes necessitarianism and affirms divine freedom and perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe that God could have created you differently or given you a different fate, does that belief free you from despair or lead you to question His justice? How does al-Maturidi's argument that God is free and not unjust affect your trust in His decree?
?
Al-Maturidi argues that if God had no alternative, He would be dependent on His actions for praise. In your own life, do you seek praise from others as if you need it? How does affirming God's absolute independence challenge your need for human validation?
?
When things don't go your way, do you ever think that God had no choice but to do that to you? How does the faqih's refutation of necessitarianism remind you that God's actions are always free and just, even when you don't understand them?

Why Reward and Punishment Are Essential for Moral Action
Maturidi explains the rational necessity of promise and threat for moral action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that divine promise and threat are not merely incentives but rationally necessary for humans to act morally.

Core Idea
Promise and threat make moral struggle possible.

Maturidi addresses a deep question: why do we need the promise of heaven and the threat of hell? He explains that human nature shuns what is difficult or unpleasant, so only the prospect of consequences can move us to obey. In this passage, he shows that this is not arbitrary but grounded in how humans are created.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem: the soul's natural aversion
Read the first part of the Arabic passage and notice how Maturidi describes the struggle of the soul against what it naturally dislikes. What is the problem he sets up?
The soul shuns what it dislikes, so commands are inherently difficult.
2
Find the only solution: promise and threat
The passage states that there is no way to overcome the soul's aversion except by bringing to mind promise and threat. See how Maturidi presents this as the sole effective motivator.
This is the core psychological function of reward and punishment.
3
Understand the deeper reason: humans judge actions by consequences
Maturidi then adds that humans are created to consider an action ugly if it does not aim at beneficial consequences or avoid harmful ones. Therefore, actions must have consequences. This is why promise and threat are not arbitrary but fit human nature.
This establishes the rational necessity, not just practicality.
4
Draw the conclusion: without consequences, moral distinctions collapse
The passage concludes that without promise and threat, the outcomes of the obedient and disobedient would be equal, which is unjust. Since people differ in choices, consequences must differ. Summarize the logical flow.
This shows the justice of reward and punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما في الأمر والنهي مجاهدة النفس وحملها على ما يكرهه الطبع ، والذى يكرهه تنفر عنه النفس فلا يجد الممتحن على قهره وصرفه إلى ما يريده ويؤمر به سبيلا إلا إحضار الوعد والوعيد ، حتى إذا رأى ذلك سهل عليه ترك الملاذ ، وهان عليه تحمل المؤن العظام . وبعد ، فإن البشر خلق خلقا قبح عليه فعل الذي لا يقصد به نفع العواقب، أو لا تتقى به ضرر العواقب ، فلا بدا أن يجعل لأعماله ذلك ، وذلك حق الوعد والوعيد ، ولولا ذلك لكان يستوى عواقب العدو والولى ، وعلى ما تفاوتا هما بحيث الاختيار والإيثار ، يجب تفاوت عواقبها ، وبالله التوفيق
Along with that, in command and prohibition is the struggle of the soul and its being compelled toward what nature dislikes; and what it dislikes, the soul shuns. So the tested person finds no way to overcome it and turn it to what he desires and is commanded except by bringing to mind promise and threat, until when he sees that, abandoning pleasures becomes easy for him, and bearing great burdens becomes light. Moreover, humans are created such that they consider ugly an action that is not intended for benefit in the consequences or from which harm in the consequences is not avoided. So it is necessary that actions be assigned that [purpose]; that is the right of promise and threat. Without that, the consequences of the enemy and the friend would be equal; and since they differ in terms of choice and preference, their consequences must differ. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 700

Commentary
This passage explains the psychological function of promise and threat. Maturidi states that commands and prohibitions involve struggling against the soul (mujāhadat al-nafs) and compelling it to do what it naturally dislikes. Since the soul shuns what it dislikes, the only way to overcome this aversion is by contemplating the promised reward and threatened punishment. When a person does that, giving up pleasures and bearing hardships becomes easy. Then Maturidi adds that humans are created to consider an action ugly if it does not aim at beneficial consequences or avoid harmful consequences. Therefore, every action must be assigned such consequences, and this is exactly what promise and threat provide. Without them, the outcomes of the obedient and the disobedient would be equal, which is unjust. Since people differ in their choices and preferences, the consequences must differ accordingly.

الوعد والوعيد
الطبع
العواقب
الاختيار والإيثار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That promise and threat are merely external inducements added to commands.
Correction: Maturidi shows they are rationally necessary: without them, actions would lack meaningful consequences and moral distinctions would collapse.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the only way to overcome the soul's natural dislike of difficult commands?
A) Habituation
B) Contemplating promise and threat
C) Social pressure
D) Intellectual argument

Key Takeaway
Promise and threat are rationally required for moral action because humans naturally consider consequences essential and without them moral distinctions would be meaningless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you struggle to obey a command that your soul dislikes, do you genuinely call to mind Allah's promise and threat, or do you rely on your own willpower? Where has that reliance led you?
?
If you truly believed that every action of yours will be met with a consequence from Allah—whether reward or punishment—would you change anything about how you treat the people who have wronged you? Why haven't you changed yet?
?
The passage says humans consider an action ugly if it does not aim at beneficial consequences. In what areas of your life have you been doing good deeds without truly expecting a reward from Allah, or avoiding sins without fearing punishment? What does that reveal about the state of your heart?

The Wisdom Behind Harmful Creatures
Maturidi explains the wisdom behind creating harmful beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's explanation of the wisdom behind creating harmful creatures, including how they serve as tangible examples for reward and punishment and as a trial that involves reflection.

Core Idea
Harmful creatures are created as tangible examples to help us imagine the consequences of our actions.

This lesson presents Imam al-Maturidi's reasoning on why Allah created harmful beings like snakes and poisonous substances. While human intellect cannot fully grasp divine wisdom, Maturidi highlights two aspects: they serve as a trial by exposing us to harm and benefit, and they provide tangible examples to make the promised reward and punishment imaginable.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how Maturidi begins by acknowledging the limits of human intellect.
The passage has two main points, though the first is not explicitly numbered.
2
Identify the first wisdom
Find where Maturidi states that harmful creatures are a trial (miḥna) by the harmful and beneficial, to teach the pleasure of reward and pain of punishment.
This is the core of the first point.
3
Understand the role of tangible examples
Maturidi explains that humans are naturally inclined to consider consequences, so Allah gave tangible examples (mithāl min al-‘iyān) to help imagine the promised reward and punishment.
This makes the path easier.
4
Examine the second wisdom
The second point (al-thānī) discusses that the trial involves a burden that is easy in some ways but difficult for the body through reflection, and people differ in their capacity for such reflection.
This point is brief and may be incomplete.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Consider how acknowledging the limitation of human intellect while still finding wisdom in creation is a key aspect of Maturidi's approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : الحكمة في خلق الحيات والجواهر الضارة وإن كانت العقول تقصر عن بلوغ كنه حكمة الربوبية، على ما سبق القول في لزوم الحكمة لكل شيء من الوجه الذى خلقه الله ، وإن لم يعرف مائيتها يكون من وجوه المحنة بالضار والنافع الحاضرين ، ليعلم بهما لذة الثواب على الطاعة وألم العقاب على المعصية ؛ إذ الخلق جبلوا على قصد العواقب في الأفعال، فيعل لها مثالا من العيان لتتصور الموعود في الأوهام ، فيسهل به السبيل ، والله الموفق . والثاني : أن المحنة تحمل المؤنة التى تسهل ، وتصعب على البدن بالنظر والفكر ، والناس فى تكلف النظر والفكر يختلفون ؛ لأنه ليست لهما منفعة حاضرة ،
Al-Faqih Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The wisdom in creating snakes and harmful substances (jawahir darra) — even if intellects fall short of reaching the core of divine wisdom, as was previously said about the necessity of wisdom for everything from the aspect which Allah created it, even if its quiddity is not known — is that it is one of the aspects of trial (miḥna) by the harmful and the beneficial present, so that by them one may know the pleasure of reward for obedience and the pain of punishment for disobedience; for creatures are naturally inclined to consider consequences in actions, so He made for them a tangible example (mithāl min al-‘iyān) so that the promised (al-maw‘ūd) may be pictured in imaginations, thereby making the path easy. And Allah is the granter of success. And the second: that the trial bears the burden that is easy, and difficult for the body through consideration and thought, and people differ in undertaking consideration and thought because they have no immediate benefit.
Source pages p. 707

Commentary
This passage, attributed to 'al-Faqih Abu Mansur' (Imam al-Maturidi), discusses the wisdom (ḥikma) behind the creation of harmful creatures such as snakes and poisonous substances (jawāhir ḍārra). The author acknowledges that human intellects cannot fully comprehend the depth of divine wisdom, but one aspect is that these creatures serve as a trial (miḥna) through the presence of both harmful and beneficial things. This trial allows people to understand the pleasure of reward for obedience and the pain of punishment for disobedience, since humans are naturally inclined to consider consequences of actions. Thus, God provides tangible examples (mithāl min al-‘iyān) so that the promised reward and punishment can be imagined, making it easier to follow the right path. The 'second' point mentions that the trial involves a burden that is easy in some ways but difficult for the body through reflection and thought, and people differ in their capacity for such reflection because there is no immediate benefit.

جواهر ضارة

عقاب
مثال

Misconception: Harmful creatures have no purpose or are only for punishment.
Correction: Maturidi explains they serve as a trial and tangible examples to teach consequences.

Checkpoint

B) To provide a tangible example of reward and punishment
C) To test the prophets
D) To show God's power

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that even harmful creations have wisdom: they serve as trials and tangible reminders of the consequences of our actions.

When you face a trial, do you tend to focus only on the physical or emotional discomfort, or do you engage your mind in understanding its purpose? What concrete step can you take today to shift from mere endurance to active reflection?

First Wisdom: The Purpose of Harmful Things
Maturidi states the first wisdom behind creating harmful substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's reasoning that harmful and beneficial things are created to serve as tangible examples for understanding reward and punishment.

Core Idea
Harmful things have a purpose: to teach us about reward and punishment.

Maturidi begins his discussion of divine wisdom by addressing a common question: why do harmful things like snakes exist? He acknowledges that human intellect cannot fully grasp divine wisdom, but he identifies one clear purpose: these things serve as means of trial (miḥna) and help us conceive the reality of reward and punishment through tangible experiences. This passage lays the foundation for understanding how creation teaches us about the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to get the flow of Maturidi's argument. Note his acknowledgment of human limitation and the specific wisdom he presents.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify key terms
Look for the terms 'ḥikma' (wisdom), 'miḥna' (trial), 'thawāb' (reward), and 'ʿiqāb' (punishment). Understanding these will help grasp the argument.
These terms are central to the lesson.
3
Understand the logical argument
Trace how Maturidi connects harmful/beneficial things to reward and punishment. He argues that tangible examples help humans, who naturally consider consequences, conceive of the afterlife.
4
Reflect on the pedagogical purpose
Consider why tangible examples are necessary. How does experiencing harm and benefit in this life prepare us for the promised rewards and punishments?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : الحكمة في خلق الحيات والجواهر الضارة وإن كانت العقول تقصر عن بلوغ كنه حكمة الربوبية، على ما سبق القول فى لزوم الحكمة لكل شيء من الوجه الذى خلقه الله، وإن لم يعرف مائيتها يكون من وجوه المحنة بالضار والنافع الحاضرين، ليعلم بهما لذة الثواب على الطاعة وألم العقاب على المعصية؛ إذ الخلق جبلوا على قصد العواقب في الأفعال، فيعل لها مثالا من العيان لتتصور الموعود في الأوهام، فيسهل به السبيل، والله الموفق.
The jurist Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The wisdom in creating snakes and harmful substances—even if intellects fall short of attaining the depth of the wisdom of Lordship, based on what preceded regarding the necessity of wisdom for everything from the aspect in which Allah created it—and even if one does not know its quiddity, it is among the aspects of trial by the present harmful and beneficial, so that by them the pleasure of reward for obedience and the pain of punishment for disobedience may be known; since creatures are naturally disposed to seek consequences in actions, He made for them an example from direct experience so that the promised may be conceived in imaginations, thereby facilitating the path. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 707

Commentary
This passage is a statement of wisdom (ḥikma) regarding the creation of harmful things like snakes. Maturidi begins by acknowledging that human intellects cannot fully comprehend the divine wisdom, but based on the principle that everything has wisdom from the aspect of its creation, he identifies one aspect: trial (miḥna) through present harmful and beneficial things. The purpose is to teach the pleasure of reward for obedience and the pain of punishment for disobedience. Since humans are naturally inclined to consider consequences of actions, God provided tangible examples (from direct experience) so that the promised (al-maw‘ūd) reward and punishment can be conceived in imagination, thus making the path easier. This is the first of several wisdoms.

Second Wisdom: Trial Through Reflection and the Analogy of Enemies and Friends
Maturidi explains the second wisdom: trial through difficulty of reflection and training through enemies and friends analogues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi explains the second wisdom: that the trial of reflection trains humans to deal with enemies and friends through created analogues.

Core Idea
Harm and benefit are training grounds for spiritual friendship and enmity.

Maturidi continues his explanation of divine wisdom by examining the role of reflection and thought in human trial. He points out that these intellectual efforts are burdensome and lack immediate pleasure, leading people to vary in their diligence. Yet, this very difficulty serves a purpose: God created harmful and beneficial things as analogues for enemies and friends, training believers in caution, readiness, and support. This lesson unpacks that profound analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi describes the difficulty of reflection and thought.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two analogues
Find where Maturidi says God created 'enemies' and 'friends' analogues. Understand what harms and benefits represent.
3
Understand the training purpose
Consider how encountering these analogues trains humans in caution and readiness for real enemies and friends.
This is the core of the wisdom.
4
Connect to child training example
Notice the analogy with training children gradually. How does this illustrate the same principle?
Maturidi uses this to show the mercy of gradual habituation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: أن المحنة تحمل المؤنة التي تسهل، وتصعب على البدن بالنظر والفكر، والناس فى تكلف النظر والفكر يختلفون؛ لأنه ليست لهما منفعة حاضرة، وبهما الشغل عن اللذات والشهوات، وتحمل مثله على البدن عسير، وفى التقصير فيها اختلاف وتفرق، وذلك يعقب المعاداة والمجادلة، وفى الموافقة مولاة ومسالمة، فجعل الله تعالى لهم فيها خلق لهم شبيه الأعداء بما فيها من المضار، ومثال الأولياء بما فيها من المنافع، ليكون بشرهم زاجرا لهم على اعتياد كيفية معاملات الأعداء والأولياء، حتى إذا بلغوا بمثلهم في جوهرهم عرفوا كيفيته من الحذر والتأهب والمعونة والنصر. وعلى ذلك يؤمر الصبيان عند احتمال وسعهم للاعتياد ليسهل سبيل ذلك عليهم وقت التكليف، فمثله في خلق ما ذكر، والله أعلم.
And the second: that the trial bears the burden that is easy, and is difficult for the body by reflection and thought, and people differ in undertaking reflection and thought; because they have no immediate benefit, and they occupy one from pleasures and desires, and bearing the like on the body is difficult, and in falling short in them there is difference and division, and that leads to enmity and argumentation, while in agreement there is friendship and peace. So Allah, exalted, made for them in them a creation resembling enemies by what is in them of harms, and a likeness of friends by what is in them of benefits, so that their warning may deter them from accustoming the manner of dealing with enemies and friends, until when they reach their likeness in their substance, they know its manner of caution, readiness, aid, and victory. And similarly, children are commanded when their capacity for habituation is probable, to facilitate that path for them at the time of obligation. So the like is in the creation of what was mentioned. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 707

Commentary
This passage is the second wisdom (al-thānī) in the series. Maturidi explains that the trial (miḥna) involves the burden of reflection and thought, which is difficult for the body and lacks immediate benefit. People vary in undertaking it, and it distracts from pleasures. Failure in reflection leads to division and enmity, while agreement leads to friendship. Therefore, God created harmful things resembling enemies and beneficial things resembling friends, so that the warning they provide trains humans in dealing with actual enemies and friends. The goal is that when they encounter similar substances (jawhar), they know how to be cautious, prepared, seek aid, and achieve victory. Maturidi draws an analogy with training children gradually for the time of religious obligation. This wisdom emphasizes the educational and preparatory function of the created world.

Key Terms
المحنة
النظر
الفكر
الأعداء
الأولياء
الاعتياد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Harmful things are purely punitive and have no constructive purpose.
Correction: Maturidi argues that harmful and beneficial things are created as analogues to train humans in dealing with real enemies and friends.

Checkpoint
According to the second wisdom, why did God create harmful and beneficial things?
A) To punish sinners
B) To serve as analogues for enemies and friends to train humans in proper conduct
C) To provide food and medicine
D) To test human patience

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's second wisdom reveals that the challenges of reflection and the presence of harm and benefit are divinely designed to train humans in proper conduct toward enemies and friends.

?
When you face a painful loss or hardship, do you instinctively see it as punishment, or can you recognize it as Allah training you to deal with enemies? How will you change your perspective starting today?
?
Think of a recent blessing—a friend, health, or provision. Did you view it as a mere favor, or as a likeness of a friend teaching you how to be a better friend to Allah and His creation? What specific lesson did you learn, and what will you do with it?
?
Reflection and thought are burdensome; you have likely avoided deep thinking about your faith, your sins, or your purpose because it's hard. What is one area you have been neglecting, and how will you embrace that burden this week as training for your soul?

Division and Refutation of the Dahriyya
Maturidi divides the Dahriyya into three groups and refutes their inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi classifies the Dahriyya into three groups and exposes the logical contradiction in the third group's claim that an eternal state can change.

Core Idea
The Dahriyya's own theories of eternal principles are logically incoherent.

Maturidi confronts the materialists (Dahriyya) who deny a Creator. He first sorts them into three camps based on how they view the relationship between the eternal principles of separation and combination. Then he zeroes in on the third group's self-contradiction, showing that if an eternal state can change, then the distinction between eternal and originated collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the three groups
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the three categories of Dahriyya that Maturidi identifies.
The passage uses terms like تباين (separation) and اجتماع (combination).
2
Identify each group's view
Match each group to its belief: Group 1 holds separation then combination; Group 2 holds combination then separation; Group 3 holds both are pre-eternal.
Group 3 includes those who say individual entities (a'yān) are pre-eternal with beginningless accidents.
3
Focus on the third group's claim
Pay attention to the third group's claim that an eternal state (separation or combination) can change while the essence remains. Maturidi finds this contradictory.
4
Understand the refutation
Maturidi argues that if an eternal state ceases, its cause must also cease, which is absurd. This would allow the eternal to become originated.
This refutation undermines any theory that posits eternal principles that undergo change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ربيع اختلاف الدهرية إلى ثلاثة ، إلى تباين ثم الاجتماع ، وذلك [قول] الزنادقة والثنوية ومن يقول بالنور والظلمة ، وإلى اجتماع ثم التباين ، وذلك قول من يقول بالطينة والهيولى مع الجهل بهما على القبول بالقدم ، ويشبه أن يكون هذا قول أصحاب الطبائع ، [على] أنه لم يظهر ذلك من قولهم ، ثم بقدم التفرق [ أو الاجتماع ويرون ما عليه العالم عليهما ، وعلى ذلك قول من يقول بقدم الأعيان مع حوادث لا أول ما . وقول المفرق بين الحالين ظاهر التناقض ؛ لأنه أوجب أحد الوجهين لنفسه من التباين أو الاجتماع ؛ إذ ذلك وصفه بالقدم ، ثم ذهب عنه ذلك من غير ذهاب نفسه ، فبطل ما كان عليه السبب الذي به كان ، وذلك وجود علة ايجاد الشيء في حال ارتفاعه ، وذلك فاسد في العقل لو جاز ذا الجاز أن يصير القديم حديثا والحديث قديما ، وفى ذلك بطلان ما لو جاز وجود ما ثبت بنفسه زائلا وما زال بنفسه ثابتا الجاز مجود
Then [he explains] the division of the Dahriyya into three: [one group holds] separation then combination, and that is the statement of the Zandaqa and the Dualists and those who say [creation is from] light and darkness. [Another group holds] combination then separation, and that is the statement of those who say [creation is from] clay (ṭīna) and hyle (hayūlā) with ignorance of them both upon acceptance of pre-eternity. It resembles that this is the statement of the proponents of natures, though that has not appeared from their statement. Then [there is the group that says] the pre-eternity of separation or combination and they consider the world as it is to be based on both. Upon that is the statement of those who say that individual entities (a'yān) are pre-eternal with accidents that have no first. And the statement of the one who separates between the two states is clearly contradictory; because he necessitated one of the two aspects for itself, of separation or combination, since that is a description of its pre-eternity, then it left that without its own self leaving. So what it was upon, the cause by which it was, became nullified. That is the existence of the cause of bringing a thing into existence at the moment of its removal, and that is intellectually corrupt. If this were possible, then it would be possible for the pre-eternal to become originated and the originated to become pre-eternal. In that is a corruption: if it were possible for what is established by itself to cease and what has never ceased by itself to persist, then existence would be...
Source pages p. 720

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation of the Dahriyya (materialists who deny a Creator). Maturidi divides them into three groups based on their view of the relationship between the eternal principles: (1) those who hold that there was first separation (tabāyun) then combination (ijtimā')—these are the Zandaqa (Manichaeans) and Dualists who posit light and darkness; (2) those who hold first combination then separation—these are the proponents of clay and hyle, who also claim these are eternal; (3) those who hold that both separation and combination are pre-eternal and that the world as we see it results from both—these are the ones who believe in pre-eternal individual entities (a'yān) with beginningless accidents. After classifying them, Maturidi focuses on the contradiction inherent in the third group's position: they claim that one of the two states (separation or combination) is eternal, but then that state changes without the essence itself changing. This implies that the cause (the eternal state) ceases while the effect (the thing itself) remains, which is absurd. If such a change were possible, then the eternal could become temporal and vice versa, completely undermining any logical distinction between the two. The argument aims to show that any theory positing eternal principles that undergo change leads to intellectual corruption.

دهرية
تباين
اجتماع
زنادقة
ثنوية
نور
ظلمة
طينة
هيولى
أعيان
حوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Dahriyya (materialists) hold the same view.
Correction: Maturidi shows at least three distinct views among them, each with its own logical flaw. The third group's view is specifically refuted.

Checkpoint
What logical contradiction does Maturidi identify in the third group of Dahriyya?
A) They claim that God is both creator and judge.
B) They affirm that an eternal state can change while the essence remains the same.
C) They deny the existence of accidents.
D) They believe in a single eternity without change.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that any theory positing eternal principles that undergo change is self-contradictory, because the eternal cannot change without ceasing to be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you hold any worldview or belief that, upon honest examination, contains a hidden contradiction like the Dahriyya? Will you set aside time this week to identify one such belief and test its consistency?
?
This lesson shows that claiming something eternal can change leads to absurdity. How does this reality affect your confidence in the Islamic understanding of Allah’s unchanging attributes? What change must you make in your heart to align with this truth?
?
If you were to explain to a friend why 'eternal principles that change' is impossible, would your own life reflect that conviction? Commit to sharing this logical argument with one person this week, and let it deepen your reliance on the Creator who alone is eternal and unchanging.
