Maturidi's Refutation of Composition by Nature
Maturidi refutes the idea that composition/separation is by nature

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that composition and separation cannot be by nature because it implies invariability, which contradicts observable reality.

Core Idea
If things were separate or joined by nature, they could never change — but they do.

Maturidi tackles a key claim of the Dahriyya (materialists) that physical states like mixing and separating are inherent properties of substances. He offers a trilemma: these states must be due to nature, choice, or an external cause. In this passage, he tests the 'by nature' option and shows it leads to contradiction. By following his reasoning, we learn why nature alone cannot account for the variety we see.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing, then read the English translation. Get a sense of the author's argumentative flow.
Notice the trilemma at the beginning: nature, choice, or external cause.
2
Identify the three possibilities
Maturidi lists three possible causes for composition/separation: nature (طبع), choice (اختيار), or an external agent (آخر). Understand each category.
3
Examine the 'by nature' case
Focus on Maturidi's reasoning against nature: if something is separate or composite by nature, its state would be constant and even increase. Why would it increase? Because natural properties intensify.
He gives examples: natural movers move more; things that rise naturally stay above; things that sink stay below.
4
Consider the counterexample
Maturidi points out that we observe variation: something moving right can later move left, etc. This contradicts the constancy required if states were by nature.
Thus, the Dahriyya position is invalid.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: why is this argument effective? It shows that the 'by nature' hypothesis cannot account for change, so composition/separation must be due to choice or an external cause.
This connects to broader kalam arguments about contingency and the need for a Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: والأصل في ذلك أنهما عند التباين لا يعدو إما أن كانا كذلك بالطبع أو بالاختيار أو بآخر يجعلها كذلك، وكذلك المجتمع منه، ثم التباين والامتزاج لا يعدوان ما ذكرنا، فإن كانا كذلك بالطبع لوجب ذلك فما كان أصله التباين أو الاجتماع أن يزداد منه. ألا كل متحرك بالطبع يزداد بحركته وكذلك يستحق، وكذلك كل جوهر بطبعه يعلو وفوق، ومن بطبعه يسفل في حال الاجتماع أبدا، وكذا هذه العبرة. بين من يتحرك من جهة اليمين مع الذي يتحرك إلى اليسار، وفي ذلك بطلان ما قالوا.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The principle in this is that they (the two states of separation and composition) do not exceed either being that way by nature, or by choice, or by something else that makes them so. Likewise for composition. Then separation and mixing do not exceed what we have mentioned. If they were so by nature, then it would be necessary that what originally had separation or composition would increase from it. Indeed, everything that moves by nature increases in its movement and likewise deserves, and likewise every substance by its nature rises and is above, and that which by nature sinks is always in a state of composition. And so is this analogy between one who moves from the right side with one who moves to the left. In that is the invalidity of what they said.
Source pages p. 721

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation argument. Maturidi lays out three possibilities for why things are separate or composite: by nature (طبع), by choice (اختيار), or by an external cause (آخر). Here he examines the first possibility: if it is by nature, then a thing's essential property would be constant and would increase over time (e.g., a natural mover moves more). But we observe that substances rise or sink differently depending on context, so the claim that composition/separation is by nature is false. He uses examples: natural movers increase movement, rising substances stay up, sinking substances stay down. But in reality, things can change their state, contradicting the necessity of natural constancy. Thus, the Dahriyya position is invalid.

Key Terms
طبع
اختيار
جوهر
متحرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi denies that things have any natural properties at all.
Correction: Maturidi does not deny natural properties; he argues that specific states of composition/separation cannot be purely natural because they are not constant.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue against the possibility of composition/separation being by nature?
A) Nature is not a real cause
B) It would require things to always stay the same, which they do not
C) Nature is always for the better
D) He accepts it and moves on

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument shows that nature cannot be the sole cause of composition/separation because it would require unchanging constancy, which contradicts observable variability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life have you assumed something is impossible to change because it is “natural” for you? How does Maturidi’s argument—that natural properties alone cannot explain variability—challenge that assumption and call you to seek a higher cause?
?
When you observe inconsistency in your own actions (e.g., sometimes patient, sometimes impatient), do you attribute it to your “nature” or to your choice? If nature is not the sole cause, then what does that imply about your responsibility to change?
?
The argument shows that variability proves nature is not the determinant. If Allah (choice) and external agents play a role, how does this affect your reliance on your own habits versus turning to Allah for transformation?

Refuting Composition by Choice
Maturidi refutes composition by choice using logical contradiction and the prerequisites of agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the states of composition and separation cannot be caused by the choices of the substances themselves, because that would lead to logical contradictions and imply ignorance or incapacity.

Core Idea
If substances chose their states, we would see contradictions; true choice requires power and knowledge.

Maturidi continues his analysis of how composition and separation occur. Having dismissed necessity, he now considers the possibility that it happens by choice (ikhtiyar). He argues that this too is impossible, because if each substance had free choice, we would expect to see contradictory outcomes, yet the world exhibits consistent patterns. Moreover, true choice requires the agent to have power and knowledge of its action, but if both agents had that, they would oppose each other, leading to impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall that Maturidi is examining how composition and separation happen. Here he rejects the idea that it is by the choice of the substances themselves.
This argument is a direct refutation of a specific claim.
2
Follow the contradiction argument
Notice that if each substance chose its state, we would expect to see cases where a separating substance chooses to be together, or vice versa. Since we do not observe such contradictions, choice cannot be the cause.
The observed consistency of the world is key.
3
Examine the power and knowledge requirement
Maturidi then deepens the argument: true choice requires the agent to have power (qudra) and knowledge (‘ilm). If either is lacking, choice is meaningless and the agent is ignorant or incapable. But if both have full power, they would oppose each other, making composition or separation impossible.
This shows the logical necessity of power and knowledge for valid choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان ذلك بالاختيار فالقول بأن كانا على غير ما عليهما فاسد؛ لأنه لا دليل على تثبت خلاف لما عليه الشاهد: أن يكون الذي اختياره التباين يقع اجتماع، أو الذي اختياره الاجتماع يقع معه تباين، فبطل الاختيار فساد فيلم من بقاء كل جوهر الآخر واحتباسه، وتحقيق ذلك أن احتباس الخير مع الشر ولو كان لهما الاختيار لكان لا يخلو كل واحد منها من القدرة على فعله واختيار ذلك والعالم بكيفية ذلك، فإن لم يكن بطل معنى الاختيار وتحقق فيها جميعا العجز والجهل، وإن كان ذلك كذلك بطل الاختلاف عما كانا عليها، لما به يصل كل إلى ما يؤديه ويضره.
And if that were by choice, then the statement that they are other than what they are is corrupt, because there is no proof for establishing the opposite of what is witnessed: that one whose choice is separation should encounter composition, or that one whose choice is composition should encounter separation. Thus choice is invalidated, corruption results from the persistence of each substance over the other and its retention. The verification of that is that the retention of good with evil – even if they had choice, each of them would not be lacking the power to do its act and choose that and knowledge of its modality. If not, then the meaning of choice is invalidated and in all of them incapacity and ignorance are realized. And if that is so, then difference from what they were is invalidated, because each would attain what benefits and harms it.
Source pages p. 722

Commentary
This passage refutes the second possibility: that composition and separation occur by choice (اختيار). Maturidi argues that if agents had free choice, they could act contrary to their nature (e.g., a separating agent could choose to be together). But then we would witness contradictions, which we do not. If an agent has choice, it must have power and knowledge to execute its choice. If it lacked power or knowledge, then choice would be meaningless, and the agent would be ignorant or incapable. But if both agents had full power, they would oppose each other, leading to impossibility. Thus choice cannot explain the actual states of composition and separation. The argument relies on the observed consistency in the world.

Key Terms
اختيار
قدرة
عجز
جهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Maturidi is denying free will altogether or that substances have no causal efficacy.
Correction: Maturidi is not denying free will in general; he is specifically arguing that the consistent states of composition and separation cannot be explained by the choices of the substances themselves, because that would lead to logical absurdities. He does not address human free will here.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say would invalidate choice?
A) Lack of power or knowledge
B) Contradictory results
C) Both agents are good
D) The world is eternal

Key Takeaway
Choice cannot explain the consistency of composition and separation, because true choice would require power and knowledge that would lead to conflict.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your day, do you ever act as if outcomes are entirely in your hands? Can you identify one area where you need to acknowledge that your power and knowledge are limited, and that true control belongs to God?
?
Think about a time you tried to 'force' a good outcome by your own effort alone, without relying on God. How did that experience reveal your own incapacity and ignorance? What would it look like to trust God's wisdom instead?
?
If even two blobs of matter cannot both be right when they have conflicting ‘choices,’ how much more must you humble yourself before the One whose power and knowledge are absolute? Will you let this truth reshape how you view your own decision-making today?

Ibn Shabib on Eternal Contradiction
Ibn Shabib argues that eternal contradiction is impossible; change proves a temporal originator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Shabib uses the impossibility of eternal contradiction to argue that the world must have a temporal originator.

Core Idea
Eternal contradiction cannot sustain existence; change demands a Creator.

This lesson examines a powerful argument from Muhammad ibn Shabib, an early Islamic theologian. He asks: if the world were eternal and filled with inherent contradictions, could it persist? By analyzing a clever analogy about a house, Ibn Shabib demonstrates that such a world could not exist unless it was brought into being by an external agent. This reasoning challenges the materialist view that the world is eternal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core problem: Eternal contradiction
The passage starts with the idea that if something has opposition and contradiction inherently and eternally, its existence would be impossible. Notice the key terms 'تضاد' (opposition) and 'تناقض' (contradiction).
These terms are central to the argument.
2
Analyze the house analogy
Ibn Shabib provides an example: 'No one enters this house until another enters it.' This creates an infinite regress—entry never happens. Similarly, if the world has contradictory natures, it could never come to be.
The condition is unfulfillable.
3
Explore the second possibility: choice
If the world could choose to change its nature, it could choose annihilation. But if it is eternally enduring by nature, it cannot change. So both options (eternal contradiction and choice) fail.
This shows the only remaining option is origination.
4
Draw the conclusion
When both possibilities are invalidated, it proves that the world originated after not existing, created by a Maker. This refutes the idea of an eternal universe.
The phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله' ends the passage as a pious reminder.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال محمد بن شبيب في ذلك بما كان معناه عندنا إنه إذ لا يخلو القائم على ما عليه من التضاد والتناقض من أن يكون كذلك أبدا، فيبطل كونه، من حيث ذلك فيكون لا يتوهم، مع كون شيء من الجملة إلا أن يكون شيء هو فيها كل كائن منها المانع لكونه، فيبطل، كمن يقول: لا يدخل أحد هذه الدار حتى يدخلها غيره، إنها لا تحتمل دخول أحد فيها على وفاء الشرط، أو إن كان عن تباين قد تقدم، فيبطل الوجود للتضاد؛ إذ حقه التنافر بما تضادا بالطبع، ولو احتمل الخروج عن طبعها الذي فيه التضاد - بالاختيار جاز اختيار الفناء له في نفسه، وإن كان هو بطبعه باق. وإذا بطل الوجهان ثبت أنه كان بعد أن لم يكن بمن أحدثه كذلك على ما فيه الاختلاف والاتفاق، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Muhammad ibn Shabib said concerning that – as its meaning according to us is: since the subsisting thing upon what it is of opposition and contradiction is not free from being that way forever, then its existence is invalidated. By that, it would not be imagined, while there is something from the whole except that something is in it – every being from it is the preventer of its existence, so it is invalidated. Like one who says: 'No one enters this house until another enters it' – it cannot admit anyone entering it upon fulfillment of the condition. Or if it is from separation that has preceded, then existence is invalidated due to contradiction, since its right is repulsion due to what contradicts by nature. And if it were possible to exit from its nature which contains contradiction – by choice, it would be permissible to choose annihilation for itself while it is by nature enduring. And when the two faces are invalidated, it is established that it came to be after not being, by one who originated it, upon what is in it of difference and agreement. And no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 722

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of Muhammad ibn Shabib, an early Mu'tazili scholar. He reasons that if something (the world) were eternal and characterized by inherent contradiction or opposition (تضاد), it could not persist. The example: 'No one enters the house until another enters it' is a condition that can never be fulfilled because the first person cannot enter, so entry is impossible. Similarly, if the world has contradictory properties like separation and composition, its existence would be impossible if those are eternal. If it is by nature contradictory, it would repel itself. If it could change by choice, it could choose annihilation. Since both options fail, the world must be originated (hadith) by a creator, with the differences and agreements we observe. This reinforces the conclusion against the Dahriyya.

Key Terms
تضاد
تناقض
مانع
فناء
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Shabib's argument is that contradiction itself is impossible.
Correction: Actually, he argues that eternal contradiction would make existence impossible; contradiction may exist in temporal things.

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Shabib's example of the house entrance illustrate?
A) The need for a key
B) The impossibility of fulfilling a condition when the first action never occurs
C) The importance of timing
D) The role of choice

Key Takeaway
Ibn Shabib demonstrates that eternal contradiction is impossible, thus the world must have been created by a Maker.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Shabib argues that if a thing is eternally contradictory, it cannot exist. What contradictions in your own life—patterns, habits, loyalties—are you trying to sustain eternally instead of acknowledging they must be resolved or ended? What would it mean to admit that they cannot last?
?
The analogy of the house shows that impossible conditions prevent anything from happening. What 'conditions' have you set for yourself (e.g., 'I will repent after I achieve X' or 'I will believe when I see Y') that are actually preventing you from turning to the Creator? Are you willing to drop those conditions now?
?
The argument concludes that the world's existence requires a Creator. If you truly internalize that your own existence is not self-sustaining but constantly dependent on Allah, what specific aspect of your daily life would you need to reorient—prayer, trust, or fear? When will you take that step?

Proof of a Creator and Refutation of the Benefit Objection
Maturidi provides three proofs for a creator and responds to the benefit objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational arguments for a creator and how Maturidi rebuts the objection that a creator would consider benefit.

Core Idea
Three rational proofs establish a creator; benefit is not a limiting factor for divine action.

Maturidi begins by establishing that the world cannot arise without a creator, presenting three clear arguments. He then anticipates an objection: if a self-existing entity would not need benefit, why would an other-existent entity require benefit? He resolves this by distinguishing between what exists by itself and what exists through another.

Guided Path
1
Read the three arguments for a creator
Carefully read the first part of the passage where Maturidi gives three proofs: possibility, form, and seasonal change. Note how each argument points to a necessary creator.
These arguments are foundational in kalam.
2
Understand the objection about timing and benefit
The objection claims that if a self-existent being is not subject to benefit, why would a dependent being be? Understand the logic of the objection.
The objection challenges the need for a creator to consider benefit.
3
Follow Maturidi's response
Maturidi distinguishes between what is by itself and what is by another. The response shows that benefit only applies to contingent beings, not to the creator.
This distinction is key to refuting the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا يجوز أن يحدث بلا محدث ؛ لما لا يكون العدم به والوجود إلا واحدا ، ولما لا يعرف صورة إلا من مصور ، ولما تغير الأوقات من شتاء وصيف ونحو ذلك ثبت أنه كان كذلك : فعورض بما لو كان فيما كان بنفسه يمنعه عن ذلك كونه في وقت دون وقت ، لم لا كان كذلك فيما كان بغيره ؟ فزعم أنه إذا كان بغيره تدبير كونه له لمصلحة فى الدين أو الدنيا ، وفيما كان لا بغيره ليس كذلك ، لذلك اختلف الأمران ، وهذا الذي يزعم يوجب أنه لا يجوز أن يجعل أول الخلق غير المستحن حتى يكون : في الذي ذكرنا ، وإذا جاز غيره بلا مصلحة لذلك الوقت دون غيره لا معنى لما قال . وقد بينا نحن القول بالخلق ، وإحالة السؤال عن ليم خلق ؟ ، وليس لنا أن نزعم أنه لا يفعل إلا الأصلح فيلزمه حق الفعل حتى يلحقه وصف ذم إن أخر أو قدم ، بل الله تعالى إذ هو حكيم لا يخرج فعله عن الحكمة ، وأما اعتبار الأصلح لغيره إنما تقدير الحق عليه لا تقدير الفعل بذاته، وسؤال كون الحق غيره عليه لا غير بل السؤال عن جملة الخلق ، فالقول فى أنه ينال النفع لهم أو صلاح لهم لا معنى له ؛ إذ ليس عليهم فيما لا يخلقهم ضرر ولا فساد ، فيكون الخلق لما ذكر ، والله أعلم .
Then it is not permissible for something to occur without an originator (muhdith), because non-existence and existence are the same with respect to it, and because no form is known except from a fashioner (musawwir), and because of the change of seasons from winter to summer and so on, it is established that it was thus. Then it was objected: if what is by itself prevents that due to its being in a time and not another, why would it not be the same for what is by another? And he claimed that when it is by another, its management of its existence is for a benefit (maslaha) in religion or the world, while in what is not by another it is not so, therefore the two matters differ. And this which he claims necessitates that it is not permissible to make the first of creation other than the approved [؟] so that it is as we mentioned. And if another is permissible without benefit for that time and not another, his saying has no meaning. And we have already stated the position on creation and the impossibility of the question 'why did He create?' and it is not for us to claim that He does only the most beneficial (al-aslah) so that the right of the action is necessitated for Him such that a description of blame attaches to Him if He delayed or advanced. Rather, God Almighty, since He is Wise (Hakim), His action does not depart from wisdom. As for considering the most beneficial for others, it is only an estimation of the right upon Him, not an estimation of the action by itself. And the question of the right being other than Him? No, rather the question is about the entirety of creation. So the saying that He bestows benefit for them or goodness for them has no meaning, because there is no harm or corruption upon them if He does not create them. So creation is for what was mentioned, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 723

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of a creator followed by a refutation of an objection. First, three arguments are presented: (1) a possible thing (al-'adam wa al-wujud) needs a determinant; (2) every form needs a fashioner; (3) seasonal changes require a changer. Then an objection is raised: if something by itself prevents being in a particular time, why would it not be the same for something by another? The response distinguishes between what is by itself (which is not subject to management for benefit) and what is by another (which is managed for benefit). The author then rejects the idea that God must do only the most beneficial, because that would impose an obligation on Him and lead to blame if He did otherwise. Instead, God is Wise, and His actions are based on wisdom, not the benefit of creatures. The question 'why created?' is invalid because there is no prior harm to creatures, and creation is a manifestation of wisdom.

Key Terms
محدث
مصلحة
بنفسه
بغيره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's actions must be motivated by benefit to creatures.
Correction: Maturidi argues that benefit is only considered for things that exist through another, but God is not bound by such considerations.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the arguments Maturidi uses for a creator?
A) Possibility requires a determinant
B) Form requires a fashioner
C) Seasons change requires a changer
D) Consensus of nations

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes the necessity of a creator through rational arguments and refutes the benefit objection by distinguishing between self-existent and other-existent beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on causes and effects daily. This passage argues that even the existence of things requires a Creator. When you last experienced a 'coincidence,' did you attribute it to chance or to the One who determines all things? How does this passage challenge your default mindset about causality?
?
The objection assumes God must act for benefit. Have you ever questioned why God allows hardship in your life? How does Maturidi's distinction between self-existent and other-existent help you trust that God's actions are not limited by your perception of benefit?
?
The three proofs in this passage are meant to settle certainty in your heart. Which of the three—equality of existence/non-existence, form requires a fashioner, or change of seasons—speaks most directly to a doubt you have faced? What will you do to let that argument take root in your conviction?

The Wisdom of Creation: Refuting the Obligation of the Most Beneficial
Maturidi refutes the doctrine of al-aslah and explains that creation is based on wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the notion that God must create for the most beneficial and why the question 'why create?' is invalid.

Core Idea
God is Wise, not obligated to create for benefit; the question 'why create?' has no basis.

After refuting the benefit objection, Maturidi addresses a deeper theological claim: that God must do what is most beneficial for creatures. He argues that this would impose an obligation on God and lead to blame if He did otherwise. Instead, God's actions are based on wisdom, not creature-benefit.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim that God must do the most beneficial
Some theologians argued that God is obligated to create for the benefit of creatures. Maturidi rejects this.
This claim is known as the doctrine of al-aslah.
2
See why Maturidi rejects it as imposing obligation
If God were obligated to do the most beneficial, He could be blamed for delay or advance. This is incompatible with divine perfection.
3
Grasp why the question 'why create?' is invalid
Since creatures did not exist prior to creation, there is no harm in not creating them. Therefore, asking for a purpose of benefit is meaningless.
The only purpose is wisdom inherent in God's action.

Objection to First Creation and the Infinite Time Response
Maturidi answers the objection that the first creation lacks benefit by denying a first moment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the question 'why did God not create earlier?' is invalid because time has no first moment, and that divine action is not bound by considerations of benefit or optimal timing.

Core Idea
No first creation exists because time is infinite, so objections based on timing collapse.

Maturidi tackles a deep objection: if God created for a purpose, why create at all? The first creation, being for Himself, seems purposeless. His response is subtle: there is no 'first' creation because time is infinite. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows why such objections fail against an eternal Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the objection and the response. The passage is dense; focus on the logical flow.
Some words may be uncertain due to OCR; try to grasp the overall argument.
2
Identify the objection
Find the part that says 'it was objected with the first creation He created for Himself, and there is no benefit there.' What is the underlying issue?
The objector assumes every act must have a benefit.
3
Understand the response about time
Maturidi says there is no first creation because time is infinite. Why does that dissolve the objection?
If there is no first moment, you cannot ask 'why not earlier?'
4
Grasp the rejection of 'most beneficial'
The author dismisses the concept of 'al-aslah' (most beneficial) as meaningless. Why?
God’s wisdom is not bound by human notions of optimal time or benefit.
5
Reflect on divine freedom and wisdom
The passage ends by affirming that God may act as He wills, provided it is wise. How does this settle the objection?
Lordship entails freedom from foolishness, not from creating for benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تم في الجملة لا يخار خلق من أن يكون للمتحن به نفع وعبره من طريق الاستدلال به والاعتبار ، سوى المنافع الآخر مما من الله عليهم بها ، وبالله التوفيق . وأصل صلاح العبد في الدين إنما هو يفعله ، وكذلك فساده ، والله تعالى بالأسباب التي بها ينال فعل الصلاح عليه أعظم المنن وأجزل النعم ، ومن فسد فهد الأغراض عن الله ، وايتاره شهرته على طاعته، خلى الله بينه وبين ما اختاره لنفسه إذ اثر هواه على أمره وشهوته على طاعته ، والفعل الذي بين له أنه فعل العداوة على ما هو العداوة على ما فعورض بأول خلق خلقه لنفسه ، وليس ثمة مصلحة ، فزعم أنه ليس ثمة أول ، وهو أصلح وقت ليقال فيه : لم لا خلق قبله ؟ وإنما ذلك متى يكون هو في التدبير وأولى بالحكمة وما هو كذلك ، فيخرج السؤال على أنه : لم لا خلق دونه فى الحكمة وحسن التدبير ؟ قال الشيخ رحمه الله : فما ذكرت من الوقت فهو ما يذكر ، على أن السؤال في مثله ساقط ؛ لأنه لا يشار إلى وقت ، وإلا لو كان الخلق قبل ذلك إلى ما لا يحتمل اللسان من عدد الأوقات ممكن ، وفى ذلك بطلان السؤال إلا عن قدمه ، وذلك تناقض ؛ لإحالة وقوع التكوين على الكائن فى القدم ، ولا قوة إلا بالله وما ذكر من الحكمة فذلك حق ، وما ذكر من الأصلح لا أدرى ما أراد به ، وما قال من دونه أو مثله فالقول به لا معنى له ، ولله تعالى أن يفعل الفعل ؛ إذ الخروج عنه يحقق السفه ، وذلك يسقط الربوبية الذي لا يخرج عن الحكمة
Then in summary, a creation is not chosen [؟] such that there be for the tested [؟] a benefit and a lesson by way of inference and consideration, apart from other benefits from what God has bestowed upon them, and with God is success. And the root of the servant's rectitude in religion is by his own action, and likewise his corruption. And God Almighty, through the means by which one attains the action of rectitude, upon Him are the greatest favors and the most abundant blessings. And whoever becomes corrupt, his aims [؟] turn away from God, and his preferring his reputation [؟] over his obedience, God leaves him between him and what he chose for himself, since he preferred his whim over His command and his desire over His obedience. And the action which is shown to him that it is an act of hostility [؟] upon what is hostility? So it was objected with the first creation He created for Himself, and there is no benefit there. So he claimed that there is no first, and it is a better time for it to be said: why was it not created before it? And that is only when it is in management and more worthy of wisdom and what is like that, so the question comes out as: why was it not created less than it in wisdom and good management? The shaykh (God have mercy on him) said: As for what you mentioned of time, it is as mentioned, but the question in such a case is invalid because no time is pointed to. Otherwise, if creation before that were possible to a number of times that the tongue cannot bear, then in that is the invalidity of the question except about its pre-eternity, and that is a contradiction, because occurrence of causation (takwin) upon the originated in pre-eternity is impossible. And there is no power except with God. And what was mentioned of wisdom is true, and what was mentioned of the most beneficial (al-aslah) I do not know what he meant by it. And what he said about less than it or like it, the statement has no meaning. And for God Almighty it is permissible to do the action, because leaving it would realize foolishness, and that negates lordship, which does not depart from wisdom.
Source pages p. 723

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. First, the author discusses that benefit in creation is for the tested, but the essential benefit comes from God. Then the objection: the first creation was for Himself without benefit. The response: there is no first creation because time is infinite; the question 'why not before?' is invalid because no specific time can be pointed to. If creation could have been earlier indefinitely, then the only meaningful question is about pre-eternity, which is impossible for originated things. Finally, the author rejects the notion of 'most beneficial' as meaningless; God's action is based on wisdom, and He is free to act as He wills as long as it is wise.

تكوين

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe God's first creation must have been at the best possible time for maximum benefit.
Correction: Maturidi argues there is no 'first' moment in time to prefer, and the concept of 'most beneficial' is meaningless; God's wisdom transcends human calculations.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the question 'why did God not create earlier?' invalid?
A) Because creation is eternal.
B) Because time is finite.
C) Because there is no specific time to point to; creation could have been earlier ad infinitum.
D) Because God's wisdom is unknown.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that objections based on timing or benefit are invalid when applied to an eternal Creator, because time has no beginning and God's wisdom is not limited by human concepts of optimization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does the truth that time has no beginning and God’s wisdom is beyond optimization change your perspective on that situation? What will you do today to surrender your timeline to Allah?
?
You often think that you deserve certain benefits from God based on your actions. The passage says the concept of 'most beneficial' is meaningless when applied to God. Are there areas where you feel entitled to specific blessings? How can you break that entitlement and instead trust that whatever Allah does is wise, even if it seems to lack benefit from your perspective? Commit to one area where you will stop judging God’s treatment of you.
?
The objection in the passage assumes that God’s actions must have a benefit for humans. But the response shows that God acts for His own wisdom. When you experience trials that seem pointless, do you complain or trust? What needs to shift in your heart to accept that His wisdom is not for you to understand? Write a sincere du‘a asking for that trust.

Creation from Nothing: Maturidi's Refutation of the Mu'tazila
Maturidi answers whether creation is from something and refutes the Mu'tazila view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument for creation ex nihilo and how he distinguishes it from the Mu'tazila doctrine of pre-existent essences.

Core Idea
Creation from nothing means God originates things without any pre-existing matter or essence.

This passage tackles a foundational question in Islamic theology: Did God create the universe from something or from nothing? Maturidi affirms creation ex nihilo, but then contrasts his view with a subtle Mu'tazila alternative that, he argues, undermines true creatorship. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping how different schools protect God's absolute sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the flow: a question, an answer, a clarification, then a doctrinal critique.
The passage uses technical terms like 'شَيْئِيَّة' (thingness).
2
Identify the two positions
Identify the view Maturidi affirms (creation 'not from something') and the Mu'tazila position he refutes (things have thingness before creation).
3
Understand the critique
See why Maturidi likens the Mu'tazila view to the Dahriyya (materialists). What is the similarity?
The key is that the Mu'tazila, like the Dahriyya, imply that things have a kind of pre-existence.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you grasp the main contrast.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أجاب من سأل : أمن شيء خلق الأشياء أو من لا شيء ؟ فقال : لا من شيء ، معناه أن اخترع الأشياء أى ابتدعها من غير أصل، وهذا فما أخبر من حدث الأجسام . لكن مذهب المعتزلة أن شيئية الأشياء لم يكن بالله بل كان به وجردها، فيكون على قولهم خلق الأشياء لا من شيء شال، بل لم يخلق الأشياء لكنه أوجد أعيانها عن العالم، وهن في العدم أشياء، وذلك من مضاهات الدهرية والحمد لله الذي عصمنا ذلك عن
Then he answered the one who asked: 'From something did He create things, or from nothing?' He said: 'Not from something.' Its meaning is that He invented things, i.e., He originated them without a basis. And this is what is reported from the occurrence of bodies. However, the school of the Mu'tazila is that the thingness of things was not through Allah, but rather they were in some way and He brought them forth. Therefore, according to their saying, the creation of things is 'not from something' — rather, He did not create things, but He brought their essences into existence from the world, and they in non-existence are things. And that is of the imitation of the Dahriyya (materialists). And praise be to Allah who protected us from that.
Source pages p. 725

Commentary
This passage answers the question whether God created from pre-existing matter or from nothing. Maturidi affirms that creation is ex nihilo: 'not from something' means He originated things without any pre-existing material. He contrasts this with the Mu'tazila position, which holds that the 'thingness' (shay'iyya) of things exists independently in non-existence, and God merely brings them into existence. According to Maturidi, this effectively means God did not create the essences but only actualized them, implying that they were pre-existing 'things' in a state of non-existence. He considers this view to be similar to the Dahriyya (materialists) who believe in eternal matter. Maturidi praises Allah for protecting Muslims from that error.

Key Terms
خلق من لا شيء
شيئية الأشياء
المعتزلة
الدهرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila view on creation is essentially the same as the Sunni view that God creates from nothing.
Correction: The Mu'tazila believe that things have a pre-existent 'thingness' in non-existence, so God does not create their essences but only brings them into existence. This contradicts the Sunni doctrine of creation ex nihilo.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi consider the Mu'tazila view similar to the Dahriyya (materialists)?
A) Both believe in creation ex nihilo
B) Both affirm that things were pre-existent in some form before creation
C) Both deny the existence of God
D) Both believe in atoms

Key Takeaway
Maturidi affirms that creation is ex nihilo and warns that any belief in pre-existent essences, like that of the Mu'tazila, undermines God's role as the sole Creator and mirrors the materialist view of eternal matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if every atom of your existence—your breath, your health, your mind—was brought into being from absolute nothingness by God's command, or do you sometimes feel these things just 'are'? When you credit your own skills or opportunities, does part of you assume they have an independent reality, like the Mu'tazila's 'thingness'? This attitude robs God of His exclusive, ex nihilo creativity. Decide today to recite 'Allāhu khāliqu kulli shayʾ' on your lips and in your heart whenever you feel self-sufficiency.
?
This passage warns that the Mu'tazila view slides into the imitation of the Dahriyya—those who believed matter is eternal. Does your daily life reflect the belief that nothing existed before God's creation, or do you harbour subtle assumptions that some things (your character, the universe, 'human nature') have always been there? If so, you are unknowingly echoing a kufr-like thought. Ask Allah to purify your tawḥīd from every residue of pre‑existence.
?
Maturidi teaches that creation from nothing is a direct sign of God's complete sovereignty. Examine your prayer's intensity: do you approach Allah as the absolute Originator who owes nothing to any prior cause? Or do you treat Him as a 'manager' of pre‑existing realities? Your answer determines whether you worship the Creator or a demiurge. Commit to performing a sajda every day with the specific thought that you and all you see are brought from non‑existence, here and now.

Motion is Known by Necessity of Transition
Maturidi proves motion is known through sensed transition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi proves that motion is an inferred attribute known through the necessity of a body's transition, not through direct sense perception.

Core Idea
Motion is not sensed; it is inferred from the difference in a body's states.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses a fundamental question: how do we know motion exists? He argues that we do not directly see motion as a separate entity; rather, we observe a body in one place and then in another, and the necessity of change compels us to affirm motion as a real attribute. This lesson unpacks that logical inference and its implications for understanding attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then its literal English translation. Notice the key terms 'حركة' (motion) and 'ضرورة' (necessity).
The passage is dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the Core Argument
Maturidi claims motion is known 'by this necessity which manifested in the body's transition.' Trace how he moves from observed change of place to inferring motion.
He says we find a difference of state in the sensed object.
3
Understand the Nature of Motion
He clarifies that motion is not identical to the body nor completely separate—it is an attribute. Reflect on why this distinction matters.
Motion is not a body, but it inheres in a body.
4
Analyze the Zayd and Amr Analogy
The final sentence contrasts Zayd and Amr to show that motion is not intrinsic but attaches. Try to rephrase this reasoning in your own words.
This part is tricky; focus on the idea of entitlement.
5
Check Your Understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you grasp the main claim: motion is known by necessity, not sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذه الضرورة التي أظهرت في الجسم الانتقال علمنا الحركة التي لا تحس؛ إذ وجدنا اختلاف الحال في المحسوس وعرفنا باعتماد الشيء في المكان الأول وانتقاله في المكان الثاني أن المسمى اعتماده في الحال الأول يصير حركة ونقلة في الحالة الثانية مما لا يوصف الحركة بمناسبة الجسم ولا مباينته؛ إذ ذلك حق الجسم، ثم زيد أحق من عمرو بها لأن توهم عدم عمرو لانعدامها عن زيد إذا وجد هو في غير المكان الأول.
And by this necessity which manifested in the body's transition, we know motion which is not sensed; for we found a difference of state in the sensed object, and we knew by the thing's dependence on the first place and its transition to the second place that what is called its dependence in the first state becomes motion and transfer in the second state, such that motion is not described as similar to the body nor as distinct from it; for that is the attribute of the body. Then Zayd is more entitled to it than Amr because the imagining of the non-existence of Amr [implies] its non-existence from Zayd when he is in a place other than the first.
Source pages p. 737

Commentary
This passage is a proof that motion (حركة) is a distinct attribute (معنى) as indicated by the observer's inference. The author argues: we perceive a body in one place, then in another; the change of state necessitates something (motion) that is not itself sensed but is known by necessity (ضرورة). This motion is neither identical to the body nor completely separate; it is an attribute. The final sentence contrasts individuals (Zayd and Amr) to illustrate that motion is not an intrinsic property but attaches to the body.

Key Terms

ضرورة
جسم
محسوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is directly sensed through vision or touch.
Correction: Maturidi argues that motion itself is not sensed; we only sense the body's presence in successive places. Motion is known by logical necessity from the observed transition.

Checkpoint

A) By direct sensory perception.
B) By necessity from the body's transition.
C) By divine revelation.
D) By logical deduction without any empirical basis.

Key Takeaway
Motion is an attribute inferred by necessity from a body's transition between places, not a directly sensed entity.

?
The passage argues that motion is an attribute, not a body. Your own actions are not just physical movements; they carry moral and spiritual meaning. Will you commit to examining the inner attributes behind your outward deeds?
?
If motion is inferred from change, then every change points to something beyond itself. Look at one recent change in your life—a change in your situation, your relationships, or your heart. What unseen reality might that change be pointing you toward? Are you willing to follow that inference to its ultimate end, the One who alone is unchanging?

Objection and Answer on How Motion is Known
Maturidi rebuts the objection that motion's 'how' is unknown.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's response to the objection that we cannot know the 'how' of motion, and how he distinguishes between knowing temporal sequence and establishing otherness.

Core Idea
We know motion by its temporal relation, not by its essence.

In this passage, an objector challenges the argument for motion by saying we do not know the 'how' of the agent. Maturidi responds by clarifying that we only need to know the 'how' of temporal sequence (advancement and retardation), which we learn from our own acts. This allows us to establish the otherness (غيرية) of motion as an accident inhering in a body, without needing to know the essence of the agent. The passage ends with an editorial note about the brevity of the presentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's question carefully: since motion is an act, and we don't know the 'how' of the doer, how can we infer motion by analogies?
Notice the assumption that knowing 'how' means knowing the essence.
2
Understand the answer
Maturidi responds by distinguishing two meanings of 'how': one is the temporal sequence (تقدم وتأخر) known from our own acts, the other is the essence of otherness (غيرية). We only need the former to prove motion as an accident.
3
Trace the proof structure
The proof: if motion occurs and the body does not precede it, then motion is an accident (حدث) and therefore otherness (غيرية) is established. This refutes those who deny otherness.
Note the use of 'تقدم وتأخر' as the observable indicator.
4
Reflect on the editorial comment
The author notes that this is a valued expression of Ahl al-Tawhid, but the question and answer were lengthy, so he summarized it. Consider why this distinction is important for kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجاب المعارض له أن كيف هي؟ إذ هي فعلكم، ولم يعرف كيفية فعله قبله، فلم استدللتم على الحركة بالمقاييس؟ قيل: إنما يعرف أن كيف التقدم والتأخر الذي هو فعلنا، لا أن يُعرف غيريتها لنا، وإنما أقمنا الدلالة على الغيرية. ألا ترى أن قوما أنكروا الغيرية للجسم على إثبات القول بالتقدم والتأخر، ثم إذ ثبت حدث ما ذكر - والجسم لا يسبقه - ثبت حدوثه. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: وهذه عبارة لم يزل أهل التوحيد يعتزون بها، لكنه أطنب فيها السؤال والجواب، فذكرت ذلك على الإيماء إلى ما ذكر دون البسط.
And the opponent objected: 'How is it (motion)? For it is your act, and you did not know the how of its doer before it, so why did you infer motion by analogies?' It was said: 'One only knows the how of advancement and retardation which is our act, not that its otherness is known to us; we only established the indication of otherness. Do you not see that some people denied otherness for the body upon affirming the statement of advancement and retardation, then when what was mentioned occurred — and the body does not precede it — its occurrence was established.' The Shaykh — may Allah have mercy on him — said: 'This is an expression with which the people of tawhid have always taken pride, but he (the objector) lengthened the question and answer in it, so I mentioned that by way of allusion to what was mentioned without expansion.'
Source pages p. 737

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by the author's editorial remark. The objector asks: since motion is an act, and we do not know the 'how' of the agent (فاعل), how can we infer motion through analogies? The answer clarifies: we only know the 'how' of temporal sequence (تقدم وتأخر) from our own acts, not the essence of otherness. The proof for otherness (غيرية) is established by the fact that the body does not precede the motion; thus motion is an accident (حدث). The author (perhaps Maturidi or an editor) comments that this expression is valued by Ahl al-Tawhid, but the question-answer was too lengthy, so he summarizes it briefly.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That we need to know the essence (كيف) of motion to prove it exists.
Correction: Maturidi says we only need to know the temporal relation (تقدم وتأخر) to establish motion as an accident (حدث).

Checkpoint

A) He agrees and abandons the argument.

C) He claims motion is known by divine inspiration.
D) He argues that motion is a body.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi distinguishes between knowing the 'how' of temporal sequence and knowing the essence of otherness, establishing motion as an accident without requiring knowledge of the agent's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You have just read a defense of motion's temporality. But do you live as if the world is in constant renewal, each moment a new act of Allah? What would it look like to treat your own actions as dependent on His creation of motion in you?

Refuting Motion as a Body: The Interpenetration Argument
Maturidi refutes the claim that motion is a body via interpenetration absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi refutes the claim that motion is a body, and how he uses the concept of interpenetration (tadakhul) to show its absurdity.

Core Idea
Claiming motion is a body leads to interpenetration and infinite absurdities.

In this passage, Maturidi tackles a subtle objection about the nature of motion. Some argue that motion itself is a body. Maturidi responds by showing this leads to impossible consequences — most notably, the idea of bodies interpenetrating each other. He also critiques the premise that a body can be neither still nor moving in its initial state.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Identify the claim that motion is a body. Note that Maturidi first dismisses it for those who believe bodies are eternal, since motion is sensed as an accident.
2
Explore the interpenetration argument
Maturidi argues: if motion were a body, it would occupy space, requiring interpenetration or another motion. This leads to an infinite regress.
Interpenetration (tadakhul) means two bodies occupying the same space.
3
Consider the absurd illustration
Maturidi gives a vivid example: if interpenetration were possible, the entire world could fit inside an egg. This shows the absurdity of the claim.
4
Critique the opponent's premise
Maturidi points out that the opponent's assumption that a body can be neither still nor moving in its first state is flawed; it removes the very attribute that defines its state.
This premise contradicts the idea of a pre-eternal body having a 'first state'.
5
Reflect on the implications
Understand that this refutation defends the view that motion is an attribute (accident) of a body, not a substance itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عورض بالحركة إنها جسم، فقال: ذا لا يسأل عنه من يقول بقدم الجسم لأنها حدثت بالحس، وقال: للإحالة أن يكون في المكان الأول على التداخل، وفي المداخلة إيجاب حركة أخرى للانتقال فيكون غير جسم، مع لو جعلت الثانية متداخلة يلزم تداخل الأجسام بلا نهاية، ولو جاز ذا لجاز تداخل الدنيا في بيضة، ومثله أجاب في التلاقي، وذلك كله تطويل بلا نفع ولو أنصف لوجد ما يمنعه عن دليله وهو قوله: الجسم في أول حاله ليس بساكن ولا متحرك فأخلاه عما ذكر، وفي ذلك سبق عن الذي وصف، لكن من يقول بقدمه لا يثبت له حال الأولية؛ إذ في ذلك القول بحدثه، فلزم الذي وصف، والله الموفق.
Then it was objected that motion is a body. He said: 'This is not asked of one who says the body is pre-eternal (qadim), because it (motion) occurs by sense.' And he said: 'It is impossible (al-iḥāla) for it to be in the first place upon interpenetration (tadākhul), and in interpenetration is the necessitation of another motion for the transition, so it would be non-body. Also, if you made the second interpenetrating, it would necessitate interpenetration of bodies without end, and if that were permissible, then the penetration of the world into an egg would be permissible.' And similarly he answered regarding meeting (talāqī). All of that is lengthy without benefit; had he been fair, he would have found what prevents his proof, which is his statement: 'The body in its first state is neither still nor moving'—so he emptied it of what was mentioned, and in that is precedence over what he described. But whoever says it is pre-eternal does not establish for it a state of priority; for in that is the statement of its temporal origination. So what he described is necessary. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 737

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that motion itself is a body. The opponent claims motion is a body, but the answer points out that this leads to absurd interpenetration (تداخل). If motion were a body, then when a body moves, two bodies (the original body and the motion-body) would occupy the same place, implying infinite regress of interpenetrations. The author illustrates this absurdity: if one body can interpenetrate another, then the entire world could fit into an egg. He also critiques the opponent's premise that a body can be neither still nor moving in its first state, arguing that this removes the very attribute that defines its state, and that positing a pre-eternal body cannot have a 'first state' without implying temporal origination.

Key Terms
جسم
تداخل
حركة
قدم
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That motion is a substance (body) rather than an accident.
Correction: Maturidi shows that treating motion as a body leads to absurd interpenetration and infinite regress, proving it must be an accident.

Checkpoint
What absurdity does Maturidi present against the claim that motion is a body?
A) Motion would be eternal.
B) The world could fit in an egg.
C) Bodies would cease to exist.
D) Motion would not be sensed.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the claim that motion is a body by showing it leads to the absurdity of interpenetration and infinite regress, affirming motion as an accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that even a small logical contradiction (like interpenetration) can unravel an entire argument. Where in your own beliefs have you accepted contradictions for convenience? Are you willing to follow logic to its conclusion, even if it means abandoning a comfortable idea?
?
The absurdity of the world fitting into an egg is used to expose the flaw in the opponent's claim. What 'absurdities' do you tolerate in your life that, upon honest reflection, reveal deeper inconsistencies in your worldview? How can you repent from such mental laxity?

Proof that Rest is Distinct from the Body
Maturidi proves rest (sukūn) is a separate meaning (maʿnā).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi proves that rest (sukūn) is an accident distinct from the body, using the example of being in a house and the disappearance of rest during motion.

Core Idea
Rest is not the body—it is a separate meaning that comes and goes.

In this passage, Maturidi presents a clear argument that rest is not identical to the body. He uses everyday examples to show that the relation of 'being in' a place is a separate meaning, and that rest disappears when motion begins while the body remains. This lesson will walk through his reasoning and his response to a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to get the overall argument. Note that Maturidi uses a simple example of being in a house.
The passage has three parts: the initial argument, Maturidi's confirmation, and an objection and answer.
2
Identify the main argument
Look for the reasoning: if there were only body and house, the person would not be 'in' the house; the house exists but the person is not described as being in it. Also, rest disappears during motion while body remains.
3
Address the objection
The opponent says rest might accompany the body everywhere, but Maturidi points out that rest varies in duration, proving each instance is distinct.
This reinforces that rest is not inherent to the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدل على أن سكون الجسم معنى غير الجسم بما يقال: هو في دار كذا، لو لم يكن سوى الجسم والدار لكان لا يكون في غيرها بموجود، والدار توجد وهو ليس بموصوف بالكون فيها. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: وهذا أمر ظاهر لا يسأله أحد؛ إذ سكناه يزول وقت تحركه من غير زوال الجسمية عنه، فثبت أنه غير. ثم أجاب من قال: لعل سكونه معه حيث كان، [مع] ما قد يذكر مدة سكونه في مكان بزيادة ونقصان، ثبت أن ثمة غير السكون الأول. وهذا مثل الأول لا يسأل عنه، وجوابه ما بينا، والله المستعان.
And he argued that the rest (sukūn) of the body is a meaning (maʿnā) other than the body by what is said: 'He is in such a house.' If there were nothing but the body and the house, he would not exist elsewhere, but the house exists while he is not described as being in it. Abu Mansur — may Allah have mercy on him — said: 'This is an obvious matter that no one asks about; for his rest disappears at the time of his movement without the body-ness (jismiyya) leaving him, so it is established that it is other.' Then he answered one who said: 'Perhaps his rest is with him wherever he is, along with what may be mentioned of the duration of his resting in a place with increase and decrease, it is established that there is other than the first rest.' And this is like the first; it is not asked about, and its answer is what we explained. And Allah is the source of aid.
Source pages p. 738

Commentary
This passage is a proof (استدلال) that rest (سكون) is an accident distinct from the body. The argument: when we say a person is in a house, the relation of 'being in' is not identical to either the body or the house; it is a separate meaning. Maturidi (Abu Mansur) confirms this is obvious: rest disappears when movement occurs while the body remains unchanged, so rest must be something other than the body. He then addresses an objection that rest might accompany the body everywhere, but notes that rest can vary in duration (increase or decrease), proving that each instance of rest is different, thus the first rest is not inherently tied to the body. The answer is straightforward and does not require elaboration.

Key Terms
سكون
معنى
جسم
جسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rest is the body's state of being still; it is not a separate entity.
Correction: Maturidi shows that rest disappears when motion begins while the body remains, proving rest is a separate accident.

Checkpoint
What evidence does Maturidi give that rest is distinct from the body?
A) Rest is invisible.
B) Rest disappears when motion begins while the body persists.
C) Rest has no effect on the body.
D) The body feels heavy when resting.

Key Takeaway
Rest is a separate accident that comes and goes independently of the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience inner peace or contentment, do you assume it is a permanent part of you that you control? This lesson proves that rest is a transient accident that can leave you while you remain. What would you change today if you truly believed that your peace of mind is not your own possession but a gift that can be taken away at any moment?
?
Think of a time when you felt physically or spiritually 'at rest' in a specific situation. Did you attribute that rest to the place or to yourself? According to this passage, rest is a separate meaning that comes and goes. How does this truth challenge your tendency to attach your well-being to external circumstances or your own efforts? What one concrete step will you take to rely more on the Giver of rest rather than the rest itself?
?
If rest is not inherent to your body or your soul, then every moment of calm you enjoy is a direct creation of Allah. Are you living with that awareness? When you feel restless, do you recognize that your restlessness is also an accident that can be replaced? What does this imply about your responsibility to seek true rest from the only One who creates it?

The Astronomers' Claim: Stars as Eternal Directors of the World
Maturidi reports the astronomers' view on stellar causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key claims made by a group of astronomers regarding the role of stars in directing the world, including their analogies and causal framework.

Core Idea
Astronomers argued that stars eternally direct the world through their motions, reducing all events to necessity.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents the doctrine of a group of astronomers who believed that stars have always been in charge of worldly affairs. They used a weaving analogy to explain how stellar motions produce different outcomes. Understanding their view is essential before examining Maturidi's refutation. Let us first carefully read and analyze their argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to grasp the basic meaning.
The passage is a report of the astronomers' view, not Maturidi's own position.
2
Identify the main claim
Notice that the astronomers assert that stars have never ceased to direct the world's affairs, and that some stars are fortunate (sa'd).
3
Understand the weaving analogy
The astronomers compare the stars' influence to a weaver using threads and a needle to create brocade patterns. The world's changes correspond to stellar motions.
This analogy suggests that the stars are the efficient cause of all worldly events.
4
Examine the causal claims
They claim that bodies are eternal and motions (accidents) occur endlessly, with each motion generating a different effect. They give the example of the egg and chicken.
This establishes a deterministic causal mechanism without divine intervention.
5
Reflect on the implication
The astronomers reduce all events to necessity forced by the stars. Consider how this conflicts with Islamic beliefs about divine will and free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم قوم من المنجمة أن النجوم لم تزل تدبر أمر العالم، هي ومنها سعد، فاختلافه باختلاف ما اتصل به منها كأداة صاحب الديباج بالخيوط الموصولة من الإبر بسم بأعلى أداتها بما يظهر فيه من الظهور وغيره برفع الخشب وحفظها، فمثله أمر النجوم بالعالم تختلف صورته باختلاف تحرك النجوم، وفي اختلافها وائتلافها السعادة والنحس. وهي لم تزل تتحرك فيحدث من كل حركة غير الذي يحدث من غيرها ويتولد ذلك، ويمثل ذلك يقولون في البيضة والدجاجة أنه يكون ذلك بضرب من حركات النجوم كالديباج الذي ذكرت. وزعموا أن الأجسام قديمة وهي غير الأعراض، والحركات أعراض، تحدث إلى ما لا نهاية لها، وصيروا أمر جميع العالم اضطرارا بما كان كذلك بالنجوم
And some of the astronomers claimed that the stars have never ceased to direct the affairs of the world—they and what is among them of fortune. So its change is according to the change of what is connected to it from them, like the tool of the owner of the brocade with the threads connected from the needle at the top of his tool, by what appears in it of appearance and other things, by lifting and preserving the wood. So similarly, the affair of the stars with the world: its form changes according to the change of the stars' motion, and in their difference and conjunction are fortune and misfortune. They never cease moving, so from each motion occurs something other than what occurs from another, and that is generated. And they give an example of that, saying about the egg and the chicken: that it comes about by a type of the stars' motions, like the brocade I mentioned. And they claimed that bodies are eternal and they are not accidents, and motions are accidents, occurring endlessly, and they made the affair of the entire world necessary, being thus by the stars.
Source pages p. 742

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrine of a group of astrologers (المنجمة) as a view to be refuted. The argument is structured as follows: First, they assert that stars have always been directing the world, with some stars being fortunate. The world's changes correspond to what is 'connected' to the stars. They offer an analogy: just as a weaver uses threads and needles to produce a brocade pattern, the stars produce worldly events through their movements. Second, they claim that the stars' different positions (difference and conjunction) cause happiness and misfortune. Third, they argue that each motion generates a different effect, and give the example of the egg and chicken as being caused by stellar motions. Finally, they assert that bodies are eternal while motions (which are accidents) occur endlessly, and they reduce all worldly events to necessary outcomes forced by the stars. The author implies that this view is false because it denies divine free will and the temporality of the world, but the refutation is not fully elaborated here.

Key Terms
نجوم
منجمة
تدبير
سعد
نحس
أعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The astronomers' view is Maturidi's own belief.
Correction: Imam Maturidi is reporting an erroneous view in order to refute it later.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The astronomers' view reduces the world's events to necessary outcomes of eternal stellar motions, denying divine free will.

Against the Naturalists: Natures Cannot Be Eternal
Maturidi refutes the claim that natures are eternal, arguing for a Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that natural principles cannot be eternal due to their mutual opposition, and how this leads to affirming a Creator.

Core Idea
Opposing natures cannot be eternal; they need a unifier.

Maturidi engages with the naturalist claim that natures are eternal and self-subsisting. He demonstrates that this view is logically self-defeating because natures are mutually opposed, and opposition leads to dissolution. This lesson unpacks his argument and shows why only a Creator can account for the world's coherence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let Maturidi's own argument come through.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key premise
Note that Maturidi states natures are mutually opposed (متضادة). This is the foundation of his argument.
Opposition is inherent in natural elements like fire and water.
3
Follow the logical consequence
Opposition requires repulsion, leading to separation and eventual annihilation. Therefore, natures cannot subsist on their own.
If natures were eternal, they would have already destroyed each other.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since natures cannot be eternal, there must be a unifier that brings them together and subdues them—the Creator. The world's unified existence proves its temporality.
This refutes the naturalist claim of eternal natures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنفسها وقيامها ، فلزم القول بموجد قديم ، مع ايجاد ما فيه وبه يدخل في حد الوجود . على أن حدث الأعراض مما لا نمانع فيه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله وبعد ، فإنه معلوم أن تلك الطبائع هى متضادة ، وحق التضاد التدافع وفى ذلك تفرق ، وفى التفرق تبدد وتفان ، فلم يحتمل أن يكون أصول الأشياء لأنفسها كائنة وقائمة مع التناقض الذي ذكرت ، ثبت أنها إن كانت كانت يمانع عن التدافع الذى فيه التبدد ، وهو الجامع بينها بعد التفرق ، القاهر لها ، و بالمجمع كان العالم ، ثبت حدوثه . وفى ذلك فساد القول بالطبائع لأن كون شيء لا عن شيء ليس بأبعد في العقول من قيام الشيء مع صده وهو ما ينقضه ، ولبعد ذلك عن عقولهم صاروا إلى ما قالوا ، فاذا لزمهم فيما قالوا مثل الذي عنه فروا بطل قولهم وذهب عذرهم ... وبالله العصمة
For themselves and their subsistence, so it is necessary to affirm an eternal Creator, with the creation of that which is in and by which it enters the limit of existence. Moreover, the temporality of accidents is something we do not dispute, and there is no power except with Allah. After that, it is known that those natures are mutually opposed, and the requirement of opposition is mutual repulsion, and in that there is separation, and in separation there is dissolution and annihilation. So it is not possible that the principles of things are for themselves existing and subsisting with the contradiction I mentioned. It is established that if they were, they would prevent the repulsion in which there is dissolution, and He is the one who unites them after separation, the subjugator of them, and by the united whole the world came to be; so its temporality is established. In that is the corruption of the doctrine of natures, because a thing coming from nothing is not more far-fetched in the intellects than a thing's subsistence with its opposite which negates it. Because of the remoteness of that from their intellects, they turned to what they said. So when they are bound by what they said to that from which they fled, their statement becomes invalid and their excuse disappears... And with Allah is protection.
Source pages p. 742

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the naturalists who claim that natural principles (طبائع) are eternal and self-subsisting. Maturidi begins by affirming that the existence and subsistence of things necessitate an eternal Creator. He notes that accidents are agreed to be temporal. Then he argues that natures are mutually opposed; opposition entails repulsion, leading to separation and eventual annihilation. Therefore, natures cannot be the eternal principles of things, because they would self-destruct. There must be a unifier that brings them together after separation and subdues them, and by this unified whole the world is constituted, proving its temporality. The naturalists' position is incoherent because they fled from the absurdity of something coming from nothing, but their alternative—that something can persist with its contradictory—is equally absurd. Thus, their argument collapses.

حادث
موجد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natural principles (natures) can be eternal and self-subsisting even though they are mutually opposed.
Correction: Opposition leads to mutual repulsion and dissolution, so such natures cannot persist without a unifier. Therefore, natures cannot be eternal.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Natures cannot be eternal because their mutual opposition would cause self-destruction; only an eternal Creator can unite and sustain them.

Refutation of Infinite Motions
Maturidi refutes infinite motions and proves beginning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an infinite series of motions cannot exist and how Maturidi establishes that the world must have a beginning.

Core Idea
An endless chain of motions must have a beginning.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi tackles a foundational question: can there be an infinite series of events? He provides a concise logical argument that every motion that has concluded points to a beginning. This refutes the possibility of an eternal world and affirms that creation started.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the key terms: حركات (motions), نهاية (end), ابتداء (beginning), فناء (passing away), انقضاء (termination).
The Arabic has some textual variants; focus on the logical flow.
2
Identify the argument's structure
Break the passage into premise, reasoning, and conclusion. The premise: every motion that has passed ends the prior motions. The reasoning: if there were no beginning, the series would have no end, but each motion ends, so the series must terminate, implying a beginning.
You may want to write the argument in your own words.
3
Understand the key terms
Reflect on 'fana' (passing away) and 'inqida' (termination). Maturidi uses these to show that what has an end cannot lack a beginning.
These terms are central to the proof.
4
Connect to the broader issue
Consider why this matters: if the world had no beginning, it would require an infinite series of events. Maturidi shows this is impossible, so the world must have been created.
This argument is a classic kalam proof for the existence of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : / أما القول بحركات لا نهاية لها فقد بينا فيما تقدم فسادها، ما لا يشك في حركة انقضت إلا أنها نهاية ما تقدم من الحركات حتى لا يكون شيء مما تقدم بعد هذه، وإذا ثبت نهاية الفناء لها والانقضاء هو كذلك، يجز أن يتناهى الاقتضاء لما لا يتناهى له الابتداء، ثبت لذلك الابتداء.
The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: As for the claim of motions without end, we have already shown its corruption. There is no doubt about a motion that has passed except that it is the end of what preceded from motions, so that nothing of what preceded comes after it. And when the end of passing away and termination is established for it, it is possible that the termination [of that which has no beginning] is reached, so beginning is established for it.
Source pages p. 743

Commentary
This passage is a proof refuting the possibility of an infinite series of motions. The argument follows: every motion that has concluded is the end of all prior motions, meaning after it there is none of those prior motions. If the series had no beginning, then there would be no end to the prior motions, but since each motion does end, the series must terminate, implying that it has a beginning. The author uses the concept of 'fana' (passing away) and 'inqida' (termination) to show that if something has an end, it cannot be without a beginning.

Key Terms
حركات
نهاية
ابتداء
فناء
انقضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that infinite motion is possible because we can imagine an endless series without contradiction.
Correction: Maturidi argues that actual motions we observe always conclude, and if the series were infinite without beginning, there would be no way for it to reach any given motion. Thus, the series cannot be actual.

Checkpoint

A) That motions are eternal
B) That the series of motions has a beginning
C) That motions are infinite
D) That motions are accidents

Key Takeaway
An infinite series of motions is impossible because each motion ends, forcing the whole series to have a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Every motion you make—every word, every deed—will pass away and be judged. If the very structure of reality demands a beginning and an end, are you preparing for the end that is coming? What specific habit will you change today to align your life with the reality that your actions are finite and accountable?
