Refutation of the Eternity of Bodies by Speed Differential
Maturidi refutes the eternity of bodies using the example of unequal speeds and circular motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses the observable difference in speeds between moving bodies to prove that all bodies have a beginning and are not eternal.

Core Idea
Differential speeds in straight or circular motion prove that bodies must have a temporal beginning.

Imam Maturidi presents a powerful argument against those who claim that material bodies (a'yan) are eternal. He points to a simple observation: when two bodies move at different speeds, one must have started before the other, or else the difference in speed would be meaningless. This argument, applied to both straight-line and circular motion, establishes that all bodies are contingent and created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Let the argument's flow sink in before analyzing.
Notice the use of 'تفاوت' (difference) as the key starting point.
2
Understand the straight-line example
Focus on the scenario of two bodies moving straight at different speeds. Ask yourself: what would be impossible if they were eternal?
If both were eternal with no beginning, the difference in speed would be unexplainable.
3
Grasp the logical consequence
Maturidi argues that denying a finite limit (beginning) makes the observed difference impossible. Therefore, the bodies must have beginnings.
This is a reductio ad absurdum against eternal bodies.
4
Extend to circular motion
The same reasoning applies to circular motion. Consider how the argument holds regardless of the path.
Circular motion also involves differential speeds, so the conclusion is general.
5
Apply to the broader refutation
Maturidi declares that this refutes all who assert the eternity of bodies (a'yan) and reminds that all bodies are subject to accidents.
This argument is part of the larger kalām tradition against pre-eternal matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما لو تفاوت - في رأي العين ذهاب سرعة . أحد سبق [آخر ] ، يسيران سيرا مستقيما، لا يحتمل أن لا يكون ابتداء أحدهما قبل ابتداء الآخر أو أحدهما أسرع الآخر، وفى رفع النهاية عنها بطلان النهاية بينهما، وفى بطلانه نقض المحسوس، ثبت لهما الابتداء، وكذلك هذا المعنى، وكذلك هذا المعنى فى المستدير من الحركات والله الموفق . وبهذا كله ننقض على جميع القائلين بقدم الأعيان، غير خارجة عن الأعراض، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Moreover, when two [bodies] differ in appearance in the speed of motion, one preceding the other, moving in a straight line, it is not possible that there is no beginning of one before the beginning of the other, or one is faster than the other. And in removing the [finite] limit from them, the limit between them is rendered void, and in its voidance, the sensed is contradicted. Thus beginning is established for them. And the same meaning applies to the circular motions. And Allah is the Provider of success. And by all this we refute all those who assert the eternity of bodies (a'yan), which are not free from accidents. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 743

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim bodies (a'yan) are eternal. Maturidi uses an example: two bodies moving at different speeds in a straight line. It is impossible that they have no beginning, because one must have started before the other or one is faster. If we remove the idea of a finite limit, then the difference in speed would be meaningless, contradicting observation. The same argument applies to circular motion. Therefore, all bodies have a beginning, refuting their eternity.

Key Terms

سرعة
ابتداء
أعيان
مستدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think differential speeds could exist in an eternal system.
Correction: Maturidi argues that difference in speed implies a temporal beginning; without a beginning, the difference would be meaningless.

Checkpoint

The observable difference in speed between moving bodies demonstrates that they cannot be eternal and must have a beginning.

Refutation of Naturalists: Inverted Causality
Maturidi refutes naturalists by reversing their causal claim using observable motion and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that naturalists have inverted cause and effect, and that heat and cold are generated by motion and rest, not the other way around.

Core Idea
Motion generates heat, stillness generates moisture — natures are effects, not causes.

Maturidi challenges the naturalists who claim that the world arises from eternal natures. He proposes turning their argument around: observable motion and rest produce heat and moisture, so natures are contingent states of the world, not its origin. This refutation uses empirical observation to undermine the naturalist claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
The Arabic has some OCR corruption, but the translation is reliable.
2
Identify the two groups addressed
Notice that Maturidi addresses 'both groups' — the naturalists and those who claim eternity of bodies. He challenges them to provide evidence.
One group claims the world from natures, the other from eternal bodies.
3
Understand the inverted reasoning
Maturidi says if the statement is reversed, it is 'nearer to existence.' Identify how he flips the naturalist claim: motion and rest generate heat and moisture, so natures are produced, not producing.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Examine the specific example
Observe that disturbance generates heat, stillness generates moisture. Then see how stellar movement is explained by changes on earth (vapors, seas, etc.), showing that stars are influenced by earthly conditions, not vice versa.
This empirical reversal undermines the naturalist argument.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi ends by saying this explanation is 'more appropriate to be a proof for what is hidden from us.' Consider how he uses observable phenomena to refute claims about unseen origins.
The argument relies on sensory experience, which the naturalists themselves claim to follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبمثله تكلم أصحاب الطبائع . ثم يقال للفريقين جميعا : بم عرفتم أنه كذلك ؟ فإن ادعوا السمع فيه عمورضوا بالسمع الذي ورد ممن فيهم حجيج الصدق، فهم أحق أن يصدقوا، الرسل . وإن ادعوا العيان والحمس أكذيهم علمهم بأنفسهم، انهم لا يا كرون شهدوا تدبير النجوم والطبائع، وإن رجعوا إلى الاستدلال بما عاينوا فليس في شيء مما عاينوا دليل تدبير النجوم ولا قدم الطبائع وتولد العالم من، بل لو قلب على الفريقين جميعا القول كان أقرب إلى الوجود وأحق في الاستدلال . فأما أمر الطبائع، فإنه في الوجود إن كثرة الاضطراب والتحرك تولد الحرارة فى نفس المضطرب المتحرك، وكثرة السكون والقرار تولد الرطوبة فتكون الطبائع في الحادثة أحوال العالم، دون أن يكون العالم هو المتول، عنها، وهذا أقرب إلى حق الجواس . ثم يقال : أن اضطراب الفلك وتحرك النجوم وتقلبها على أحوال الاجتماع والتفرق يكون بتقلب أحوال الأرضين وما فيها من أنواع الأشجار البحار والمياه، وجوهر التفرق التي يعلو بخارها، أو هى بجوهرها كالنيران والجواهر وما ذكر فهذا أحق؛ إذ هو أقرب إلى العين من الحقيقية وبها ينقلب أمر النجوم . وأولى أن يكون دليلا لما غاب عنا، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And similarly spoke the naturalists. Then it is said to both groups: How do you know it is so? If they claim audition (sam') in it, and they accept the audition that came from those among them who are truthful, then they are more deserving to believe the messengers. And if they claim direct sight and [something] we deny them? Their knowledge from themselves? They do not [??] they witnessed the management of stars and natures. And if they return to inference from what they observed, there is nothing in what they observed that indicates the management of stars nor the eternity of natures and the generation of the world from them. Rather, if the statement is reversed for both groups, it is nearer to existence and more truthful in inference. As for the matter of natures, indeed in existence, the abundance of disturbance and movement generates heat in the disturbed and moving thing, and abundance of stillness and settlement generates moisture, so natures become in the contingent states of the world, without the world being generated from them. And this is nearer to the truth of the senses. Then it is said: The disturbance of the sphere, the movement of stars, and their alternation in states of conjunction and separation occur due to the changing states of the earths and what is in them of types of trees, seas, and waters, and the essence of separation whose vapor rises, or they are in their essence like fires and jewels and what is mentioned; this is more truthful, as it is nearer to the eye than [something] and by it the matter of stars is changed. And it is more appropriate to be a proof for what is hidden from us. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 744

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the naturalists who claim that the world is generated from natures (taba'i') and that the stars are governed by them. Maturidi first challenges both groups (the naturalists and those who claim eternity of bodies) to provide evidence. If they rely on revelation, they should accept the true messengers. If they rely on observation, it only shows how disturbance generates heat and stillness generates moisture, meaning natures are contingent effects, not eternal causes. Furthermore, the movement of the stars can be better explained by changes on earth, such as vapors rising, which is more immediate to the senses.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natures (taba'i') are self-subsisting eternal causes that generate the world.
Correction: Maturidi shows that natures like heat and moisture are effects of motion and rest, and are thus contingent states of the world, not its origin.

Natures Are Effects, Not Causes: Maturidi's Refutation of Naturalists
Maturidi refutes naturalists by arguing natures are effects of worldly states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that natural phenomena like heat and moisture are generated by worldly states (agitation and stillness) and therefore natures are effects, not independent causes.

Core Idea
Natures are not causes; they are effects of the world's states.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi directly challenges the naturalists who claim that natures (such as heat, moisture) are eternal causes of the world. He argues instead that what we observe through our senses shows that these qualities arise from physical states like agitation and stillness. This places natures as effects (ahwal) of the world, not its origin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Maturidi first addresses both astrologers and naturalists before focusing on natures.
2
Identify Maturidi's challenge
Maturidi begins by asking how the naturalists and astrologers know their claim. He then examines three possible sources of knowledge: revelation (sam'), direct observation (ayn/hiss), and inference (istidlal). Find these three and note how he refutes each.
The refutation of revelation is brief: if they accept revelation from their own authorities, they should accept the messengers too.
3
Trace the argument about natures
Focus on the last part of the passage where Maturidi gives an example: agitation and motion generate heat; stillness and quiescence generate moisture. He concludes that natures are 'states' (ahwal) of the world, not its cause.
Understand that 'heat' and 'moisture' are examples of natures that naturalists considered eternal causes.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi ends by saying this reasoning is 'closer to the truth of the senses.' Consider why he says this and what it means for the naturalist argument.
This is an empirical argument based on observable causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال للفريقين جميعا: بم عرفتم أنه كذلك؟ فإن ادعوا السمع فيه فيرضوا بالسمع الذي ورد ممن فيهم حجج الصدق، فهم أحق أن يصدقوا الرسل. وإن ادعوا العيان والحس أكذبهم علمهم بأنفسهم، أنهم لا يقرون [؟] شهدوا تدبير النجوم والطبائع، وإن رجعوا إلى الاستدلال بما عاينوا فليس في شيء مما عاينوا دليل تدبير النجوم ولا قدم الطبائع وتولد العالم منه، بل لو قلب على الفريقين جميعا القول كان أقرب إلى الوجود وأحق في الاستدلال. فأما أمر الطبائع، فإنه في الوجود إن كثرة الاضطراب والتحرك تولد الحرارة في نفس المضطرب المتحرك، وكثرة السكون والقرار تولد الرطوبة فتكون الطبائع في الحادثة أحوال العالم، دون أن يكون العالم هو المتولد عنها، وهذا أقرب إلى حق الحواس.
Then it is said to both groups: How did you know that it is so? If they claim audition (sam') in it, then they be satisfied with the audition that came from those among them who [have] truthful proofs, they are more entitled to believe the messengers. And if they claim direct sight and sense, their knowledge of themselves belies them, that they do not acknowledge [؟] they witnessed the governance of the stars and natures. And if they resort to inference (istidlal) from what they have observed, then in none of what they have observed is there evidence for the governance of the stars nor the eternity of natures and the generation of the world from them. Rather, if the statement were reversed against both groups, it would be closer to existence and more rightful in inference. As for the matter of natures, it is that in existence, the abundance of agitation and motion generates heat in the agitated and moving thing itself, and the abundance of stillness and quiescence generates moisture, so natures become, in occurrence, states of the world, without the world being generated from them. And this is closer to the truth of the senses.
Source pages p. 744

Commentary
This passage refutes both astrologers and naturalists who claim that the world is governed by stars or natures. The author first challenges them to justify their claim. If they rely on revelation (sam'), they should accept the messengers who brought revelation. If they rely on observation (ayn and hiss), their own knowledge disproves them because they do not even consistently observe what they claim. Then he turns specifically to the naturalists' argument about natures (taba'i'). He argues that what we observe is that agitation and motion generate heat, and stillness and quiescence generate moisture. These phenomena (heat, moisture) are the 'natures' that naturalists speak of, but they are actually generated by the states of the world (agitation, stillness). Therefore, natures are effects (ahwal) of the world, not its cause. The world is not generated from them; rather they arise from the world. This reasoning is closer to sensory evidence (haqq al-hawass). The point is to show that the naturalists' argument is circular and contradicts observable causality.

Key Terms
سمع (sam')
عيان (ayn)
حس (hiss)
طبائع (taba'i')
استدلال (istidlal)
اضطراب
حرارة
رطوبة
أحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi rejects all causality or that he claims natures don't exist.
Correction: Maturidi does not deny that natures (heat, moisture, etc.) exist; he denies that they are eternal, independent causes. He argues they are generated effects (ahwal) of the world's states, thus dependent on God's creation of those states.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what do agitation and motion generate?
A) Coldness
B) Moisture
C) Heat
D) Stillness

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that natures are generated effects (ahwal) of worldly states, not eternal causes independent of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that something you observe (like a pattern or cause) is independent and eternal, when in fact it might be a temporary effect of something deeper? What in your life right now might be a 'nature' you mistakenly treat as a cause?
?
Maturidi challenges the naturalists to examine how they 'know' their claims. How do you know that your own beliefs about the world (not religious, but everyday assumptions) are sound? Are you relying on revelation, direct observation, or inference? Which ones are you most prone to over-trust?
?
If natures like heat and moisture are generated from worldly states, then everything in creation points to its own contingency. Does your daily life reflect a deep conviction that every phenomenon is sustained by God, or do you live as if things have independent power? What would change if you truly saw all 'natures' as momentary states?

Against Celestial Governance
Maturidi argues stellar motions are effects of earthly changes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that celestial motions are caused by earthly changes, not the other way around.

Core Idea
The stars are not the governors; they are governed by earthly conditions.

Maturidi begins by challenging the common belief that the movements of stars and the sphere control earthly events. He argues instead that the apparent connections are better explained by changes in the earth and its vapors. This lesson examines his reasoning and its implications for causality and divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let Maturidi's argument sink in before interpreting.
Notice that the passage starts with 'ثم يقال' indicating a continuation of the refutation.
2
Identify Maturidi's alternative explanation
Find the specific earthly causes (trees, seas, waters, vapors) that Maturidi says cause stellar motions. He lists several possibilities.
He uses 'يكون بتقلب أحوال الأرضين' – the variation of earthly states.
3
Understand the epistemological argument
Maturidi says this explanation is 'nearer to the true eye' and thus a better proof for unseen matters. Consider why he prioritizes perceptual evidence.
This reflects his empirical tendency in refuting astrological claims.
4
Reflect on the refutation's scope
Ask yourself: Does Maturidi deny any effect of stars? Or does he only deny that they are self-caused? This passage targets the direction of causality.
He ends with a reliance on God's power, affirming divine sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال: أن اضطراب الفلك وتحرك النجوم وتقلبها على أحوال الاجتماع والتفرق يكون بتقلب أحوال الأرضين وما فيها من أنواع الأشجار والبحار والمياه، وجوهر التفرق التي يعلو بخارها، أو هي بجوهرها كالنيران والجواهر وما ذكر فهذا أحق؛ إذ هو أقرب إلى العين الحقيقية وبها ينقلب أمر النجوم. وأولى أن يكون دليلا لما غاب عنا، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم تكلم هؤلاء بما تكلم به أصحاب الطبائع: إن ذلك الصانع إنما خرج فعله حكما متقنا بما عنده من العلم وله من القدرة، ولولا ذلك لا احتمل ما ذكرت فإنه بما سبق من التدبير استقام ذلك، فمثله أمر النجوم، أو كان على ما يقول / كان يكون ذلك كذلك بتدبير عليم حكيم أنشأه على ذلك. ولو كان إليها التدبير لما يحتمل أن تتعب نفسها بالسير والشركات الدائمة وتؤلمها؛ إذ كذلك حال الأحياء في الشاهد، إن تلك الأحوال تتعبهم وتؤلمهم، أو أن يكون من الموات.
Then it is said: that the agitation of the sphere (falak) and the movement of the stars and their variation in states of conjunction and separation occur due to the variation of the states of the earths and what they contain of types of trees, seas, and waters, and the essence of separation whose vapor rises, or they are by their essence like fires and jewels and what was mentioned—this is more rightful; for it is nearer to the true eye and by it the affair of the stars is reversed. And it is more fitting to be evidence for what is unseen by us. And there is no power except with God. Then these people spoke with what the partisans of natures spoke: that the Maker (al-sani')—His action emerged as a precise judgment due to the knowledge He possesses and the power He has, and were it not for that, what you mentioned would not be possible, for it is by prior planning (tadbir) that it became upright. Similarly is the affair of the stars, or as he says—it would be so by the planning of a knowing, wise one who instituted it upon that. And if planning belonged to them [the stars], it would not be possible that they would fatigue themselves with perpetual motion and conjunctions and suffer pain; for such is the case of living beings in the witnessed world—these states fatigue and pain them—or that they be from the inanimate.
Source pages p. 744

Commentary
In this passage, Maturidi continues his refutation, now addressing the claim that celestial bodies (stars, sphere) govern earthly changes. He first argues that the motions and states of the stars are actually caused by changes in the earth and its vapors; the stars are affected by earthly conditions, not vice versa. This is more evident to the senses and thus a better proof for unseen matters. Then he presents the argument of the naturalists (ashab al-taba'i') and astrologers: they say that the Maker's action is precise due to His knowledge and power, and that such precision requires prior planning (tadbir); similarly, the stars must have a wise planner. Maturidi refutes this by pointing out that if the stars themselves were the planners (i.e., if planning belonged to them), they would not voluntarily endure perpetual motion and suffering, because living beings in this world are fatigued and pained by such states. Thus the stars must be directed by another, not self-governing.

Key Terms
فلك (falak)
نجوم (nujum)
تدبير (tadbir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what causes the motion and variation of the stars?
A) The stars' own will and planning
B) The variation of states of earths and their vapors
C) The direct command of God without any intermediary
D) The influence of other celestial bodies

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that stellar motions are effects, not causes, of earthly changes, shifting the direction of causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the stars, do you secretly feel they exert some pull over your life or destiny? How does Maturidi's argument challenge you to redirect that sense of dependence solely to Allah?
?
Maturidi uses what is 'nearer to the eye' as proof for the unseen. In your daily life, do you trust your physical senses more than the unseen realities of faith? How can you strengthen your conviction in the unseen while still using sensory evidence as a tool?
?
The passage ends with 'there is no power except by Allah.' Think of a situation where you feel helpless or controlled by external forces—like your health, finances, or others' opinions. Do you truly believe that only Allah's power governs that outcome? What concrete act of tawakkul can you perform this week to live that belief?

Refuting the Maker's Wisdom Argument
Maturidi refutes the claim that stars are wise agents by arguing against self-imposed fatigue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that the precision of the Maker's work implies stars are wise agents.

Core Idea
If stars were wise planners, they would not choose perpetual fatigue.

In this continuation, Maturidi addresses an argument from naturalists: the Maker's precise action requires prior planning, so the stars must also have a wise planner. Maturidi turns this back on them, showing that if the stars themselves were planners, they would not voluntarily endure endless motion and suffering. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note that this is a direct response to a previous claim.
The phrase 'ثم تكلم هؤلاء' signals a shift to the opponents' argument.
2
Identify the naturalists' argument
They argue that the Maker's precise action (ḥukman mutqanan) requires knowledge and power, and that this order stems from prior planning (tadbir). They extend this to the stars' motion.
They say 'فمثله أمر النجوم' – similarly is the affair of the stars.
3
Understand Maturidi's counter-argument
Maturidi points out that if planning belonged to the stars themselves, they would not voluntarily fatigue themselves with perpetual motion and suffering. Living beings in the witnessed world avoid such pain.
He uses an analogy from the witnessed world (shahid) to argue against self-planning.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by implying that since the stars cannot be self-planning wise agents, they must be directed by another—God. Or alternatively, they are inanimate and thus not agents.
The final phrase 'أو أن يكون من الموات' offers an alternative: they could be inanimate.

Maturidi's Refutation of Hyle's Motionlessness
Maturidi refutes the view that hyle cannot be described by motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the claim that hyle cannot be described by motion, using logical contradictions and empirical observations.

Core Idea
Denying motion in hyle leads to absurdities about contact, corporeality, and the origin of atoms.

This passage presents Maturidi's critical response to those who argue that prime matter (hyle) is not subject to motion. He begins by noting a contradiction in their position: they call power 'motion' while denying it to hyle. He then shows that if hyle had no motion, it could not be conceived as either in contact with or separate from anything—both of which imply corporeality and accidentality. Finally, he refutes the astrological theory that atoms arise from a single motion, noting that atoms exist from all directions, which requires diverse motions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the overall argument structure: doubt, logical deduction, empirical refutation.
Pay attention to terms like هيولى (hyle) and قوة (power).
2
Identify the logical dilemma
Maturidi argues that if hyle is not described by motion, it cannot be described as either in contact with something (مماسة) or separate from it (مباينة). Both options entail corporeality and accidentality, which the opponents likely reject.
Contact and separation are qualities distinct from the substance itself.
3
Understand the refutation of the astrological view
The opponents claim atoms come from motions of the origin (أصل). Maturidi counters that atoms exist everywhere, from all directions, which would require contradictory motions, thus invalidating their theory.
This is an empirical argument based on observed diversity of atoms.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how Maturidi uses both logical reasoning and empirical observation to refute a philosophical position. This shows the integration of rational and empirical methods in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بما لا توصف به الهيولى، وقد ذكر عنهم الآراء في الهيولى، فلا أدرى أيصح ذا أو لا؟ إلا أن سمى القوة حركة وهي فيه، فيبطل قوله: إن الهيولى لا يوصف بحركة إذ قد وصفه بها لا يتجلّى من أن يكون مماسة له أو مباينة عنه، وأيهما قال، فيه إثبات الجسمية والعرضية؛ إذ البينونة والمماسة غير الذي يماس ويباين. ثم قول هؤلاء أن حدثت الجواهر من حركات الأصل، وكذلك قول المنجمة، ومعلوم وجود جواهر من علو وسفل ومن كل جانب، على إحالة تلك الحركات المختلفة، فثبت أن ذا باطل.
By what hyle (al-hayula) is not described, and he mentioned their opinions about hyle, so I do not know whether this is correct or not? Except that he called power (al-quwwa) motion and it is in it, so his statement is invalidated: that hyle is not described by motion since he had described it by it. It cannot be avoided that it is either in contact (mumassa) with it or separate (mubayana) from it, and whichever he says, therein is the affirmation of corporeality (al-jismiyya) and accidentality (al-ardiyya); for separation and contact are other than that which touches and separates. Then the statement of these people that atoms (al-jawahir) came to be from the motions of the origin (al-asl), and likewise the statement of the astrologers, while it is known that atoms exist from above and below and from every side, negating those various motions, so this became false.
Source pages p. 749

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim that hyle (prime matter) cannot be described with motion. The author begins by noting that he has reported their opinions about hyle but is uncertain of their correctness. However, they have called power (quwwa) motion, and since motion is in hyle, this invalidates their claim that hyle cannot be described by motion. The author then argues that if hyle is not described by motion, it cannot be conceived as either in contact with something or separate from it; but both contact and separation entail corporeality and accidentality, because the state of being in contact or separate is distinct from the thing that touches or separates. Consequently, rejecting motion in hyle leads to contradictions. Furthermore, these people claim that atoms come to be from the motions of the original element (like the stars), but it is well known that atoms exist from all directions, which would require contradictory motions, thus proving their theory false.

Key Terms
هيولى (hyle)
قوة (power)
حركة (motion)
مماسة (contact)
مباينة (separation)
جسمية (corporeality)
عرضية (accidentality)
جوهر (atom)
أصل (origin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hyle (prime matter) cannot be described by motion because it is purely potential and passive.
Correction: Maturidi argues that denying motion in hyle leads to contradictions: hyle must be either in contact with or separate from something, both of which are motions or their results, implying corporeality and accidentality. Moreover, atoms exist from all directions, contradicting a single motion origin.

Checkpoint

B) Hyle must be either in contact with or separate from something, which implies corporeality and accidentality.
C) Motion is transferred from hyle to atoms directly.
D) Hyle becomes identical with God's essence.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the denial of motion in hyle by showing it leads to contradictions regarding contact, corporeality, and the diversity of atomic existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The passage shows that even our most abstract theological denials have concrete implications for how we view creation. Right now, where in your life do you intellectually deny something about God's creation (like dependence on causes) but still live as if it were true? What step can you take today to bring your lived reality in line with your stated beliefs?

Objection on Hyle and Accidents Answered by the Nura-Zarnikh Analogy
Objection about hyle and accidents, and answer using nura and zarnikh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of lime and arsenic refutes the objection that hyle and accidents cannot change in properties.

Core Idea
Combined effects explain how hyle acquires length from non-long components.

Muhammad ibn Shabib raises a logical objection: if hyle before accidents is not long, and accidents are not long themselves, how does hyle become long? The answer uses a striking analogy from chemistry: lime and arsenic do not burn alone, but together they ignite. This shows that mutually counteracting properties can produce a new state.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage where Muhammad ibn Shabib presents his objection about hyle and accidents. Note the logical structure: hyle is initially not long, accidents are not long, so how does hyle become long?
The objection extends to other accidents like blackness.
2
Examine the nura-zarnikh analogy
Read the answer provided by the author. Understand how lime and arsenic alone do not burn, but together they do. This is used to explain how hyle and accidents can combine to produce new properties.
The analogy illustrates that preventers can be overcome when combined.
3
Apply the analogy to the objection
See how the author applies the same reasoning to hyle and accidents. The preventer (man'i) cannot reside in length or blackness, but the combination allows for new states. This refutes the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعارفهم محمد بن شبيب بما الهيولى قبل حدوث الأعراض ليست بطويلة والأعراض ليست بطويلة فكيف صارت عند الوجود طويلة؟ وكذلك العرض، ولو جاز ذا الجاز أن يجمع بين ما ليس يخلو وما لا يخلو، فيصير خلو، ومثله في جميع الأعراض كلا سواد ولا سواد. فأجاب بالنورة والزرنيخ، أن كل واحد منهما على الانفراد لا يحرق، يمنع عنهم وعند الاجتماع يحرق. فيقال: ما يبعد أن يكون أحدهما يحرق لكن فيه ما عن الإحراق، وفى الآخر ما يمنع هذا المانع عن المانع فيحرق، لا أن لم يكن فيه إحراق، أو كلاهما كانا كذلك، وأمر الأعراض عندك على ما ذكرنا، ومحال حلول المانع فيه لو كان طويلا أو سوادا وكذا في الهيولى؛ لذلك اختلفا
And their knower Muhammad ibn Shabib [objected] that hyle (al-hayula) before the occurrence of accidents (al-a'rad) is not long, and accidents are not long, so how did it become long upon existence? And likewise the accident. And if this were permissible, it would be permissible to combine what is not devoid and what is not devoid, so it becomes devoid, and likewise in all accidents: neither black nor not black. So he answered with lime (al-nura) and arsenic (al-zarnikh): that each one of them alone does not burn, it prevents [burning] by themselves, and when combined, it burns. So it is said: what is far-fetched that one of them burns but in it there is something that prevents burning, and in the other there is something that prevents this preventer from the preventer, so it burns, not that there was no burning in it, or both were like that. And the matter of accidents with you is as we mentioned, and it is impossible for the preventer to reside in it if it were long or black, and likewise in hyle; therefore they differed.
Source pages p. 749

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by Muhammad ibn Shabib and the author's response. The objection states that hyle before accidents occur is not long, and accidents themselves are not long, so how does hyle become long when accidents come into existence? Similarly for other accidents. The objection uses an absurdity: if it were allowed to combine what is not empty and what is not empty to become empty, then by analogy one could claim that accidents can combine to produce contradictory states (neither black nor not black). The answer uses the example of lime (nura) and arsenic (zarnikh): each alone does not burn, but together they burn. The explanation is that one substance may have something that prevents burning, and the other may have something that prevents that preventer, so burning occurs. The author then applies this to accidents: the preventer (man'i) cannot reside in an accident like length or blackness, nor in hyle, which is why scholars differ on this issue.

Key Terms
هيولى (hyle)
عرض (accident)
طول (length)
سواد (blackness)
نورة (lime)
زرنيخ (arsenic)
مانع (preventer/deterrent)
حرق (burning)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the function of the nura and zarnikh analogy in this passage?
A) To prove that hyle cannot be described by length or blackness.
B) To show that two things that individually have no effect can produce an effect when combined, refuting the objection about hyle and accidents.
C) To demonstrate that lime and arsenic are opposites.
D) To argue that accidents cannot combine to produce new states.

Key Takeaway
The nura-zarnikh analogy demonstrates that properties can emerge through combination, refuting the objection that hyle and accidents cannot acquire new characteristics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see something good happen through combined efforts—like success in work or repair in a relationship—do you credit the people involved or the One who removes the preventers? This lesson shows that effects are not inherent in causes. Which of your current projects are you trusting too much in means and not enough in Allah?
?
Are there areas of your life where you feel 'deficient' (like hyle without length) and think you can never change? This passage teaches that new qualities can emerge through combination. What preventers might you be allowing to stay, and what 'zarnikh' (remover) has Allah provided (e.g., du'a, repentance, good company) that you are ignoring?
?
If the nura-zarnikh analogy teaches that outcomes are not in the ingredients but in Allah's permission, then how much of your spiritual growth (e.g., increase in faith) do you attribute to your own efforts versus Allah's grace? Will you today ask Allah to remove the 'preventers' in your heart that block His light?

Perceptual Variation and Objective Reality
Maturidi explains that differences in perception do not negate objective reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that variations in sense perception do not negate objective reality; they arise from differences in the condition of the senses.

Core Idea
Perceptual differences reflect the state of the perceiver, not the unreliability of reality.

Maturidi addresses a classic skeptical challenge: if one person sees a thing as two and another as one, how can we know which is true? He explains that sense knowledge varies with the condition of the sense, and that such variation actually confirms the existence of an objective reality by showing the factors that distort perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
The text has some OCR uncertainty; focus on the overall argument.
2
Identify the challenge
Note the question posed by Ibn Shabib: seeing one thing as two versus one—which perception is true? Consider his initial explanation.
This sets up the objection that Maturidi will answer.
3
Understand Maturidi's principle
Find where Maturidi states that sense knowledge varies according to the conditions (aḥwāl) of the sense. Recognize that an infirmity (āfa) causes perception to differ from reality.
This is the core of the lesson.
4
See the application to jaundice
Read how Maturidi applies the principle to the jaundiced person who tastes honey as bitter. Note that the bitterness is attributed to an infirmity in the taste sense, not to the honey.
This example illustrates the principle in a concrete case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسأل محمد بن شبيب نفسه بما يرى الشيء الواحد شيئين، وآخر يرى شيئا واحدا، فأيها الحق ؟ فزعم أن الأول حسبه كذلك لنظره، يبصره من جهته، يرى نكل عين غير الجهة التي يرى بالآخر، دليله أنه لو أعور لا يرى الآفة، فيه فيعلم أن الآفة حجاب، قال الفقيه رحمه الله : والأصل في هذا ونحوه أن علم الحس يختلف باختلاف أحوال الحس، يعلم ذو الحواس ما به من فبالحاسة يتعلم خلاف الحقيقة عند الآفة، وحقيقته ممتدا ارتفاعها، وذلك يكون في الذي وقعت عليه الحاسة لطافة أو بعد أو ستر الجو بما يغشاه، ومرة يكون من في البصر، وعلى ذلك شأن كل حاسة، وذلك كله معلوم بالحواس، فلا نقيض عليه ما أنه على هذا القول يبطل القول بالخلاف، وبه يحتج، أو يثبت فيبطل قوله بنفي الحقيقة؛ إذ ثبت الاختلاف، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ويعلم الذي يذكر بالقرب منه أو بالزيادة من الضوء ليعلم حقيقته إن ضعف بصره عن إدراكه بالآفة، فنى مثل هذه الأحوال يظهر، ولا قوة إلا بالله وجوابنا في صاحب السفراء الذي يجد العسل مرا، هذا من نفسه الآفة فيما يجد به الطعم، ولا قوة إلا بالله مع ما يعلم هو وقال ابن شبيب : أختلف فيه، قال قوم : في العسل مرارة، فاذا اتصل بما في ذائقه فيقوى [المرارة) فيجده [مُرا] . وقال قوم : إن في ذائق صاحب
And Muhammad ibn Shabib asked himself: [How is it that] one sees a single thing as two, and another sees the same thing as one—which of them is the truth? So he claimed that the first [sees] according to his sight, he sees it from his angle, [and] he sees from each eye a direction different from the direction the other sees, the proof of which is that if he were one-eyed he would not see the defect; thus he knows that the defect is a veil. The Faqih, may God have mercy on him, said: The principle in this and similar matters is that the knowledge of sense perception varies according to the conditions of the sense. The possessor of senses knows what is from [؟]... by the sense he learns contrary to the reality when there is an infirmity; and its reality is extended in its height, and that occurs in what the sense perceives: fineness, distance, or the veiling of the air by what covers it, and sometimes it is from the vision. And such is the case of every sense. And all that is known by the senses, so there is no contradiction against it; that on this statement, the statement of difference is invalidated, and by it he argues, or [the difference] is established, so his statement is invalidated by negating reality, since difference is established. And no power except by God. And one knows what is mentioned by proximity or increase of light to know its reality if his eyesight is too weak to perceive it due to infirmity. So in such conditions it becomes apparent. And no power except by God. Our answer concerning the one with the jaundice who finds honey bitter: this is from an infirmity in himself regarding what he tastes. And no power except with God, with what he himself knows. And Ibn Shabib said: There is disagreement about it. Some said: There is bitterness in honey, so when it connects with what is in his taste it strengthens the bitterness, so he finds it bitter. And some said: It is in the taste of the person with jaundice...
Source pages p. 753

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection from perceptual variation: one person sees one thing as two (double vision), another sees one thing correctly—so which is true? Ibn Shabib first offers an explanation: double vision arises from each eye seeing from a different direction, and if one eye were closed the defect is not seen, so the 'defect' is a veil. Maturidi (referred to as al-Faqih) then gives the principle: sense perception (ʿilm al-ḥiss) varies according to the condition (aḥwāl) of the sense organ. When there is an infirmity (āfa) in the sense, the perception differs from reality. For example, an object may appear small due to distance, or unclear due to air obstruction, or vision may be affected by a defect in the eye. All such variations are themselves known through the senses, so they do not negate objective reality; rather, they confirm that differences in perception occur precisely because of varying conditions. Maturidi then applies this to the example of a jaundiced person (ṣāḥib al-sufrā’) who finds honey bitter: the bitterness is not in the honey but in the infirmity of his taste. He also reports a disagreement: some say honey contains bitterness that is activated by contact with the jaundiced taste, others attribute it to the taste itself. The overall argument defends the consistency of external reality against skeptical claims that perceptual variation implies no stable truth. The lesson guards against relativism based on sense experience.

Key Terms
الحس
الآفة
الحقيقة
الاختلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If perceptions differ, reality itself must be relative.
Correction: Variation stems from conditions of the perceiver, not from the object.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does a jaundiced person taste honey as bitter?
A) Honey actually contains bitterness.
B) There is an infirmity in the person's taste sense.
C) Honey changes its taste based on the person.
D) It is because of psychological suggestion.

Key Takeaway
Variations in perception arise from conditions affecting the senses, not from instability in the objective world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent disagreement where you insisted your perception was correct while someone else saw the same situation differently. What 'infirmity'—like fatigue, anger, or bias—might have affected your senses without you realizing? Will you ask Allah to heal your inner sight before your next conflict?
?
The jaundiced person calls honey bitter. What is a blessing in your life (e.g., a test, a person, a duty) that you have perceived as bitter due to a 'jaundice' in your heart? What would it take to see it as it truly is—a sweet gift from Allah? Commit now to one concrete action to change that perception.

Refuting Natural Temperaments as Ultimate Causes
Maturidi refutes Samarrian natural temperaments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects natural temperaments as the ultimate explanation for animal differences, and how he affirms divine sovereignty over all creation.

Core Idea
Nature describes, not explains—only Allah creates and ordains.

In this passage, Maturidi confronts a rival theological school that claims animal behaviors and traits are caused by the inherent balance of natural elements. He first summarizes their arguments, then delivers a sharp refutation, declaring such causal reasoning an unauthorized imposition on God's creation. This lesson unpacks both the opposing view and Maturidi's response.

Guided Path
1
Read the Samarrian claim
Notice how they explain differences in animals (fish, otter, bat, lion) by the dominance of elemental qualities like moisture, cold, heat, and dryness.
The examples are meant to show that nature alone determines survival and abilities.
2
Understand Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi calls this whole approach 'nonsense' because it treats natural temperaments as independent agents rather than effects of God's will.
He argues that saying 'it is by nature' is merely describing God's creation, not explaining it.
3
Grasp the deeper principle
Maturidi emphasizes that causal reasoning about ultimate causes is beyond human perception and is not sanctioned by revelation. Only God has authority over such matters.
This reinforces the Ash'ari/Maturidi doctrine of occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تكلف نوع ما كلم النظام السمنية مما لا يجدي نفعا، فزعم أن الحيتان كان الغلبة فى طبائعها الرطوبة والبرودة، فإذا صارت إلى الجدب، والغالب عليه الحرارة واليبوسة غلبتا على الرطوبة والندوة فأهلكا. وكذلك كل متضادين من الطبائع إذا غلب واحد ضده أهلكه. وكذلك أمر الطائر في السماء وكلب الماء فإنه أشد اعتدالا من الحوت، يعيش في الماء والبر، والجتماش فإن بصره مسترقة ليست بالقوية، يذهبه ضوء الشمس، نحو ما يعشى الرجل إذا نظر إلى عين، فإذا غابت الشمس ذهب ما أضعف بصره فأبصر، فإذا اشتدت الظلمة لا. وأما الأسد فهو قوى البصر، يبصر بالنهار، وأكثر ما يبصر غيره، وكذلك المانع له بالليل أقل مما يمنع غيره. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : وذلك كله عبث، بل القول إنه كذلك يسبح في خلق، وبهذا الطبع جبل: بعض الجواهر يطير في السماء، وآخر الماء، والثالث يمشى على وجه الأرض، فتكلف الاعتلال لمثل هذا تحكم على رب العالمين، واعتلال بما لم يؤذن له، ولا له به درك، وليس ذلك من نوع ما ضمن الشرع فيه من تحقيق الأعيان، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he undertook a kind of speech of the Samarrian system that yields no benefit. He claimed that the predominant natures in fish are moisture and cold; when they come to dry land, where heat and dryness predominate, these overcome the moisture and dew and destroy them. Likewise every pair of opposites in natures, if one overcomes its opposite, it destroys it. Similarly the matter of the bird in the sky and the water dog (otter), for it is more balanced than the fish, living in water and land. And the [bat?] (al-jatmāsh), its sight is weak and easily taken away by sunlight, similar to how a man is dazzled when looking at the sun; when the sun sets, what weakened his sight goes away and he sees; when darkness intensifies, he does not. As for the lion, it has strong sight, it sees by day, and sees more than others; likewise the impediment for it at night is less than what impedes others. Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: All that is nonsense. Rather the saying is that it is so glorifying in creation, and by this nature He created: some gems fly in the sky, others in water, and a third walk on the earth. So undertaking a causal explanation for such things is an arbitrary ruling over the Lord of the worlds, and an explanation for which no permission has been given, nor does he have perception of it, and that is not of the type that the Law has guaranteed concerning the verification of entities. And there is no power but Allah.
Source pages p. 754

Commentary
This passage refutes the Samarrian claim that differences among animals are due solely to their natural temperaments. Maturidi first presents the opponent's argument: fish perish on land because their cold/moist nature is overcome; birds in the sky, otters, a bat-like creature, and lions each have abilities determined by their elemental balance. Maturidi then declares this whole approach 'nonsense' (ʿabath). He argues that saying animals are created with these natures is merely a description of God's creative act, not a causal explanation. To claim that nature itself causes these traits is to impose human reasoning on God without divine authorization. Such explanations are beyond human perception and are not part of what revelation has entrusted us to investigate about the essence of things. The refutation upholds divine sovereignty and the inadequacy of natural causation as an ultimate explanation.

Key Terms

غلبة
اعتدال
تحكم
شرع
تحقيق الأعيان

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Maturidi denies all natural causation entirely.
Correction: Maturidi denies that nature is an independent agent; he affirms that all causal power belongs to Allah, and natural regularities are merely descriptions of God's consistent creation.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi rejects natural explanations as ultimate causes, affirming that all creatures act by God's creation and ordinance.

?

?
The Samarrians thought they had a rational explanation for animal traits. Maturidi calls it 'nonsense' and 'arbitrary ruling over the Lord of worlds.' Are there areas in your life where you explain events by secondary causes (your personality, upbringing, circumstances) and forget that Allah is the ultimate Creator and Ordainer? What would it mean for you to see everything—including your own strengths and weaknesses—as directly from Him?
?
Maturidi says such causal reasoning is 'not of the type that the Law has guaranteed concerning the verification of entities.' In other words, some questions are beyond human reach and we should stop seeking to answer them. Refutation of the Unchanging Nature Argument
Maturidi refutes the dualist argument from natures and asserts that darkness acts by choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that if mixture is by nature it must be eternal, and that darkness acts by choice, not nature.

Core Idea
Nature cannot change, so dualists' claim of a beginning contradicts their own premise.

Maturidi begins exposing the dualists' confusion: if the mixture of light and darkness is by nature, it would be eternal and unchanging, yet they claim a beginning. He also points out that darkness acts by choice, not by nature, undermining their entire argument from natures.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Focus on the two key claims: mixture by nature and darkness acting by choice.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the logical consequence
Notice Maturidi's deduction: if mixture is by nature, it must always be so because natures do not change. This contradicts the dualists' idea of a beginning.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the choice argument
Maturidi asserts that darkness acts by choice, not nature. Why does this refute their whole argument? Consider that choice implies will and contingency, not necessity.
If darkness acts by choice, then its actions are not determined by nature, so arguments from natures fail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما لا يخلو من الافتراق؛ إذ الامتزاج أن يكون بالطبع، والطبائع لا تنقلب، فيجب أن يكون أبدا كذلك. وأطنب في نوع الطبائع . الظلمة فعاله باختيار، فالقول في الطباع على ذلك فاسد. وأخيرا عنهم تحرك الظلمة إلى أن لقيت [النور] فدخلت عليه، إن قالوا: أبدا ما مر فى كلام " الدهر، وإن قالوا بالابتداء لزم الحدث، والله الموفق . مع هذا الطبع ثم تمام الجهل فى قولهم: يتخلصان بما كان من طبع الثقيل الانحدار وطبع الخفيف الارتفاع، ثم في الابتداء قد امتزجا، فلو لا أن كل واحد منهما على طبع الآخر في / الثقل والخفة ما احتمل الامتزاج، وإذا [۸۲ ] احتمل دل أن الطبعين كانا فى كل واحد، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وإذا احتمل الواحد الأمرين استمل الخير والشر فيبطل الثاني، ولا إلا بالله قوة . على أن اللازم إذ جعلوهما متضادين في الطبيعة أن يجعلوا أحدهما شأنه الامتزاج والآخر البينونة، وقد غلب أحدهما أن يكون على ذلك. ثم من قولهم إنهما إذا تفرقا لا يمتزجان من بعد، فما أدراهم ؟ ووجدنا باليقين لم يؤنس الاجتماع ، فكيف أنهم أبدا على تفرق واجتماع ؟ ، وكذلك في وجود تفرق بجهد؟ وما يدريهم الأزل، فيبطل القول بالنور والظلمة . وبعد، فإن حكمهم هذا عجيب؛ لأنهم لا يخبرون" لأنهم لا يخيرون" عن أحوال كانت، ويكون ما عندهم من جوهر هذين، ولم يكن لهما علم من قبل بالامتزاج، ولا علم بكيفية الفراق، والله الموفق . ثم يطالب على كل فصل مما قالوا من قطع النهاية، وما قالوا من ابتداء العالم دون أن يكون عالم على أثر عالم بلا نهاية، وكذلك يكون بالدليل، وكذلك
Along with what is not free from separation; since mixture is by nature, and natures do not change, so it must always be so. And he elaborated on the type of natures. Darkness acts by choice, so the statement about natures in that regard is false. Finally, from them: the movement of darkness until it met light and entered upon it. If they say: always, it passed in the speech of time; if they say by beginning, origination follows, and Allah is the giver of success. With this nature, then complete ignorance in their statement: they are delivered by what was from the nature of the heavy to descend and the nature of the light to rise, then in the beginning they mixed. Were it not that each one of them is upon the nature of the other in heaviness and lightness, mixture would not be possible. And since mixture is possible, it indicates that the two natures were in each one, and there is no power except with Allah. And if one admits both matters, it contains good and evil, so the second is invalidated. There is no power except with Allah. Upon that the necessary implication: when they made them opposite in nature, they should make one of them characterized by mixture and the other by separation, and one of them has prevailed to be upon that. Then from their statement: when they separate, they do not mix afterwards; what made them know? And we found by certainty that union was not perceived, so how can they be always upon separation and union? Likewise, in existence, separation by effort? And what makes them know pre-eternity? So the statement of light and darkness is invalidated. Moreover, their judgment is strange; because they do not inform about states that were, and what they have is from the substance of these two, and they had no prior knowledge of mixture, nor knowledge of the manner of separation, and Allah is the giver of success. Then he demands for every chapter of what they said of cutting off infinity, and what they said of the beginning of the world without there being a world upon the trace of a world without end, and likewise by proof, and likewise.
Source pages p. 760

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation, now focusing on arguments from natures (طباع). Maturidi points out that if mixture is by nature, it would be eternal and unchanging, contradicting the dualists' claim of a specific event. He then argues that darkness acts by choice, not nature, making the nature-based argument unsound. He addresses the claim that darkness moved towards light and entered it, asking whether this was always (eternal) or began—if eternal, it's inconsistent; if began, it implies origination. He exposes the dualists' ignorance in saying that the two principles separate and reunite based on natures of heaviness and lightness, noting that if each had the other's nature, mixture would be impossible, but if it is possible, then both natures are in each, leading to the absurdity that each contains both good and evil. He further criticizes their arbitrary claims about separation and reunion without empirical evidence or knowledge of pre-eternal states. Finally, he demands proof for their claims about cutting off infinity and the beginning of the world without a prior world, concluding that their entire system is baseless.

Key Terms
طبع
طباع
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does the dualists' claim that mixture is by nature lead to a contradiction?
A) Because nature is changeable
B) Because if by nature, it would be eternal
C) Because darkness is light
D) Because choice is involved

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the dualists' nature argument by showing that nature implies eternal permanence, and that darkness acts by choice, not nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I can't help it, it's just my nature,' are you really hiding from the fact that you choose to act that way? What would it cost you to take full responsibility for that choice today?
?
The dualists assumed that because something has always been a certain way, it must always remain so. Is there a sin or weakness in your life you've labelled as permanent because it feels natural? Can you name it and commit to asking Allah for a change?
?
If darkness—something so seemingly 'natural'—acts by choice, what does that say about your own excuses? What is one area where you can stop blaming your temperament and start exercising your God-given choice to obey?

Causality and Created Things
beginner

Refutation of the Dualists' Claim about Darkness's Movement
Maturidi presents a dilemma on the movement of darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the dualists' claim that darkness moved towards light, questioning whether this movement was eternal or originated.

Core Idea
If darkness's movement was eternal, it contradicts time; if it began, it implies origination.

The dualists claimed that darkness moved towards light and entered it. Maturidi poses a dilemma: either this movement was always (eternal) or it began. If always, it implies a concept of eternal time (دهر) that is inconsistent. If it began, then darkness itself is originated, undermining its status as an eternal principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text. Notice the two options Maturidi presents: 'always' or 'by beginning'.
The word 'الدهر' refers to an eternal time concept.
2
Analyze the dilemma
Maturidi shows that both options lead to problems. For 'always', it falls under 'الدهر' which they likely deny. For 'by beginning', it implies origination (حدث) for darkness, contradicting its eternality.
This is a forced dilemma to expose inconsistency.
3
Draw the conclusion
Since both alternatives are problematic, the dualists' narrative is untenable. The only valid response is to accept that Allah is the giver of success, indicating dependence on divine guidance.
The phrase 'والله الموفق' is a common formula after refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما لا يخلو من الافتراق؛ إذ الامتزاج أن يكون بالطبع، والطبائع لا تنقلب، فيجب أن يكون أبدا كذلك. وأطنب في نوع الطبائع . الظلمة فعاله باختيار، فالقول في الطباع على ذلك فاسد. وأخيرا عنهم تحرك الظلمة إلى أن لقيت [النور] فدخلت عليه، إن قالوا: أبدا ما مر فى كلام " الدهر، وإن قالوا بالابتداء لزم الحدث، والله الموفق . مع هذا الطبع ثم تمام الجهل فى قولهم: يتخلصان بما كان من طبع الثقيل الانحدار وطبع الخفيف الارتفاع، ثم في الابتداء قد امتزجا، فلو لا أن كل واحد منهما على طبع الآخر في / الثقل والخفة ما احتمل الامتزاج، وإذا [۸۲ ] احتمل دل أن الطبعين كانا فى كل واحد، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وإذا احتمل الواحد الأمرين استمل الخير والشر فيبطل الثاني، ولا إلا بالله قوة . على أن اللازم إذ جعلوهما متضادين في الطبيعة أن يجعلوا أحدهما شأنه الامتزاج والآخر البينونة، وقد غلب أحدهما أن يكون على ذلك. ثم من قولهم إنهما إذا تفرقا لا يمتزجان من بعد، فما أدراهم ؟ ووجدنا باليقين لم يؤنس الاجتماع ، فكيف أنهم أبدا على تفرق واجتماع ؟ ، وكذلك في وجود تفرق بجهد؟ وما يدريهم الأزل، فيبطل القول بالنور والظلمة . وبعد، فإن حكمهم هذا عجيب؛ لأنهم لا يخبرون" لأنهم لا يخيرون" عن أحوال كانت، ويكون ما عندهم من جوهر هذين، ولم يكن لهما علم من قبل بالامتزاج، ولا علم بكيفية الفراق، والله الموفق . ثم يطالب على كل فصل مما قالوا من قطع النهاية، وما قالوا من ابتداء العالم دون أن يكون عالم على أثر عالم بلا نهاية، وكذلك يكون بالدليل، وكذلك
Along with what is not free from separation; since mixture is by nature, and natures do not change, so it must always be so. And he elaborated on the type of natures. Darkness acts by choice, so the statement about natures in that regard is false. Finally, from them: the movement of darkness until it met light and entered upon it. If they say: always, it passed in the speech of time; if they say by beginning, origination follows, and Allah is the giver of success. With this nature, then complete ignorance in their statement: they are delivered by what was from the nature of the heavy to descend and the nature of the light to rise, then in the beginning they mixed. Were it not that each one of them is upon the nature of the other in heaviness and lightness, mixture would not be possible. And since mixture is possible, it indicates that the two natures were in each one, and there is no power except with Allah. And if one admits both matters, it contains good and evil, so the second is invalidated. There is no power except with Allah. Upon that the necessary implication: when they made them opposite in nature, they should make one of them characterized by mixture and the other by separation, and one of them has prevailed to be upon that. Then from their statement: when they separate, they do not mix afterwards; what made them know? And we found by certainty that union was not perceived, so how can they be always upon separation and union? Likewise, in existence, separation by effort? And what makes them know pre-eternity? So the statement of light and darkness is invalidated. Moreover, their judgment is strange; because they do not inform about states that were, and what they have is from the substance of these two, and they had no prior knowledge of mixture, nor knowledge of the manner of separation, and Allah is the giver of success. Then he demands for every chapter of what they said of cutting off infinity, and what they said of the beginning of the world without there being a world upon the trace of a world without end, and likewise by proof, and likewise.
Source pages p. 760

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation, now focusing on arguments from natures (طباع). Maturidi points out that if mixture is by nature, it would be eternal and unchanging, contradicting the dualists' claim of a specific event. He then argues that darkness acts by choice, not nature, making the nature-based argument unsound. He addresses the claim that darkness moved towards light and entered it, asking whether this was always (eternal) or began—if eternal, it's inconsistent; if began, it implies origination. He exposes the dualists' ignorance in saying that the two principles separate and reunite based on natures of heaviness and lightness, noting that if each had the other's nature, mixture would be impossible, but if it is possible, then both natures are in each, leading to the absurdity that each contains both good and evil. He further criticizes their arbitrary claims about separation and reunion without empirical evidence or knowledge of pre-eternal states. Finally, he demands proof for their claims about cutting off infinity and the beginning of the world without a prior world, concluding that their entire system is baseless.

Key Terms
الدهر
حدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say follows if the dualists claim the movement of darkness began?
A) It is eternal
B) It is part of time
C) Origination (حدث) follows
D) It is by nature

Key Takeaway
Maturidi exposes that the dualists' narrative of darkness moving towards light leads to a dilemma: eternal movement or origination, both of which are problematic for their system.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that something in your life is eternal or unchangeable, like a habit or worldview? How does Maturidi's dilemma force you to examine whether such things truly have no beginning?
?
When you think about good and evil, do you unconsciously treat them as co-existing forces? How does this passage call you to recognize that only Allah is eternal and everything else is created?
?
What belief about yourself do you hold as 'always there' that might actually have a starting point? What would it mean for you to trace it back to its origin and submit it to Allah's authority?

Contradictions in the Dualists' Theory of Mixture
Maturidi critiques the mixture argument from natures of heaviness and lightness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the dualists' claim that light and dark mix due to opposing natures of heaviness and lightness.

Core Idea
The dualists' mixture argument implies each principle contains both natures, leading to absurdity.

Maturidi now attacks the dualists' claim that the two principles mix because one has a nature of heaviness (descending) and the other lightness (ascending). He points out that if each had a different nature, mixture would be impossible, but if mixture occurs, then both natures must be present in each, which leads to each containing both good and evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic carefully. Identify the dualists' claim about heavy and light natures and the implication that each has the other's nature.
The text uses 'يتخلصان' meaning they are delivered or separated by these natures.
2
Understand the contradiction
Maturidi argues: if mixture occurs, it must be that each had the other's nature (in heaviness/lightness). That means the two natures are in each one, so each principle contains both good and evil, destroying their dualism.
The phrase 'استمل الخير والشر' means 'it contains good and evil'.
3
See the required consistency
If they are opposite in nature, one should be prone to mixture and the other to separation. But they claim both mix, so they are not truly opposite.
Maturidi notes that one of them has prevailed, indicating inconsistency.

?

How does Maturidi's critique of dualism challenge you to see that both good and evil ultimately come from Allah alone, even if we cannot fully understand His wisdom? Will you repent from any hidden dualism in your heart and affirm that all power is with Allah?
?
If mixture requires shared natures, what does that imply about the mixture of good and evil you experience in your own soul? Are you willing to admit that both good and evil inclinations come from the same created self, and that only Allah's guidance can separate them? What practical step will you take today to rely on Allah alone for purity?
?
The dualists separated the world into light and dark principles. Are there areas in your life where you separate your successes (from 'good luck') and your failures (from 'bad circumstances') instead of attributing all to Allah's decree? How does this lesson move you to say 'There is no power except with Allah' in every situation?

Refutation of Ignorance about Pre-Eternity and Separation
Maturidi criticizes dualists' ignorance and demands proof for their claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the dualists' ignorance about the pre-eternal state and their arbitrary claims about separation and reunion.

Core Idea
The dualists have no knowledge of pre-eternity, so their claims about separation and reunion are baseless.

Maturidi continues by attacking the dualists' claim that after separation they never mix again. He asks: how do they know this? There is no empirical evidence, and they have no knowledge of the pre-eternal state. Their entire system is based on conjecture, not certain knowledge. Finally, he demands proof for their claims about the beginning of the world without a prior world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic from 'ثم من قولهم' to the end. Note the rhetorical questions: 'فما أدراهم?' and 'وما يدريهم الأزل?'
These questions highlight their ignorance.
2
Understand the epistemic critique
Maturidi points out that the dualists cannot know about states before the world because they lack empirical evidence ('وجدنا باليقين لم يؤنس الاجتماع') and have no access to pre-eternity ('وما يدريهم الأزل').
This is an epistemological refutation.
3
Examine the demand for proof
Maturidi demands proof for their claims about cutting off infinity and the beginning of the world. This shows that their system lacks rational foundation.
The phrase 'على كل فصل مما قالوا' means 'for every chapter of what they said'.

?

Maturidi shows the dualists lack evidence for their claims about pre-eternity. What belief in your own life currently rests on assumption rather than evidence? How will you seek knowledge to replace that assumption with certainty?
?

What claim about your own existence have you never challenged? Will you commit to examining it with reason and revelation, starting today?
?
The dualists spoke confidently about states they had no knowledge of. Are there areas of your life—your character, your relationships, your purpose—where you speak with confidence but lack true understanding? What is one such area, and what step will you take this week to replace ignorance with knowledge?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Contradiction in Dualists' Theory of Mixture
Maturidi presents a logical critique of the dualist theory of mixture

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi refutes the dualist claim that two opposite natures can mix by showing it implies each nature contains the other.

Core Idea
Opposite natures cannot mix without each containing the other's nature.

Maturidi begins by pointing out the logical flaw in the dualists' theory of two natures (heavy and light). He argues that if they truly mixed, then each must have had the quality of the other, otherwise mixture would be impossible. This leads to the conclusion that each nature would then contain both good and evil, invalidating the dualist framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the repetition of 'لا قوة إلا بالله' as a theological anchor.
2
Identify the dualist claim
The dualists claim that heavy and light natures mix at the beginning and then separate based on their natural tendencies.
Heavy tends to descend, light to ascend.
3
Analyze Maturidi's logical argument
Maturidi argues: if mixture happened, each nature must have had the other's property (heaviness/lightness), otherwise mixture is impossible. This implies each nature contains both qualities.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
See the consequence
If each nature contains both, then it would include good and evil, thus invalidating the second nature (the duality). The dualist theory collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع هذا الطبع ثم تمام الجهل فى قولهم : يتخلصان بما كان من طبع الثقيل الانحدار وطبع الخفيف الارتفاع ، ثم في الابتداء قد امتزجا ، فلو لا أن كل واحد منهما على طبع الآخر في الثقل والخفة ما احتمل الامتزاج ، وإذا احتمل دل أن الطبعين كانا فى كل واحد ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وإذا احتمل الواحد الأمرين استمل الخير والشر فيبطل الثاني ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . على أن اللازم إذ جعلوهما متضادين في الطبيعة أن يجعلوا أحدهما شأنه الامتزاج والآخر البينونة ، وقد غلب أحدهما أن يكون على ذلك . ثم من قولهم إنهما إذا تفرقا لا يمتزجان من بعد، فما أدراهم ؟ ووجدنا باليقين لم يؤنس الاجتماع ، فكيف أنهم أبدا على تفرق واجتماع ؟ ، وكذلك في وجود تفرق بجهد ؟ وما يدريهم الأزل ، فيبطل القول بالنور والظلمة .
With this nature, then the height of ignorance in their saying: they are distinguished by what was from the nature of heavy to descend and the nature of light to ascend, then at the beginning they mixed. Were it not that each one of them had the nature of the other in heaviness and lightness, mixture would not be possible. And if it is possible, it indicates that the two natures were in each one, and there is no power except with God. And if the one is capable of both affairs, it would include good and evil, thus the second is invalidated, and there is no power except with God. Moreover, it is necessary, since they made them opposites in nature, that they make one of them characterized by mixing and the other by separation, and one of them has prevailed to be upon that. Then from their saying: when they separate, they do not mix afterwards. How do they know? We find with certainty that combination is not known. So how can they be always upon separation and combination? Similarly, is there any separation with effort? And what informs them about the eternal? Thus the statement about light and darkness is invalidated.
Source pages p. 760

Commentary
This passage refutes the dualist theory of two natures (طباع) – heavy (descending) and light (ascending). Maturidi argues that if they mixed at the beginning, each must have had the other's nature, otherwise mixture is impossible. That would imply each contains both natures, thus mixing good and evil. Additionally, if they are opposites by nature, one should be characterized by mixing and the other by separation, but they claim both mix. On their claim that after separation they never remix, Maturidi questions how they know this, since we do not observe such eternal cycles. The conclusion is that their entire doctrine of light and darkness is invalid.

Key Terms
طبع
الثقل
الخفيف
الامتزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualists believed that two opposite natures can mix without contradiction.
Correction: Maturidi shows that mixture requires each nature to have the other's quality, leading to internal contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would be implied if the two natures (heavy and light) mixed?
A) They are actually one nature.
B) Each must have had the nature of the other.
C) They cannot mix at all.
D) Only light can mix.

Key Takeaway
The dualist theory of mixture logically requires each nature to contain both qualities, thus invalidating the duality itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have witnessed how contradictory beliefs collapse under scrutiny. What belief do you hold that, if examined honestly, might contain a contradiction you have been ignoring? Will you examine it today?
?
Maturidi repeatedly says 'لا قوة إلا بالله' — no power except by God. In what area of your life are you acting as though your power or wisdom is sufficient without His? What would it look like to truly depend on Him in that area?
?
The dualists' theory failed because it tried to separate good and evil into two sources. In your own life, do you tend to attribute your successes to yourself and your failures to external factors? How can you recognize God's hand in both?

Challenging the Dualists' Claims on Separation and Remixing
Maturidi questions the dualists' knowledge about separation cycles and concludes the theory's invalidity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the dualists' claims about the separation and permanence of the two natures, leading to the invalidation of their entire doctrine.

Core Idea
The dualists cannot prove their claims about eternal cycles of mixing and separation.

Maturidi continues his refutation by addressing the dualists' assertion that the two natures, once separated, never mix again. He questions how they know this, pointing out that we do not observe such regular cycles. He also challenges their knowledge of the eternal state, ultimately concluding that the entire doctrine of light and darkness is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the second part of the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi shifts to questioning the basis of dualist knowledge.
The phrase 'على أن اللازم' signals a new argument.
2
Identify the dualist claim about separation
The dualists claim that after separation, the natures never mix again. Maturidi asks: how do they know this?
He challenges both the empirical and metaphysical basis.
3
Understand the objection from observation
Maturidi states that we do not observe combination (الاجتماع) with certainty, so how can the dualists assert a pattern of separation and combination?
This is an appeal to experience.
4
See the final conclusion
Maturidi concludes that their ignorance about the eternal state invalidates the whole theory of light and darkness.
The phrase 'فيبطل القول بالنور والظلمة' is the final verdict.
