Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
Critiquing Dualist Knowledge of Mixture and Separation
Maturidi criticizes dualists for lacking empirical basis and argues for combination over separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the dualists' claims about mixture and separation as baseless and presents an alternative view that things tend to combine.

Core Idea
The dualists' claim about separation lacks evidence; combination is the observed norm.

Maturidi opens his refutation by pointing out a fundamental flaw in the dualists' reasoning: they speak about mixture and separation as if they have direct knowledge, yet they never witnessed these events nor received reliable reports. He then challenges them to provide proof for their claims about the infinite past and the nature of things. This lesson examines his critique and his alternative argument that combination, not separation, is the natural state.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the dualists' claims
Read the Arabic passage carefully and note how Maturidi describes the dualists' lack of knowledge about mixture and separation.
Key terms: الامتزاج (mixture), الانفصال (separation)
2
Understand Maturidi's demand for proof
Notice that Maturidi asks for proof regarding the beginning of the world and the nature of things. He then counters the dualists' claim that things tend to separate with the opposite claim that they tend to combine.
Look for the phrase 'بل شأنهم الاجتماع'
3
Reflect on the logical move
Consider why Maturidi says that if the dualists can assume an unseen property without evidence, they open the door to arbitrary claims. Think about how this undermines their whole system.
This connects to the theme of methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ، فإن حكمهم هذا عجيب ؛ لأنهم لا يخبرون عن أحوال كانت ، ويكون ما عندهم من جوهر هذين ، ولم يكن لهما علم من قبل بالامتزاج ، ولا علم بكيفية الفراق ، والله الموفق. ثم يطالب على كل فصل مما قالوا من قطع النهاية ، وما قالوا من ابتداء العالم دون أن يكون عالم على أثر عالم بلا نهاية ، وكذلك يكون بالدليل ، وكذلك الامتزاج والانفصال؛ ليعلموا تعنتهم ، ويقال : لم يعاينوا شيئا ممتزج من خير وشير، ولم يرد لكم خبرا يحتمل الصدق، فإن قال : علمنا بالأدلة أن شأن الأشياء التفرق، وكل شيء يرجع إلى أصل جوهره. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : يقال : بل شأنهم الاجتماع، فمنتهى كل على أصل جوهره، وإذا كان وقع هذا قد اجتمع، فاجعل ذلك أبدا كذلك. ويقال إذ التفرق تبدد، والاجتماع تأكد وقوعه لم لا كان شأنهم الاجتماع، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ولو جاز تثبيت ما لا شاهد له في الشاهد، مع كونه من جوهره، لجاز القول يفعل الحواس على المعروف، أو الدرك باضداد ما به الدرك. وعُورضوا : لا يكون من النور غير الخير ولا من الظلمة غير الشر، فإذا قتل رجل ثم أقر، فإن كان المقر هو الذي قتل، وهو صدق، فقد عمل به الخير بعد الذي لم يقتل، فهو كذب، وهو شر، قد كان بقولهم هو الشر، وإن كان المقر منه الخير، وهو ترك القتل. وكذلك من قولهم : إن كل حاسة لا تدرك ما تدركه الأخرى، ثم فيما قال : سمعت، أو فما رأى قال : رأيت، وما قال به رأيت وسمعت غير الذي به سمع ورأى، وذلك جواب بما لم يدرك. وسئل عن سواد الظلمة إذا زيد على سواد النور، وهل زاد في السواد شيئا؟ فإن قالوا : لا، صيروا ما كثر هو الذي لم يكثر، فإن قالوا : ازداد، قيل : أهو النور أو الظلمة أو غيرهما؟ فإن قال بالأولين فازداد النور أو الظلمة، وذلك بعيد؛ إذ يزداد كل واحد منهما بالجوهر الآخر، وإن قال : غيرهما، للأمرين غيرا. ثم ما يدريهم أن ليس فى النور أو الظلمة زيادة على تلك الأجناس لا يعلمون يجميع أجزاء الجنسين بما لا نهاية لكل واحد، فإن ادعى الاستدلال بالشاهد على الغائب أبطل قوله بالتفرق وارتفاع النهاية؛ لأنه لم يشهد الخمسة، وهم
And after that, their judgment is strange because they do not inform about states that were, and their knowledge comes from the essence of these two, and they had no prior knowledge of mixture, nor knowledge of the manner of separation. And God is the grantor of success. Then he demands for every chapter of what they said about cutting off the infinite, and what they said about the beginning of the world without there being a world after a world without end, and likewise it is by proof, and likewise mixing and separation; that they may know their obstinacy. And it is said: they have not witnessed anything mixed of good and evil, nor has there come to you a report that admits truth. If they say: we know by proofs that the nature of things is separation, and everything returns to the origin of its essence. The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: it is said: rather their nature is combination, and the end of everything is upon the origin of its essence. And if this has occurred and has combined, then consider that always so. And it is said: since separation is dispersion, and combination is confirmed in its occurrence, why should their nature not be combination? And there is no power except with God. And if it were permissible to establish what has no witness in the witnessed, while being of its essence, then it would be permissible to say that the senses act upon the known, or perception by opposites of that by which perception occurs. And they are countered: from light nothing but good comes, and from darkness nothing but evil. So if a man kills and then confesses, if the confessor is the one who killed, and it is truth, then good has been done by him after [the one] who did not kill, so it is a lie, and it is evil, which according to them is evil. And if the confessor [acts] from good, and it is refraining from killing. Similarly from their saying: every sense does not perceive what the other perceives, then concerning what he said: I heard, or what he did not see he said: I saw, and what he said by which he saw and heard is other than that by which he heard and saw, and that is an answer by what is not perceived. And he was asked about the blackness of darkness when added to the blackness of light, and whether it added anything to the blackness? If they say: no, they make what is more the same as what is not more. If they say: it increased, it is said: is it the light or the darkness or something else? If they say the former two, then the light or the darkness increased, and that is far-fetched, since each one of them increases by the other essence. If they say: something else, then the two matters have changed. Then what informs them that there is not in light or darkness an increase over those genera, they do not know all the parts of the two genera with infinity for each? If he claims inference from the witnessed to the unseen, his statement about separation and the removal of infinity is invalidated, because he has not witnessed the five...
Source pages p. 761

Commentary
This passage contains several refutations. First, Maturidi criticizes the dualists for claiming knowledge about mixture and separation without having observed or received reliable reports. He demands proof for their claims about the beginning of the world and mixing. Then he presents an alternative: everything tends to combine, not separate. He argues that if one can assume an unseen property without evidence, they could claim anything. Next, he uses an example of a confession to show that actions attributed to good or evil are ambiguous according to their own principles. He also critiques sensory perception: claiming that each sense perceives only its own object leads to contradictions when someone says 'I heard' and 'I saw' using the same faculty. He then poses a question about adding blackness of darkness to blackness of light: if it does not increase, then more equals less; if it does increase, the result is either light or darkness or a third thing, each leading to absurdity. Finally, he argues that if they rely on analogy from the witnessed to the unseen, their claim of eternal separation and infinity is invalid because they have not witnessed the 'five' (possibly the five senses or something else).

Key Terms
النهاية
الامتزاج
الانفصال
الشاهد
الغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dualists think their claim about separation is based on reason and evidence.
Correction: Maturidi shows they have no evidence and that their reasoning is arbitrary; they could just as easily claim combination.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the nature of things?
A) Separation
B) Combination
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the dualists' claim about separation lacks evidence, and that combination is the more consistent conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The dualists claimed separation without ever witnessing a mixture of good and evil. What is one belief you hold about the world that you cannot trace back to any concrete evidence or experience? Are you willing to re-examine it today, even if it feels foundational?
?
Maturidi points out that mixture occurs and then argues that combination is the norm. Look at your own life: where do you see good and evil, joy and sorrow, faith and doubt coexisting? Does the reality of their coexistence push you to accept a single source for both, or do you insist on separating them? What would it cost you to embrace combination?
?
If the dualists' argument fails because it lacks evidence, what about the arguments you rely on for your most important convictions? Are they built on testimony, observation, or sheer assumption? Before you finish reading this, pick one conviction and ask yourself: Would this withstand Maturidi's challenge? What would it take for you to let go of it if it couldn't?

Refuting Dualist Analogies: Confession and Sensory Perception
Maturidi uses counterexamples to refute dualist analogies and moral principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses examples of confession and sensory perception to expose inconsistencies in dualist reasoning.

Core Idea
Dualists' own principles lead to contradictions in moral and sensory claims.

Maturidi continues his refutation by attacking the dualists' method of analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen. He argues that if they can assume unseen properties without evidence, they could claim anything. He then tests their principle that light produces only good and darkness only evil with a case of a murder confession, showing an inconsistency. He also critiques their claim about sensory perception, pointing out that when someone says 'I heard' and 'I saw', they imply a single perceiver, contradicting the idea that each sense is separate.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument about establishing unseen properties
Read the first part of the passage where Maturidi says that if the dualists can assume an unseen property, they could claim anything about the senses.
Key phrase: 'ولو جاز تثبيت ما لا شاهد له'
2
Analyze the confession example
Notice how the example of a murder confession is used to challenge the dualists' claim that light only produces good and darkness only evil. Consider the implications.
Think about who confesses and whether the confession is good or evil.
3
Understand the sensory perception critique
Read the part about senses. Maturidi argues that if each sense perceives only its own object, then saying 'I heard and saw' implies a contradiction because the same subject perceives both.
This shows the dualists' epistemology is flawed.

And after that, their judgment is strange because they do not inform about states that were, and their knowledge comes from the essence of these two, and they had no prior knowledge of mixture, nor knowledge of the manner of separation. And God is the grantor of success. Then he demands for every chapter of what they said about cutting off the infinite, and what they said about the beginning of the world without there being a world after a world without end, and likewise it is by proof, and likewise mixing and separation; that they may know their obstinacy. And it is said: they have not witnessed anything mixed of good and evil, nor has there come to you a report that admits truth. If they say: we know by proofs that the nature of things is separation, and everything returns to the origin of its essence. The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: it is said: rather their nature is combination, and the end of everything is upon the origin of its essence. And if this has occurred and has combined, then consider that always so. And it is said: since separation is dispersion, and combination is confirmed in its occurrence, why should their nature not be combination? And there is no power except with God. And if it were permissible to establish what has no witness in the witnessed, while being of its essence, then it would be permissible to say that the senses act upon the known, or perception by opposites of that by which perception occurs. And they are countered: from light nothing but good comes, and from darkness nothing but evil. So if a man kills and then confesses, if the confessor is the one who killed, and it is truth, then good has been done by him after [the one] who did not kill, so it is a lie, and it is evil, which according to them is evil. And if the confessor [acts] from good, and it is refraining from killing. Similarly from their saying: every sense does not perceive what the other perceives, then concerning what he said: I heard, or what he did not see he said: I saw, and what he said by which he saw and heard is other than that by which he heard and saw, and that is an answer by what is not perceived. And he was asked about the blackness of darkness when added to the blackness of light, and whether it added anything to the blackness? If they say: no, they make what is more the same as what is not more. If they say: it increased, it is said: is it the light or the darkness or something else? If they say the former two, then the light or the darkness increased, and that is far-fetched, since each one of them increases by the other essence. If they say: something else, then the two matters have changed. Then what informs them that there is not in light or darkness an increase over those genera, they do not know all the parts of the two genera with infinity for each? If he claims inference from the witnessed to the unseen, his statement about separation and the removal of infinity is invalidated, because he has not witnessed the five...
Source pages p. 761

Commentary
This passage contains several refutations. First, Maturidi criticizes the dualists for claiming knowledge about mixture and separation without having observed or received reliable reports. He demands proof for their claims about the beginning of the world and mixing. Then he presents an alternative: everything tends to combine, not separate. He argues that if one can assume an unseen property without evidence, they could claim anything. Next, he uses an example of a confession to show that actions attributed to good or evil are ambiguous according to their own principles. He also critiques sensory perception: claiming that each sense perceives only its own object leads to contradictions when someone says 'I heard' and 'I saw' using the same faculty. He then poses a question about adding blackness of darkness to blackness of light: if it does not increase, then more equals less; if it does increase, the result is either light or darkness or a third thing, each leading to absurdity. Finally, he argues that if they rely on analogy from the witnessed to the unseen, their claim of eternal separation and infinity is invalid because they have not witnessed the 'five' (possibly the five senses or something else).

Key Terms
الشاهد
الغائب
الحواس
الخير
الشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dualists think their moral dichotomy is consistent.
Correction: Maturidi's confession example shows that the same action can be judged both good and evil, undermining their principle.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi's confession example illustrate?
A) That confession is always good
B) That actions cannot be strictly categorized as good or evil from light alone
C) That murder is evil
D) That dualists are correct

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses logical examples to show that the dualists' principles lead to contradictions in moral and perceptual claims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that claiming a rigid dichotomy between good and evil can lead to absurd contradictions in real life. Where in your own life have you insisted on a simplistic moral binary that actually hides deeper truth? How can you commit to seeing the complexity of situations as Maturidi did?
?
Maturidi refutes the idea that each sense operates independently, pointing to the unified self that says 'I saw and I heard'. Reflect on your own experience: when you perceive something, do you ever separate your senses from your identity? How does this insight affect your understanding of your own soul's unity and accountability before Allah?
?
The dualists' system failed because it could not account for a single act (confessing) being both good and evil depending on context. Consider an action you took recently that appeared praiseworthy to others but was actually done with a bad intention. How does this passage challenge you to examine your own motivations rather than relying on outward appearances?

The Blackness Addition Argument and the Limits of Analogy
Maturidi uses a reductio ad absurdum on blackness addition and critiques the dualists' analogy method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument about adding blackness to blackness and his concluding critique that the dualists' analogy fails because they lack experience of the infinite.

Core Idea
Adding blackness reveals absurdities in dualist ontology, and their analogy method is self-defeating.

Maturidi poses a clever question: if you add the blackness of darkness to the blackness of light, does it increase the blackness? This challenges the dualists' view of light and darkness as separate essences. He explores the logical consequences: if no increase, then more equals less; if increase, then either light or darkness changes into the other, or a third thing emerges. Each option is absurd. Finally, he points out that their entire method of inference from the witnessed to the unseen fails because they have not witnessed the 'five'—likely the five senses or the infinite chains.

Guided Path
1
Read the blackness addition question and the possible answers
Read the passage where Maturidi asks about adding blackness of darkness to blackness of light. Note the two possible answers the dualists could give.
Look for 'فإن قالوا: لا' and 'فإن قالوا: ازداد'
2
Understand why each answer leads to absurdity
Consider why 'no increase' makes 'what is more equal to what is less', and 'increase' forces a change in essence or emergence of a third thing. Each is problematic.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Grasp the final point about the inability to witness the infinite
Read the final part where Maturidi says that the dualists' analogy from witnessed to unseen fails because they have not witnessed the 'five'. Consider what 'the five' might refer to.
The text ends mid-sentence, but the idea is clear: their method is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Argument from Pain and Fatigue in Actions
Maturidi argues from the discrepancy between human intention and actual outcome in actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the mismatch between intended pleasure and actual pain in actions to argue for divine involvement in human acts.

Core Idea
When you intend pleasure but get pain, the pain must come from another—Allah.

Maturidi presents a third argument focusing on the experience of pain and fatigue. He begins by observing that human actions often result in harm and exhaustion even though the doer intended to find pleasure and benefit. This contradiction points to a cause beyond the human agent, establishing that all actions ultimately involve God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic original and its English translation carefully to grasp the logical flow.
Notice the key terms: أذى (harm), تعب (fatigue), ألم (pain), قصد (intention).
2
Identify the contrast
Identify the contrast between what the doer intended (pleasure/enjoyment) and what actually occurs (pain/fatigue/harm).
This discrepancy is the starting point of the argument.
3
Understand the logical implication
Reflect on why Maturidi says 'it is impossible for the nature to be harmed without a harmer' and how this leads to the conclusion that the pain must come from God.
The final phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله' affirms that all power is from God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إنا نجد الأفعال مؤذية لأهلها ومتعبة ومؤلمة، ومحال تأذى الطبع بلا مؤذ وتعبه بلا متعب وتألمه بلا مؤلم ، فثبت أنها مؤلمة متعبة مؤذية إن قصد أربابها إلى أن يتلذذوا بها ويتمتعوا ، فثبت أنها كذلك لا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And also we find that actions are harmful to their doers, tiring and painful. And it is impossible for the nature to be harmed without a harmer, tired without a tiring agent, and pained without a pain-inflicter. So it is established that they are painful, tiring, and harmful, while their owners intended to find pleasure and enjoyment in them. So it is established that they are not [as intended], and there is no power except by God.
Source pages p. 829

Commentary
This third argument focuses on the discrepancy between intention and result. Maturidi notes that human actions often cause pain, fatigue, and harm, even though the agent intended pleasure and benefit. Since natural harm cannot occur without a cause, the fact that the actual outcome differs from the intention shows that there is another agent—God—who brings about the pain and fatigue. Thus, the action is not entirely under the servant's control; God's involvement is evident.

Key Terms

ألم
قصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that all outcomes of an action are solely due to one's own intention and effort.
Correction: Maturidi shows that unintended consequences like pain and fatigue prove that not everything is under human control; God is the ultimate cause.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that the pain and fatigue from actions prove God's involvement?
A) Because pain is an illusion
B) Because the doer intended pleasure but got pain, so the pain must come from another
C) Because humans always enjoy their actions
D) Because pain is created by Satan

Key Takeaway
The pain and fatigue we experience in actions, despite our intentions for pleasure, point to God's control over outcomes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often experience fatigue and pain even when you seek pleasure. Do you truly believe that every outcome is from Allah, or do you secretly attribute your hardships to your own actions or circumstances? How does this affect your trust in Allah when things go wrong?
?
When you feel tired or hurt after an action you intended to benefit from, do you remember that the pain is from Allah? What would change in your heart if you genuinely accepted that?
?
Maturidi argues that harm cannot come from nature itself. Do you live as if your nature (your habits, your strength) is the source of your comfort? Or do you see every ease and difficulty as directly from Allah? Pause and make a silent commitment to say 'there is no power except by Allah' when you face unexpected pain.

The Second Proof: God Creates the Goodness of Faith
Maturidi presents a second proof that God creates the act of faith as good.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second argument that God creates the act of faith as good, by distinguishing the unchangeable rational goodness of faith from changeable sensory goodness.

Core Idea
Faith's rational goodness is unchangeable, proving its creator is God alone.

Maturidi presents a second proof focusing on the nature of goodness. He distinguishes between sensory goodness, which can change, and rational goodness, which is fixed. Since the reward for faith is based on its immutable goodness, the act of faith must be created by God, not by the servant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how Maturidi distinguishes two types of goodness and builds his argument step by step.
The passage is a complete argument; try to follow its logic from premise to conclusion.
2
Identify the two types of goodness
Find where Maturidi contrasts sensory goodness (حسن حسي) and rational goodness (حسن عقلي). Notice the property he assigns to each: sensory can change, rational cannot.
This distinction is key to the proof.
3
Trace the argument to its conclusion
Understand how the difference in changeability leads to the conclusion that recompense is limited, and God's promise of tenfold reward confirms that God created the act of faith as good.
The proof relies on the stability of rational goodness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثانى أن ثوابه إذ حسنه حسى وحسن الإيمان عقلى وما حسن في الحس دون الذي يحسن فى العقل ، إذ قد يجوز انقلاب مثله على ما مر بيانه ، ولا يجوز انقلاب الآخر ، وإذا كان كذلك فيقتصر الجزاء على قدر المجرى ، والله وعد جزاء الحسنة بعشرة أمثالها ، ثبت أن خلق فعل الإيمان حسنا الله ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The second is that its reward, since its goodness is sensory, and the goodness of faith is rational, and what is good in the sensory is not like what is good in the rational, for it is possible for the like of it to change as previously explained, and it is not possible for the other to change. And if that is so, then the recompense is restricted to the extent of the current state, and God promised a reward for a good deed ten times its like. It is established that God created the act of faith as good. And there is no power except from God.
Source pages p. 834

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the second in a series) that God creates the act of faith as good. The author argues that reward for an act depends on its goodness. Sensory goodness (e.g., physical pleasure) is changeable and subject to alteration, as previously explained. But the goodness of faith (iman) is rational, unchangeable, and stable. Since the recompense is based on the nature of the goodness, and God promises a specific reward (tenfold) for a good deed, the goodness of faith must be created by God, not by the servant. Thus, God is the creator of the act of faith. The author concludes that God created the act of faith as good.

Key Terms
ثواب
حسن
حسى
عقلي
خلق
إيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that sensory goodness and rational goodness are the same or equally changeable.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states that sensory goodness can change (يجوز انقلاب مثله) while rational goodness cannot change (لا يجوز انقلاب الآخر). This distinction is crucial for the proof that God creates the goodness of faith.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is the goodness of faith different from sensory goodness?
A) Because sensory goodness is rational and faith's goodness is emotional.
B) Because sensory goodness can change but the goodness of faith cannot change.
C) Because the reward for faith is only tenfold.
D) Because faith is created by the servant.

Key Takeaway
The act of faith is good by God's creation, because its rational goodness is unchangeable, grounding a fixed divine reward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the goodness of your faith is not from your own effort but created by God, how does that reshape your daily reliance on Him? What is one concrete way you will acknowledge this dependence today?
?
When your faith feels weak or shaken, do you treat it as something changeable like sensory pleasures? How does knowing that its rational goodness is unchangeable affect your response right now?
?
God promises a fixed, multiplied reward for the good of faith, precisely because He created that goodness. Does your heart truly trust that reward, or do you seek validation from people instead? What step can you take today to shift your focus solely to God’s promise?

Maturidi's Refutation of Mu'tazila on Divine and Human Power
Maturidi critiques Mu'tazila for granting the servant greater power than God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the Mu'tazila doctrine of acquisition (kasb) inadvertently elevates human power above God's, and why he concludes that God alone creates all acts.

Core Idea
If the servant can act like God, the servant's power exceeds God's.

Maturidi takes aim at the Mu'tazila's claim that humans 'acquire' their own actions. He argues that by defining God's act as origination and then applying the same to humans, they give humans a power even greater than God's. This lesson unpacks that startling conclusion and its logical basis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The Arabic contains some unclear phrases; do not get stuck on them.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila position
According to Maturidi, how do the Mu'tazila define God's act and the servant's act? What do they give to the servant but not to God?
Focus on the words 'إبداع' and 'كسب'.
3
Follow the absurd consequence
How does the author show that the servant becomes greater in power than God? Look for the phrase 'فصار العبد بذلك أعظم في القدرة' and the reasoning that follows.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Why does the author say that reason and revelation both reject the negation of God's creation of acts? How does he use the classification of acts (nature vs. free choice) to support his conclusion?
This step ties together the entire argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن معنى فعل الله هو الإبداع والإخراج من العدم إلى الوجود ، وصيرت المعتزلة ذلك معنى فعل العبد ، ثم جعلت للعبد قدرة على الكسب ، ولم تجعل الله ، فصار العبد بذلك أعظم في القدرة ؛ إذ هي تقع على مختلف الأمر من الله ؛ إذ قدرته ترجع إلى أحد الوجهين ، ومما يبين أن كل شيء فعله نوع جعلوه طباعا ، ومن كان فعلين جعلوه أخيارا عن قدرة ، فيجب في الأول كذلك ، وذلك هو الحق عند المعتزلة ؛ لأنهم يجعلون للعبد قدرة على منع الرب عن ينفى [عنه] الحيرة ، ولا يجعلون مثله لله إلا أن يذهب عنه قدرة العبد ، وإذا ثبت أن فى نفى خلق الأفعال تحقيق ذلك وذلك مما يأباه العقل والسمع جميعا - ثبت أن الله خالق الأفعال كلها ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Also, the meaning of God's act is origination and bringing forth from non-existence into existence, and the Mu'tazila made that the meaning of the servant's act, then they gave the servant power over acquisition, and did not give it to God, so the servant thereby became greater in power; because it applies to different matters from God, since His power returns to one of two aspects. And among what shows [this] is that everything whose act is one type they made it a nature, and whoever has two acts they make it free choice from power; so it must be the same for the first, and that is the truth according to the Mu'tazila, because they give the servant power to prevent the Lord from what negates perplexity from Him, and they do not give the like to God unless the servant's power goes away from Him. And when it is established that in negating the creation of acts there is a realization of that, and that is what both reason and revelation reject—it is established that God is the creator of all acts. And there is no power except from God.
Source pages p. 834

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila. The author first defines God's act as origination (ibda`) and bringing from non-existence to existence. The Mu'tazila applied this same meaning to the servant's act, then gave the servant the power of acquisition (kasb) while denying it to God. This makes the servant greater in power, because the servant's power (according to them) can affect different matters, while God's power is restricted to one of two aspects. The author then mentions that according to the Mu'tazila, something with only one type of act is attributed to nature, while something with two types of act is attributed to free choice from power. If the servant can both do good and evil, then he must have free choice, but they deny that God creates all acts. This leads to absurdities where the servant's power can supposedly prevent God. Since reason and revelation both reject the denial of God's creation of acts, the author concludes that God is the creator of all acts.

Key Terms
فعل
إبداع
خلق
كسب
قدرة
طباع
اختيار
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans have the power to create their own acts by acquisition (kasb) independently of God.
Correction: Maturidi argues that this leads to the absurd consequence that the servant's power is greater than God's, and that reason and revelation require that God is the creator of all acts.

Checkpoint
What consequence does the author draw from the Mu'tazila's view that the servant has power of acquisition?
A) The servant becomes equal to God in power.
B) The servant becomes greater in power than God.
C) The servant has no power at all.
D) God's power is increased.

Key Takeaway
God alone is the creator of all acts; any doctrine that gives humans independent creative power diminishes God's sovereignty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you ever feel that you alone deserve credit? Read Maturidi's argument again: what does your feeling imply about God's power? Will you consciously attribute every action today solely to God's creation?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they were exalting human responsibility, but they ended up diminishing God's sovereignty. Is there any belief you hold about your own free will that might inadvertently reduce God's majesty? Examine your heart: do you truly believe God creates all your actions, including your sins? If so, how does that change the way you seek repentance?
?
Maturidi says denying that God creates all acts leads to absurdities that reason and revelation reject. Have you ever found yourself rationalizing away a verse or a logical truth about God's control because it felt uncomfortable? Will you now submit your intellect to this truth and let it transform how you see every event in your life?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Objection: If God Creates Acts, Commanding Becomes Absurd
Maturidi presents and partially answers the objection about God commanding what He creates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili objection that if God creates all acts, His commands would be to Himself, and the beginning of Maturidi's response that distinguishes the command from the creation.

Core Idea
God commands the servant, not Himself.

Maturidi now addresses a crucial objection from those who deny that God creates all human acts. They argue that if God creates both good and evil actions, then His commands and prohibitions become absurd—He would be commanding Himself and forbidding His own creation. This lesson presents the objection and the opening move of Maturidi's answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from objection to response.
The text cuts off at 'أمر', so the answer is incomplete.
2
Identify the objection's structure
The objector begins with 'First, that they were commanded and prohibited' and concludes 'then it would be commanding Himself'. Understand the logical chain: if God creates acts, then His command is to Himself.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Observe the beginning of the answer
Maturidi asks: 'Do you say that God commanded the servant to create faith and prohibited the creation of disbelief?' This forces the objector to admit that God's command is directed to the servant, not to Himself.
The full answer is not given, but this initial question already reframes the issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاحتج من يأبى القول به فى خلق الأفعال : أول شيء أنهم أمروا بها ونهوا عنها ، وذكروا الآيات فى الأمر بها والنهى ولو جعلناها خلقا له لكان أمر / نفسه ونهى عن خلق ذلك . يصير قال الفقيه رحمه الله : فيقال لمن احتج به : أتقول أمر العبد بخلق الإيمان ونحوه ، ونهى عن خلق الكفر ونحوه ؟ فإن قال : بلى ، صرح بأن الله تعالى أمر
So the one who refuses the statement about the creation of actions argued: First, that they were commanded and prohibited regarding them, and they mentioned the verses about commanding and prohibiting them. If we make them His creation, then it would be commanding Himself and forbidding His own creation. It becomes... The faqih (may God have mercy on him) said: So it is said to the one who argues thus: Do you say that God commanded the servant to create faith and the like, and prohibited the creation of disbelief and the like? If he says: Yes, then he has explicitly stated that God commanded...
Source pages p. 835

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the opponents (likely the Mu'tazila) against the doctrine that God creates all human acts. The objector argues: If God creates both good and bad actions, then when He commands people to do good and forbids evil, He would be commanding Himself and forbidding His own creation, which is absurd. The author begins the answer by asking the objector: Do you agree that God commanded the servant to create faith and prohibited the servant from creating disbelief? If the objector says yes, then he has admitted that God commanded something to the servant, which implies that the servant is capable of creating. The answer is incomplete; the text cuts off. The full answer would likely show that the objection fails because God's command is to the servant, not to Himself.

Key Terms
أمر
نهى
خلق
أفعال
أمر نفسه
فقيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector assumes that if God creates acts, then His command must be to Himself.
Correction: God commands the servant to perform or avoid actions, while God Himself creates those actions. The command is directed to the servant, not to the Creator.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection presented in this passage?
A) That God cannot create both good and evil.
B) That if God creates acts, commanding and forbidding becomes commanding Himself.
C) That God did not create any acts.
D) That the servant has no power.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because God commands the servant, not Himself; God's creation of acts does not make the command self-directed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fall into sin, do you ever think, 'God created this act, so why am I blamed?' How does this lesson's truth—that God commands you, not Himself—confront that excuse? What will you do the next time that thought arises?
?
If you truly believed that every command of God is directed to you personally—not to some abstract humanity—how would your response to His commands change starting today?
?
The objector tried to escape accountability by claiming God's command is absurd. In what areas of your life do you subtly reject God's commands by rationalizing that they don't apply to you? What is one command you have been avoiding, and how will you obey it this week?

Causality and Created Things
beginner

Evidence and Refutation of Necessity
Maturidi presents evidence and refutes necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the evidence from Muslim consensus for divine creation and the rational refutation of the doctrine of necessity.

Core Idea
Divine creation is established by consensus, and necessity is refuted by sensory self-awareness.

Maturidi begins by grounding the discussion in the clear evidence from Muslim consensus: God is Creator and Lord. He then confronts the claim of necessity (idhtirar), arguing that every person knows by direct sense that he is free. This foundational point sets the stage for the deeper analysis of human choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on evidence
Read the first part where Maturidi states that the evidence from Muslim consensus is sufficient. Note the key attributes: God is Creator, powerful, Lord, God.
2
Understand the refutation of necessity
Then read the refutation of idhtirar. Maturidi says it is corrupt because everyone knows he is free by sense. Consider why he calls it sensory.
The argument is that if one could claim necessity based on inner conviction, all knowledge would be subjective.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with 'There is no power except with God.' This phrase is a reminder of divine power and human dependence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدلالة، فقد أوضحنا لمن عقل لو أنصف ما في جملة ما أدى مع المسلمون أن الله خالق وما سواه مخلوق، وأنه قادر على كل شيء، وهو رب كل شيء، وألهه من غير اضطراب فى ذلك أو ميل قلب إلى خصوص، في ذلك دليل كاف. وسنذكر أيضا بعض ما في ذلك. وأما القول بايجاب الضرورة فانه محال فاسد؛ لأنه حسّى أن يعلم كل أنه مختار، ولو جاز القول مما يعلمه كل على جهة قلبه الجاز ذلك فى جميع العالم، ولا قوة إلا بالله. فإن قلت: إذ لم توجب الضرورة دل أنه لا تدبير فيه لغيرك، قيل: قد فرغنا عن دلالة ذلك، ما مع أن يقال: هو من طريق الخلق اضطرار، ولا صنع للعبد من ذلك الوجه؛ إذ لا يسمى به، ومن طريق الكسب اختيار، فعلى ذلك تقسيم الأمر. وقد بيتنا. ألا تری أن قول الكفر كذب، وهو من حيث الدلالة على سفه القائل صدق، فمثله يكون اختيارا من حيث الكسب ومن حيث الخلق لا، وجهة الخلق لا تدفع عنه الاختيار بما ثبت، فسواء لو كان خلق ذلك الفعل أو خلق السماء والأرض؛ إذ ليس فى واحد صرف فعل الخلق عن الخلق، ولا إزالة الاختيار، فمثله خلق الأفعال، ولا قوة إلا بالله على أن تسمية الخلق لا وصف الاضطرار؛ إذ القدرة للفعل مخلوقة، وهى سبب جعله مختارا لا مضطرا، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقد قال الكعبى: إن كل مختار فى فعله مضطرا في تألمه به وتأذيه به، فألزمه الأمرين فى الشيء الواحد، وكذلك أن قد يجوز أن يعرف الفعل من لا يعرفه كفرا وايمانا، أو شيئا عرضا، وحركة وسكونا، وهو ذلك بعينه، ولم يجز فى الجملة أن يُقال: الذى يجهله هو الذى يعلمه، والذي هو مضطر فيه
As for the evidence, we have clarified for one who understands and is fair what is in the summary of what the Muslims have established: that God is the Creator and everything besides Him is created, that He is powerful over everything, and He is the Lord of everything and its god, without any confusion in that or inclination of heart towards particularity. In that is sufficient evidence. We will also mention some of that. As for the statement about necessity (idhtirar), it is impossible and corrupt; because it is sensory that everyone knows he is free (mukhtar). If it were permissible to make a statement about what everyone knows in his heart, then that would be permissible in all matters. There is no power except with God. If you say: 'Since necessity is not established, it indicates that there is no disposal for other than you,' then it is said: we have finished with the evidence for that. What about the fact that it is said: 'From the aspect of creation it is compulsion (idtirar), and there is no act of the servant from that aspect, because he is not described as such. And from the aspect of acquisition (kasb) it is choice (ikhtiyar).' So the division of the matter is according to that. We have clarified it. Do you not see that the statement of unbelief is a lie, but from the aspect of its indication of the foolishness of the speaker, it is true. So similarly, it is choice from the aspect of acquisition, and not from the aspect of creation. The aspect of creation does not negate choice, as has been established. So it is the same whether He creates that action or creates the heavens and the earth; because in neither case is the creation of the act diverted from the creature nor is choice removed. So similarly is the creation of actions. There is no power except with God. Moreover, the name 'creation' does not imply compulsion; because the power for the action is created, and it is the cause that makes him free, not compelled. There is no power except with God. And al-Ka'bi said: 'Everyone who is free in his action is compelled in his pain and suffering from it.' So he makes him subject to both matters in one thing. Similarly, it is permissible that someone knows an action while he does not know it as unbelief and belief, or as an accident, movement and stillness, and it is the same thing. It is not permissible in general to say: 'That which he ignores is that which he knows, and that which he is compelled in...'
Source pages p. 838

Commentary
This passage argues for human free choice (ikhtiyar) while affirming divine creation. It begins by stating that the evidence from Muslim consensus is sufficient: God is Creator, everything else is created, and He is powerful over everything. Then it refutes the doctrine of necessity (idhtirar), saying that everyone knows by sense that he is free (mukhtar). If one could claim necessity based on what everyone knows in his heart, then all knowledge would be subjective. Then the author addresses an objection: if the act is compelled from the aspect of creation, then the servant has no part. He responds by distinguishing two aspects: from the aspect of creation, it is compulsion, but from the aspect of acquisition (kasb), it is choice. Creation does not negate choice. He gives an analogy: the statement of unbelief is a lie, but it is true as an indication of the speaker's foolishness. So the act is chosen from the kasb aspect. He further explains that the power (qudra) for the action is created, and that power is what makes the servant free, not compelled. Finally, he criticizes al-Ka'bi's view that a free agent is compelled in his pain, showing that this leads to contradiction.

Key Terms
الدلالة
الاضطرار
الاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God creates all actions, then humans are compelled and not free.
Correction: Maturidi argues that sensory experience directly informs us of our freedom, so necessity is false.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is the doctrine of necessity impossible?
A) It contradicts the consensus of Muslims
B) Everyone knows by sense that he is free
C) It leads to infinite regress
D) God's power prevents it

Key Takeaway
The sensory self-awareness of human freedom refutes the doctrine of necessity and supports the evidence of divine creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim you are free, but how often do you blame your circumstances or others for your choices? What would Maturidi's argument about sensory self-awareness of freedom say to you today in a situation where you are about to shift blame?
?
When you sin, do you truly admit to yourself that you chose it, or do you hide behind 'necessity' saying 'I couldn't help it'? What would change if you fully accepted your freedom and responsibility before God starting today?
?
Maturidi ends with 'There is no power except with God.' How does this balance between human freedom and divine power sit with your own sense of accountability? Will you embrace both without leaning to either extreme, and how will that change your next decision?

Creation and Acquisition: The Two Aspects of Action
Maturidi explains the two aspects of action and refutes a counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi distinguishes between the aspect of creation (khalq) and the aspect of acquisition (kasb) to preserve human choice while affirming divine creation.

Core Idea
Actions are compelled by creation but chosen by acquisition, preserving both divine sovereignty and human responsibility.

Having refuted necessity, Maturidi now addresses an objection: if the act is compelled from the aspect of creation, then the servant has no part. He responds by introducing the two aspects: creation (khalq) is from God, while acquisition (kasb) is from man. This distinction allows human choice without compromising divine creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the part starting with 'فإن قلت...' where the objector argues that if creation implies compulsion, man has no disposal. Note Maturidi's response differentiating khalq and kasb.
2
Study the analogy of the statement of unbelief
Maturidi gives an example: the statement of unbelief is a lie, but it is true as evidence of the speaker's foolishness. Similarly, an act is chosen from the kasb aspect but created from the khalq aspect.
3
Understand the role of power (qudra)
Later, Maturidi explains that created power (qudra) is what makes the servant free, not compelled. This reinforces that creation does not imply compulsion.
4
Consider the critique of al-Ka'bi
Maturidi criticizes al-Ka'bi's view that a free agent is compelled in pain. He shows this leads to contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدلالة، فقد أوضحنا لمن عقل لو أنصف ما في جملة ما أدى مع المسلمون أن الله خالق وما سواه مخلوق، وأنه قادر على كل شيء، وهو رب كل شيء، وألهه من غير اضطراب فى ذلك أو ميل قلب إلى خصوص، في ذلك دليل كاف. وسنذكر أيضا بعض ما في ذلك. وأما القول بايجاب الضرورة فانه محال فاسد؛ لأنه حسّى أن يعلم كل أنه مختار، ولو جاز القول مما يعلمه كل على جهة قلبه الجاز ذلك فى جميع العالم، ولا قوة إلا بالله. فإن قلت: إذ لم توجب الضرورة دل أنه لا تدبير فيه لغيرك، قيل: قد فرغنا عن دلالة ذلك، ما مع أن يقال: هو من طريق الخلق اضطرار، ولا صنع للعبد من ذلك الوجه؛ إذ لا يسمى به، ومن طريق الكسب اختيار، فعلى ذلك تقسيم الأمر. وقد بيتنا. ألا تری أن قول الكفر كذب، وهو من حيث الدلالة على سفه القائل صدق، فمثله يكون اختيارا من حيث الكسب ومن حيث الخلق لا، وجهة الخلق لا تدفع عنه الاختيار بما ثبت، فسواء لو كان خلق ذلك الفعل أو خلق السماء والأرض؛ إذ ليس فى واحد صرف فعل الخلق عن الخلق، ولا إزالة الاختيار، فمثله خلق الأفعال، ولا قوة إلا بالله على أن تسمية الخلق لا وصف الاضطرار؛ إذ القدرة للفعل مخلوقة، وهى سبب جعله مختارا لا مضطرا، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقد قال الكعبى: إن كل مختار فى فعله مضطرا في تألمه به وتأذيه به، فألزمه الأمرين فى الشيء الواحد، وكذلك أن قد يجوز أن يعرف الفعل من لا يعرفه كفرا وايمانا، أو شيئا عرضا، وحركة وسكونا، وهو ذلك بعينه، ولم يجز فى الجملة أن يُقال: الذى يجهله هو الذى يعلمه، والذي هو مضطر فيه
As for the evidence, we have clarified for one who understands and is fair what is in the summary of what the Muslims have established: that God is the Creator and everything besides Him is created, that He is powerful over everything, and He is the Lord of everything and its god, without any confusion in that or inclination of heart towards particularity. In that is sufficient evidence. We will also mention some of that. As for the statement about necessity (idhtirar), it is impossible and corrupt; because it is sensory that everyone knows he is free (mukhtar). If it were permissible to make a statement about what everyone knows in his heart, then that would be permissible in all matters. There is no power except with God. If you say: 'Since necessity is not established, it indicates that there is no disposal for other than you,' then it is said: we have finished with the evidence for that. So it is the same whether He creates that action or creates the heavens and the earth; because in neither case is the creation of the act diverted from the creature nor is choice removed. So similarly is the creation of actions. There is no power except with God. Commentary
This passage argues for human free choice (ikhtiyar) while affirming divine creation. It begins by stating that the evidence from Muslim consensus is sufficient: God is Creator, everything else is created, and He is powerful over everything. Then it refutes the doctrine of necessity (idhtirar), saying that everyone knows by sense that he is free (mukhtar). He responds by distinguishing two aspects: from the aspect of creation, it is compulsion, but from the aspect of acquisition (kasb), it is choice. Creation does not negate choice. He gives an analogy: the statement of unbelief is a lie, but it is true as an indication of the speaker's foolishness. So the act is chosen from the kasb aspect. He further explains that the power (qudra) for the action is created, and that power is what makes the servant free, not compelled. الكسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an action is created by God, then man cannot be said to choose it.
Correction: Maturidi shows that from the aspect of acquisition, the act is chosen, even though it is created by God.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say is the cause that makes the servant free (mukhtar) rather than compelled?
A) The act's creation by God
B) The created power (qudra) for the action
C) The aspect of creation
D) The necessity of the action

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's distinction between creation and acquisition allows that human actions are both divinely created and freely chosen, preserving responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment when you fell into sin despite knowing better. In that moment, did you feel like a helpless victim of your circumstances, or did you acknowledge your own choice? How does Maturidi's teaching that your action is both created by Allah and chosen by you change how you will approach repentance? What will you do differently next time you face that temptation?
?
The analogy in the lesson shows that even a lie reveals truth about the speaker. When you act in worship—prayer, charity, fasting—what does your outward action reveal about the state of your heart? Are you using 'Allah created my action' as an excuse for hypocrisy? Commit to one honest assessment of your intention today.
?
Maturidi says the power for your action is created, and that power makes you free, not compelled. Do you live as if you are genuinely free to choose obedience? Or do you feel trapped by your habits and desires, blaming your nature? What would it look like this week to embrace the freedom that Allah's created power gives you? Name one specific choice you will make today to exercise that freedom.

intermediate
6 min
God's Creation of Insulting Actions
Maturidi defends God's justice regarding the creation of insulting actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God is not blameworthy for creating insulting actions, because the action's moral description depends on the agent's state.

Core Idea
The moral quality of an action is not inherent; it depends on the agent's knowledge and intention.

Maturidi addresses a challenging objection: if God creates the disbeliever's insult, does that make God insulted? In this passage, he carefully distinguishes between the action itself and its moral characterization. By showing that the same action can be wise or foolish depending on the agent, Maturidi defends God's transcendence from blame.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to understand the flow of the argument. Notice the objection and Maturidi's response.
The passage uses a hypothetical: 'if He created insult to Himself, He would be insulted.'
2
Identify the key distinction
Look for where Maturidi separates the action as an accident (عرض) from its moral description. He argues the action itself is not inherently insulting.
The phrase 'فعله من حيث كان عرضا أو شيئا' indicates the action considered as an accident.
3
Analyze the example
Examine the example of the wise and foolish person. Maturidi shows that the same action of insult is described differently based on the agent's knowledge.
The wise person's action is not insult, while the foolish person's is.
4
Understand the summary
Read the final summary: God's action, as creation, is not described as insult or ugliness. This is the core of the defense.
The phrase 'لا يوصف بشتم ولا قبح' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكر نفسه وكفر به ونحو هذا ، فهو الذي لم يزل يعود نفسه من الذب على خصومه ، وليس أحد منهم يقول ذلك ، بل لو خلق شتم نفسه يكون مشتوما في الحقيقة مذموما ، بل خلق فعل الشتم من الكافر كذبا وجورا وسفها ، وفى ذلك دفع كونه مشتوما مذموما في الحقيقة . ألا أن تری من عرف فعل الشتم ؛ لذلك كان يكون عالما حكيما ، ومن أخبره عنه كذلك يكون صادقا ، ومن عرفه على ما عليه عند الكافر كان جاهلا سفيها ، وبالخبر به كذلك يكون كاذبا ، فمثله الذى ذكر ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وجملته أن فعله من حيث كان عرضا أو شيئا أو دليلا على سفهه أو حركة ونحو ذلك لا يوصف بشتم ولا قبح ، فمثله من وجه خلقه إياه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And what He mentioned about Himself, and disbelief in Him, and the like—that is what He has never ceased to defend Himself from against His opponents, and none of them says that. But if He created insult to Himself, He would be in reality insulted and blamed. Rather, He created the action of insult from the disbeliever as falsehood, injustice, and foolishness, and in that is the repulsion of His being insulted and blamed in reality. Do you not see that whoever knows the action of insult is accordingly knowing and wise, and whoever informs about it accordingly is truthful, and whoever knows it as it is according to the disbeliever is ignorant and foolish, and by informing about it accordingly is a liar. So the like of that is what he mentioned—and there is no power except with God. And the summary is that His action, in that it is an accident or a thing or evidence of foolishness or a movement and the like, is not described as insult or ugliness; so the like of it is from the aspect of His creating it—and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 844

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection is that if God creates insulting actions (like the disbeliever's insult), then He would be insulted or blameworthy. Maturidi answers by distinguishing between the action itself and its moral description. He argues that the action of insult, as an accident or movement, is not inherently insulting or ugly; its characterization depends on the agent's knowledge and intention. If a wise person performs it, it is not insulting; if a foolish person does it, it is. God creates the action, but the moral blame attaches to the disbeliever's ignorance and falsehood, not to God. Thus, God is not actually insulted or blamed. The summary emphasizes that the action qua creation is neutral.

Key Terms
شتم
فعل
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is blameworthy for creating evil actions because He creates the act of insult.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the action of insult, as an accident, is morally neutral. Its blameworthiness depends on the agent's ignorance and falsehood, which belong to the disbeliever, not to God.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is God not actually insulted when He creates the action of insult from a disbeliever?
A) Because God is not the agent of that action.
B) Because the action of insult is not inherently insulting; it is described as insult only based on the agent's knowledge and intent.
C) Because the disbeliever's insult is not real.
D) Because God's creation of the action is a separate matter.

Key Takeaway
God is not insulted by creating insulting actions because the moral quality of an action depends on the agent's knowledge and intention, not on the action itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak ill of someone, do you consider that the moral weight of your words depends on your intention and knowledge, not just the words themselves? How can you take responsibility for your intentions today?
?
Have you ever blamed God or circumstances for a sin you committed? This lesson shows that blame attaches to the agent's ignorance, not to God's creation. Will you repent and own your ignorance or falsehood instead of shifting blame?
?
The passage contrasts a wise person who knows the action of insult and a foolish one. Examine your own speech: do you speak from knowledge and truth, or from ignorance and falsehood? What specific step will you take today to ensure your words reflect wisdom?

Distinguishing Divine Action from Human Action: Maturidi's Response to the Reeds Objection
Maturidi counters an objection about distinguishing divine and human action with an example of moving reeds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why empirical observation alone cannot distinguish between God's action and human action, and how Maturidi uses the example of moving reeds to demonstrate this.

Core Idea
What we perceive does not reveal its ultimate cause.

This passage presents a key argument from Imam Maturidi against those who claimed that human and divine actions can be distinguished by mere observation. Using the analogy of two moving reeds, Maturidi shows that because unknown agents could be the cause, one cannot determine which reed is moved by God. The lesson explores this argument and the two objections raised by the faqih.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to follow the objection and response. Notice the structure: opponent's claim, Maturidi's counter, comparison, and the faqih's two reasons.
The passage is fragmented but the main argument is clear.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent argues that if two reeds move, one cannot tell which is moved by God, so they resemble each other. He claims that distinction requires searching for a cause.
This sets up the problem of empirical indistinguishability.
3
Understand Maturidi's counter
Maturidi suggests that an angel, devil, or animal could be moving the reed, so the opponent has no way to know the true cause. Therefore, the alleged resemblance does not prove anything.
The point: unknown causes block empirical certainty.
4
Analyze the comparison
Maturidi compares this to trying to distinguish the eternal from the originated by empirical observation alone – it fails if not considered properly.
This reinforces the limitation of sense perception.
5
Examine the faqih's two reasons
The faqih first argues there is no access to the cause in reality, and second that the opponent's counter-example cannot be from one aspect only (i.e., it is not univocal).
The second reason is incomplete in the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض قول خصمه أن من عاين أعلى القصبتين تتحركان لا يفصل بين يحركها آخر، ثبت أنهما تشابها، أنه يجب الفصل التي يحركها الله والتي بينها بالبحث عن السبب. فرعم قال الشيخ رحمه الله : يقال له : لعل ملكا تحرك أو شيطانا أو دابة تحت الأرض، فأي سبب له يصل به إلى ما الله حقيقة دون ما لأحد من الخلق لا يعلمه ليعلم أن الله عندهم لا يقدر أن يذهب بما خلق، وليعلم أنه لشدة التشابه انقطع [سبيل] العلم به من حيث نفسه، على أن السبب ليس يفصل عنده فيما كان من الله، إذ ليس غير الذي يعاينه، فأنى يعرف ذلك، ولا قوة إلا بالله. قال : وهذا كمستدل بالشاهد : ربما يعجز عن الفصل بين القديم والحديث لم ينظر من وجهه، فمثله الأول. قال الفقيه رحمه الله : وذلك عليه لوجهين : أحدهما أن لا سبيل في الأول : إلى السبب والعلم به في الحقيقة، مع ما ليس من الله غير الذي نراه ليعلم به فلا معنى لهذا. الثاني أن الذي عارض به لا يجوز أن يكون جهة واحدة، يدل
Then he countered the statement of his opponent: that whoever sees the tops of two reeds moving does not distinguish between what another moves, it is established that they resemble each other, that it is necessary to distinguish between those moved by God and between them by searching for the cause. So he claimed. The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: It is said to him: Perhaps an angel moves it, or a devil, or an animal under the earth. So what cause does he have to reach what is truly from God apart from what is from any of the creation that he does not know? So that he may know that God, according to them, is not able to take away what He created, and so that he may know that due to the intensity of resemblance, the way to knowledge of it from its own self is cut off, given that the cause does not distinguish for him in what is from God, since there is nothing other than what he perceives. So how can he know that? And there is no power except with Allah. He said: And this is like one who argues by the witnessed: perhaps he is unable to distinguish between the eternal and the originated if he does not consider from its proper aspect, so the first is similar to it. The faqih, may Allah have mercy on him, said: And that is against him for two reasons: The first is that there is no way in the first to the cause and knowledge of it in reality, while there is nothing from God other than what we see to know it, so this has no meaning. The second is that what he used to counter cannot be from one aspect only, indicating.
Source pages p. 849

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer concerning the distinction between divine and human action. The opponent argues that if one sees two moving reeds, one cannot tell which is moved by God and which by another, so they resemble each other, necessitating a distinction by searching for the cause. The Shaykh (Maturidi) responds that unknown agents (angel, devil, animal) could be the cause, so the opponent has no way to know the true cause. He further argues that the cause does not distinguish in what is from God because we only perceive the phenomenon. He then compares this to one who tries to distinguish the eternal from the originated by empirical observation and fails. The faqih adds two objections: first, there is no access to the cause in reality; second, the opponent's counter-example cannot be from one aspect only (meaning the argument is not univocal). The passage ends abruptly, likely continuing the argument.

Key Terms
عارض (mu'arada)
سبب (sabab)
تشابه (tashabuh)
قديم (qadim)
حديث (hadith)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Empirical observation can distinguish God's action from human action.
Correction: Maturidi shows that unknown causes (angels, devils, animals) make it impossible to distinguish by perception alone.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi's response to the moving reeds argument illustrate?
A) That empirical observation is sufficient to distinguish divine action
B) That unknown causes make it impossible to distinguish by perception alone
C) That reeds cannot move without a mover
D) That God only moves some things

Key Takeaway
Empirical observation alone cannot distinguish divine action from human action because unknown causes are possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own achievements, how often do you assume they are entirely the result of your effort? In what specific area of your life does this lesson's claim—that you cannot empirically distinguish your action from God's—challenge that assumption most directly? What would change if you started each day acknowledging that the true cause of every outcome is unknown to you?
?
Think of a recent trial or blessing where you felt you clearly understood the cause. Now consider that, according to Maturidi, you cannot know whether it was from God, an angel, a devil, or some other hidden agent. Does this uncertainty unsettle you? How might embracing this unknowability actually deepen your reliance on God rather than your own interpretation?
?
The opponent's analogy of the reeds suggests that without a cause, we cannot distinguish. This passage argues that even with a supposed cause, we cannot be sure. In your prayer life, do you ever pray for something and then look for signs that God 'caused' it to happen? How would it transform your relationship with Allah to admit that you will never have empirical certainty of His action in your life, and to trust Him without such evidence?

Objection and Response on Tashabuh
Maturidi presents an objection about similarity and responds to it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi responds to the objection that observable similarity prevents distinguishing divine acts from creaturely acts.

Core Idea
Similarity in appearance does not negate divine agency; hidden causes prevent certainty.

In theological debates about divine action, one common objection is that if we cannot distinguish God's acts from natural events by observation alone, then we cannot claim any act is distinctly from God. Maturidi addresses this by pointing out the limitations of human knowledge of causes. Let's read his response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to understand the objection and response.
The Arabic text is partially corrupted; focus on the main argument.
2
Identify the objection
The objector argues that if two moving reeds appear similar and one is moved by another, the inability to distinguish them proves similarity prevents knowing their source.
3
Understand Maturidi's response
Maturidi responds by listing possible hidden causes (king, demon, animal) that could also cause movement, showing the observer cannot rule them out.
This undermines the certainty that the act is not from God.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The objection fails because it assumes knowledge unattained; the observer cannot definitively attribute similarity as evidence against divine action.
5
Connect to broader theology
This reinforces divine transcendence and the limits of human reason in discerning divine acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض قول خصمه أن من عاين أعلى القصبتين تتحركان لا يفصل بين يحركها آخر، ثبت أنهما تشابها، أنه يجب الفصل التي يحركها الله والتي بينها بالبحث عن السبب. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: يقال له: لعل ملكا تحرك أو شيطانا أو دابة تحت الأرض، فأى سبب له يصل به إلى ما الله حقيقة دون ما لأحد من الخلق لا يعلمه ليعلم أن الله عندهم لا يقدر أن يذهب بما خلق، وليعلم أنه لشدة التشابه انقطع سبيل العلم به من حيث نفسه، على أن السبب ليس يفصل عنده فيما كان من الله، إذ ليس غير الذى يعاينه، فأنى يعرف ذلك، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he objected to the statement of his opponent that if someone sees two reeds moving and cannot distinguish between the one moved by another, it is established that they are similar, and that distinction is necessary between what God moves and what is among them by seeking the cause. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: It is said to him: Perhaps a king moved [it] or a demon or an animal under the earth; what means does he have to reach what is truly from God without [knowing] what none of the creation knows, so that he may know that God – according to them – cannot remove what He created, and that due to the intensity of similarity the path of knowledge of it from its very self is cut off; moreover, the cause does not distinguish for him what is from God, since there is nothing other than what he observes; so how can he know that? And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 849

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a response in a theological argument about distinguishing divine acts from created acts. The objector argues that if two moving reeds appear identical to an observer, but one is moved by another and the other by itself, the inability to distinguish them shows that similarity prevents knowing their source. Therefore, one cannot claim a specific act is from God versus from a creature. Maturidi responds by highlighting the uncertainty: the observer cannot rule out other causes like a king, demon, or animal. Since the true cause is unknown to humans, they cannot definitively attribute similarity as evidence against divine action. Moreover, the cause does not separate for the observer what is from God, because the observer only sees the external event, not its inner cause. Thus, the objection fails because it assumes a level of knowledge that is unattainable.

Key Terms
تشابه (tashabuh)
سبب (sabab)
فعل الله (fi'l Allah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two events look similar, the cause must be similar or cannot be divine.
Correction: Maturidi shows that hidden causes (like a king, demon, or animal) could produce the same observable effect, so similarity does not rule out divine agency.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Maturidi's response to the objection about two similar reeds?
A) God always distinguishes His acts by visible signs.
B) The observer cannot be certain that no other cause is involved.
C) Similarity is impossible for divine acts.
D) The reeds are not actually similar.

Key Takeaway
The inability to distinguish observable effects does not prove the absence of divine action because hidden causes could be involved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see two events that look similar, one seemingly caused by you and one seemingly caused by God, do you automatically assume both have the same underlying cause? This lesson shows the danger of such assumption. How has this assumption affected your reliance on God in your daily life? Will you now pause to consider hidden agents (like angels or tests) before concluding that something is merely 'natural' or 'coincidence'?
?
The objector claimed that lack of visible distinction prevents knowledge. Maturidi responded that hidden causes undermine that certainty. Think of a specific situation where you doubted God's involvement because you could not see any difference from worldly causes. How does this lesson challenge that doubt? What will you change about how you perceive God's action in your life from now on?
?
You are called to admit your ignorance of hidden causes. This requires humility. When have you felt most certain about the cause of an event in your life? How does this passage force you to reconsider that certainty? Write down one area where you need to accept that you do not know the full cause, and trust that God's action is real even if indistinguishable.
